“THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps equal
to the price stated, books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address :
Pretty Frosers.
The English language has the defects of its qualities. Macaulay made its best
eulogy when he said that in force, in richness, in aptitude for all the highest purposes
of the poet, the philosopher, and the orator, it is inferior to the tongue of Greece
alone. Now that science has complicated thought, even Greek must cede it pre-
cedence. For emotional speech the languages of Southern Europe are better fitted;
but the evolution of tongues has produced for the writer no intellectual instrument
so noble as English. Yet it cannot be played with. You must take it seriously, as
you take the British matron—-in whose severe shape, indeed, a sculptor might fitly
idealise it. In the most delicious love-transports, an Englishwoman seems always
on the point of saying her prayers; and English is the finest language
in the world to say one’s prayers in. Like its feminine type, it is too proper, too
rigid, too formal ;it has an uneasy sense of its own dignity; it hovers continually on
the brink of anti-climax. It is a machine which goes when you wind it up, not clay
which lives to the touch of the modeller. You cannot throw up your cap and be a
boy with it, for it interrupts one as awkwardly as Mrs. Shandy. Its wardrobe is
incomplete : it has innumerable morning suits, evening suits, and even tourist suits ;
but never yet has English felt at home in dressing-gown and slippers. Once, when
Lamb persisted, it attempted to try them on, but how obvious its discomfort! with
what x elief it donned again its starched shirt and chimney-pot ! And Mrs. Grundv
never liked Lamb.
Men of other nations feel the force, the breadth, the majesty of English, yet find
it ponderous and dull. It is brawny enough, and full enough; but (in spite of
A\ hitman) it is not limber enough. It can uproot a tree, but it cannot pick up a
pin .it can forge an ironclad, but it cannot crack a nut. Madame de Stael com-
plained that English was monotonous : its beauties were , all melancholy : one would
think the English clouds had colored it and the English waves had made its harmo-
nies. How different Italian—-shining like a holiday ! echoing like those musical
instruments which people have compared to scarlet ! Then Southerners suffer serious
distress from superfluous English sibilants. Charles the Fifth antithetically said
that Spanish was a tongue for gods, and English for geese. French he kept for
one s mistress. And readers of Dumas will remember that D’Artagnan only con-
sented to stay in London on condition that he should not be made to' learn English.
This is not wholly Gallic prejudice, for unfamiliarity turns many of our ill-spelt
words into cacophonous monstrosities. Without entering the nightmare-realm of
polysyllables, look, for instance, at words like “struggle,” “kitchen,” “satisfy”—
dissociating them from their meaning and regarding them as one would regard
unknown words in a foreign language. There are thousands of similar terms which
old custom has made sufficiently sweet to us, but which to strangers appear horribly
harsh and uncouth.
sibilants, short vowels, and consonant-collocations make it impossible to get the
color and vivacity of French prose into English, whatever the dexterity of a De
Quincey may do for its melody. English cannot causer ; The articles in London
journals meet you with a stiff bow, or, at most, a friendly nod ; while Parisian articles
are smiling, chattering, and shaking both your hands in eager welcome. The
learning is generally on the foggy side of the Channel; but how gladly would we
barter some of it for the sunshine of free-and-easy grace ! Compare, for example,
Pater with Sainte-Beuve—the style of the one Galatea in marble, of the other
Galatea warm with life. Yet there is nowadays noticeable a stronger English
striving after delicacy and lightness of touch. Some of the sketches in the Speaker
and National Observer are models of literary form. R. L. Stevenson, who has
penned some of the prettiest prose in the language, is closely followed by J. M.
Barrie, Quiller Couch, and Barry Pain, gifted with more than Addisonian felicity of
diction. Gilbert Parker is less perfect; but Richard Le Gallienne brings a poet’s
hand to write his “ Book Bills ” and his « Fancies,” and Grant Allen is an admirable
interpreter of the technicalities of science. The books mentioned below, of course,
appeal rather to cultured than to common tastes, and should only be bought by
people with a fine sense of sub-tones and side-aspects. For such they are classics.
“ VIRGINTBUS PUERISQUE,” by R. L. Stevenson. Cloth, 6s. 6d.
Essays now well-known, but as fresh as Spring, and perennially charming.
“MY LADY NICOTINE,” by J. M. Barrie. Cloth, 6s. 6d.
A book chief among ten thousand and altogether lovely. One is almost inclined
to retract aspersions on the English language, seeing what delicate humor it is
capable of translating. The smoker who has not read it has not smoked.
“ NOUGHTS AND CROSSES,” by T. Quiller Couch. Cloth, 6s. 6d.
Surely the quaintest, sweetest stories ever penned, with art reaching its acme
in deliciously subtle simplicity.
“IN A CANADIAN CANOE,” by Barry Pain. Cloth, 4s.
Sketches, verses and stories varying in merit, but with keen thought and a
clever style at the back of all.
“ PROSE FANCIES,” by Richard Le Gallienne. Cloth, 6s. 6d.
A recent book of idyllic essays, throwing the glamour of imagination over the
desert of commonplace, and transmuting lead to gold by the alchemy of exquisite
prose. “ A Borrowed Sovereign ” should be read at least six times.
“ POST-PRANDIAL PHILOSOPHY,” by Grant Allen. Cloth, 4s.
In these articles Grant Allen brings his scientific knowledge and scientific train-
ing to bear effectively on subjects of the day. He is invariably clear and suggestive,
and his tendencies make always for progress. And he tells us, by-the-by, in an essay
on “ The Struggle for Life among Languages ”
“ By the middle of the twentieth century, in all probability, English will be the world’s
dominant speech ; and the English-speaking peoples, a heterogeneous conglomerate of all
nationalities, will control between them the destinies of mankind. Spanish will be the
language of half the populous southern hemisphere. Russian will spread over a moiety of
Asia. Chinese, Malay, Arabic, will divide among themselves the less civilised parts of
Africa and the East. But French, German, and Italian will be insignificant and dwindling
European dialects, as numerically unimportant as Flemish or Danish in our day.”
Thus, in the stern prose of existence, utility conquers esprit.
NOTE.—As rnosi of the books mentioned are in strong demand, some may
be temporarily out of stock. In such case, orders will be filed, and the books
forwar. ed immediately on arrival of next shipment.
Wicked Woman Books.
“A SUPERFLUOUS WOMAN.” Cloth, 316 pp.; 3s. 9d.
In many points the best of the batch.
“ OUR MANIFOLD NATURE,” by Sarah Grand. Cloth, 271 pp.; 3s. 9d.
Short readable stories, with the_ sex-element prominent.
“ THE HEAVENLY TWINS,” by Sarah Grand. Cloth, 679 pp.; 7s. 6d.
Over long, but clever and attractive.
“ IDEALA,” by Sarah Grand. Cloth, 269 pp.; 3s. 9d.
Suggestive, and rather dull.
“THE YELLOM ASTER,” by lota. Cloth, 317 pp.; 6s. 6d.
A smart and amusing book.
“DODO,” by E. F. Benson. Cloth, 441 pp.; 6s. 6d.
An excellent presentment of the Very Newest Woman.
“ KEYNOTES,” by George Egerton. Cloth, 4s. 3d.
Subtly, shamelessly audacious ; the cleverest and most unblushing of all.
Listen, for example, to “my maid Belinda ” :
“ She is one bump of philo-progenitiveness, but she hates men. She says : ‘lf one
could only have a child, ma m, without a husb&nd or the disgrace ; ugh, the disgusting
men ! Do you know, 1 think that is not an uncommon feeling amongst a certain number
°f women. I have known many, particularly older women, who would give anything in
God s world to have a child of ‘ their own ’ if it could be got iust as Belinda says, ‘ without
the horrid man or the shame.’ ”
Pleasant reading this for poor brow-beaten Man ! Lucky it’s “if.”

The Queensland Grammar School system
is receiving a naaty knock from the present Govt.
At present the Grammar Schools are in receipt of
a handsome yearly endowment from the State
and 16 guineas per annum in addition for every
scholarship pupil in attendance. It is now pro-
posed, however, to make the first endowment pay
for the scholarship granted, so that the second
allowance of 16 guineas per head will be withheld.
Whereat the rich parent is wrathful, for it simply
means that he will have to pay out of his own
pocket the cost of the superior educational pap
purveyed for his offspring, inasmuch as aforesaid
endowment is insufficient.
The Bulletin lately chronicled the slimy
absurdity of one Szlumper, Mayor of Richmond
(Eng.), in sending telegrams of congratulation to
the Queen and her nearest relatives re the birth of
the York baby. Szlumper got his reward. The
Queen journeyed to Richmond to attend the
christening of that precious infant, and Szlumper
waylaid her at the railway-station with an
address. The claims of Szlumper having by this
time become firmly embedded in the royal brain
he was knighted in the usual manner. Szlumper
and J. B. Patterson are now of equal distinction,
but Szlumper has the better chance of achieving
further greatness before he dies,
Mr. Owen, a Brisbane sui’geon, relates, in
the current issue of the Australian Medical
Gazette, how, in Dec. ’B7, he removed a large
par-ovarian cyst from a Mrs. S., a lady 79 years
and 10 months, who for some years remained the
oldest case ever operated on. As time went on,
however, it was exceeded ; Dr. Herbert Spencer
recording one of 82 years and two months, and
also Mr. Bush another in Her 85th year. In ’93,
Mrs. S. again consulted Mr. Owen, and another
rapidly-growing tumor was diagnosed. At first
the surgeon rather ridiculed any operative inter-
ference ; but not so the patient, who, when she
distinctly understood it was an ovarian tumour,
called'her family together, they acting as jury,
she as judge, the verdict being “ that as her life
had become a burden through the size of the
tumor, and she had survived one operation, there
was no reason why she should not survive a
second.” So the doctor again operated with com-
plete success and the old lady is now well.

While We May:
A Moral from “ Oil Bias."
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Comrades in many a revel,
O’er many a sparkling glass,
You remember the tale the Devil
Told to Don Oleophas !
A painter and music-master
Like brothers lived here on earth;
If one of them owned a piastre.
Then both made gallant mirth.
When toil worn folk were taking
Their rest, these spirits bright
With merry old songs were shaking
The rafters black of night.
If Care tried in their revel
To join, with humour fine,
They sent him back to the Devil,
Carolling-drunk with wine.
Time never so gaily passes
As when he is cheered along
With glorious clink of glasses,
And story, and jest, and song!
But he sends his bill in duly,
And these two comrades tried,
Like the King who reigned in Thule,
Their last cup drank and died.
They met Below soon after,
But not as in days before —
Dead was the old-time laughter,
They knew each other no more.
What is the moral ? Marry,
The moral is this, I think—
While here on the earth we tarry
Oh, let the canakin clink !
V. J. Daley.
In Melbourne there is a barber named
Taylor whose next-door neighbour is a tailor named
Shave.
For adultery in Portugal the wife is sen-
tenced to eight years’ cellular confinement with
hard labor, and her paramour is similarly
punished. A husband guilty of adultery is fined
£2. The philosophy of it: (1) Every woman
knows who is the father of her child. (2) No man
can swear who is the father of his wife’s child.
For breach-of-promise defendants. At
Liverpool a postman was sued by Miss Winters, a
barmaid. His defence was that he was in bad
health and had only one lung, and that, therefore
—for the good of the race, presumably—he should
not marry. On this defence, the jury promptly
nonsuited the plaintiff. If this is to be a legal
precedent, then for the future Angelina before
accepting Edwin will, if she is wise, put the
pertinent questions ; “ How many lungs have you,
and how are you generally ?”
Why should a sea-serpent be always repre-
sented as swimming above the water ? The Gulf
of Carpentaria abounds in yellow sea-snakes,
sometimes nearly 6ft. long. They invariably swim
just beneath the surface of the water, and but for
the elevation of a vessel’s deck would rarely be
seen. They certainly could not be seen from a
low shore. It was Saturday afternoon when the
last big sea-serpent was seen.

Kaiser William evidently regards his poo r
father’s brief reign as an incident which didn’t
count with the Almighty. His bloodthirsty old
grandsire and himself were both of them created
for the purpose of wearing the crown of Germany,
as he recently stated for the fifteenth time. But
the intervening monarch with the cancerous
throat—well, he simply held a commission from
Heaven to keep the crown aired in the meantime.

Messrs. Winchcombe, Carson and Co., of
Sydney, opened their new wool-stores at Wattle-
street, Pyrmont, theotherday, with theaccustotmed
festivity. The premises are built on a novel plan,
being two storeys high instead of the customary
five or six, and running into breadth and length
rather than height. The lower floor—the one
on which the banquet was held —looks like
the very biggest apartment in all Sydney,
and the table which was laid in one
corner was suggestive of a sandwich left
all alone in a cathedral. Premier Reid was
present on the occasion, and after lunch he rose
and exploded, apropos of nothing in particular, in
a violent burst of fury against the Leg. Council.
Premier Reid is not a very large man, and when
he pranced about indignantly in one far corner, of
the huge, lonesome room he might have been an
ant using strong language in a fortress. When a
very middle-sized man proposes to let himself
loose he should pick his building more carefully;
it is only a person of great presence who can
look impressive in an empty wool-store. U.S.
Consul Bell, who was also present, made an
elaborate joke on the subject of wool, but
his best effect was when he addressed himself to
the young Australian and wanted to know what
he meant by alluding to England as “Home.”
The home of the Australian, as he justly remarked,
is Australia and nowhere else, and if he hasn’t
dropped to that rudimentary fact then he hasn’t
even begun the work of nation-making. And with
this great truth ringing in its ears, the company
drank several toasts, and then went and lost itself
in the vast wilderness of floor while looking for
the exit.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 13, 1894.
The War Scare.
Last week the periodical war-scare broke
out in an aggravated form, and for twenty-
four hours or so various London papers were
frantically challenging France to “ come on ”
and be utterly extinguished. No one knew,
and no one knows, or is likely to know, what
the trouble was really about. The only pos-
sible visible cause of the sudden riot which
arose out of the midst of a dead calm the
that France was vaguely alleged to have
“designs ” upon Madagascar, and on was
strength of this or some other rumour the
Jingo press furiously threatened to fill five
continents with blood and devastation.
Madagascar is not, and never was, British
territory, and Britain does not even profess
to have any shadow of a claim on that
troubled isle. She has no “ interests ” there,
except a very small trade and a few
rather unsuccessful missionaries, and there
was no reason to believe that, in any case,
either of these would be interfered with.
Moreover, Britain herself has annexed about
one-seventh of the earth, and never a year
passes but she adds some few yards or few
hundred square miles to her bloated empire,
therefore her frenzy whenever some other
nation threatens to imitate her on a small
scale is a weird and ludicrous emotion. If
she had gone to war—as she seemed to be
within an ace of doing—on the present
occasion nobody outside the United King-
dom, and hardly anybody in it, would have
had a shadow of an idea as to the purpose in
view, save the one great fact that the
“ honor of the British flag ” had to be up-
held. Nevertheless, the hysterics of London
Standard and one or two other inflamed
publications might have led to Sydney and
Melbourne being laid in ashes and this
country being filled with murder and deso-
latiou. For the most notable advantage of
the British connection is that Britain has
many quarrels and many enemies, and Aus-
tralia, which has no quarrels and no enemies
of its own, inherits a share of them all.
* » #
Apparently, the scare has died away, for
the time, at all events, as suddenly and un-
accountably as it arose. Except for a certain
ill-feeling which may lead to further trouble
hereafter, nothing remains of last week’s
panic save sundry hair-raising newspaper
articles in which it was announced that
France would be despoiled of all her
colonies, and made to pay a vast indemnity,
and utterly trodden beneath the feet of her
antagonist. Also, there remain in print
various allusioils to that flag which is alleged
to have braved for a thousand years the
battle and the breeze, and various gaudy
boasts about the everlasting nature of the
empire, and how its many scattered frag-
ments will remain for ever united by a bond
of sympathy as one mighty nation. To the
outside observer, however, who is not drunk
with jingoism, the chances of that averted
war were sufficiently precarious. The general
European struggle which is always threaten-
ing will be fought under so many new con-
ditions, that no one who is not filled with a
deranged variety of patriotism can venture
to foretell the result. The iron monsters
which form the sea defences of the Great
Powers are as brittle as glass, and seemingly
almost as uncertain. So far, Britain has put
her trust mainly in her metal leviathans,
while France has exerted herself rather to
provide a host of swift torpedo boats, and,
up to date, the relative merits of these diverse
engines of destruction are mainly a matter of
conjecture. So are the relative merits of the
various magazine rifles, the various schemes
of mobilisation, and almost everything else
directly and indirectly involved. The next
great Avar will be for all parties a frantic
leap in the dark, and when the Standard
calls on Britain to go into the struggle with
a light heart, it is partly mad and wholly
criminal. No nation can afford to go to war
with a light heart in this last decade of the
nineteenth century.
* • •
It is interesting to sum up Britain’s
chances in the hideous gamble. The British
empire is, to all intents and purposes, a
thing of yesterday. Three centuries ago it
was represented solely by England, Wales,
and the more than half-lost kingdom of
Ireland, where one Owen Eoe O’Neil was
all but supreme. Six centuries of almost
chronic Avar had only resulted in the loss of
all the proA’inces Avhich the Norman and
Angevin kings had inherited on the Con-
tinent. Despite innumerable battles and
many victories, England was, in many
respects, the most consistently unlucky of
the great European states. But almost
from the date of the union with Scot-
land the tide turned, and in two cen-
turies or so of dazzling good fortune
the great empire was built up, though the
most valuable part of it Avas lost again in
the interim. The empire, and the flag
which is alleged to have braved the storms of
ten centuries, are both things of a very short
time ago. The British possessions have been
heaped together too fast to allow of any
genuine process of consolidation, and lioav
the great, straggling combination would
hang together under the pressure of any
serious disaster is wholly a matter of conjec-
ture. So far, the luck, save in the matter of
the American provinces, has been so consis-
tent that the loyalty of the disjointed league
has never been put to the test.
* • •
Never during this century has Britain
fought a great war single-handed. In the
Napoleonic campaigns she had more than
half Europe for allies. In the Crimea she
was merely part of a league of four States
against one. In the Indian Mutiny half the
native population was Avith her. There
is absolutely no evidence to go upon
in forming an estimate of Britain’s
capacity in a single-handed struggle with an
enemy of her own size, or anything
resembling her own size. Her army, in her
old fighting days, was about as large as that
of any other of the Great Powers ; now it is
by far the smallest of them all. Her reserve
system is almost absolutely untried. Under
the benign influences of Freetrade, the
British agricultural population, which for-
merly supplied all her best fighting material,
has almost faded away. The very best of
her regiments, in the Peninsular era, were
those raised in the Scotch Highlands, and
the genuine Highland population has
vanished almost en masse. Almost equally
good were her Irish regiments, and Free-
trade, absentee landlordism, and coercion
has reduced the Irish population almost 50
per cent. Even the bucolic population of
England has declined very heavily. In the
next protracted struggle the British army
must be recruited very largely from the
urban population, for Britain has become
mainly a land of bloated cities, and whether
this new element will be anything like the
old is a question which no one can answer in
advance.
* • •
The British navy, like every other navy,
is now entirely an unknown quantity.
Therefore, so far as the ships themselves are
concerned, she has probably as good a
chance as she fever had, and in past days her
success on the seas has been remarkable.
But the British sailor has gone, to a great
extent, the same way as the British agricul
turiat, and, both in the navy and the mer-
chant service, he has been largely replaced
by Scandinavians and other foreigners. To
them the British flag is a matter of no
special importance, and in the war of the
future they form another unknown quantity.
But though the British navy is the
largest in the world, it has not grown
in anything like due proportion to the
commerce it has to defend, or to
the enormous expanse of territory to
be protected. Moreover, Britain must fight
its next great war under a disadvantage
which is absolutely unique. In the days of
the Napoleonic struggle she was practically a
self-supporting country, but Freetrade has
long since changed all that. British agricul-
ture is now not much better than a dead
industry, and if it is possible, by an evil
chance, that her navy should be utterly
defeated and she should lose the command
of the seas, a very few weeks’blockade would
reduce her inhabitants to starvation. If
such an apparently improbable event should
happen—and nothing is absolutely impossible
in war—there would be no necessity for an
invasion ; the army and volunteer force and
the various reserves would never get a chance
to fire a shot, but would be left to be
eaten up by hunger on the unguarded
shores, and the whole structure of the British
empire—that gorgeous creation of yesterday
which vainly professes to have endured since
the days of the Heptarchy—would crumble
to pieces. Even if the experience of the
Napoleonic war were repeated, the result
would be sufficiently disastrous. In the
course of that conflict, despite her constant
successes at sea, Britain lost over 10,000
merchant vessels, large and small. The
consequences then were comparatively slight.
Now, under the fostering influences of Free-
trade, such success as that of Nelson’s days
would mean semi-starvation, while failure
would be synonymous with hopeless ruin.
* # *
Under all these new conditions it is not a
good thing for Britain to go to war with a
light heart and a placid assurance of victory.
In a single-handed contest with such a
power as France, she would have very much
to lose and very little to gain. She would
stake one-seventh of the world against a few
hopeless little colonies which her opponent
would be far better without. If she destroyed
her enemy’s navy she would be hardly any
nearer conquering France than when she
started, for more than two million bayonets
would still be in the way ; if her own was
destroyed she would be absolutely at the
mercy of her foe, and her army might as
well be non-existent. Such a war would be
a gamble against unheard-of odds, so that,
taking all the circumstances into account,
the present is not a favorable period for
jingoistic fervor. Australia, at all events,
which has to take a share of all Britain’s
dangers, and gets little in return save the
privilege of being a field for the investment
of British capital, has every cause to be glad
that the flag which did not brave the battle
and the breeze for a thousand years, or any-
thing even faintly resembling that period, is
likely to remain furled for a little while
longer.
Dead in the Bush.
The human tragedy has no grimmer scenes
than those daily acted in the vast theatre of
the Australian bush. Here the soul’s infinite
capacity for anguish is strained through
exquisite gradations of torture, till the bars
of the body are broken, and merciful Death
snaps the thread which binds the Ego to
existence. Pitilessly pursued by the °blind
forces of destructive Nature, isolated Man
staggers continually across the stage, racked
by hunger, consumed with thirst, crushed in
sickness, yet turning feebly to confront his
Furies, and fighting to the last against the
Inexorable which overwhelms him. Nowhere
appears the human figure more august, more
terrible than in this Promethean combat, when
an atom of dust stands at bay with the
Universe, and desperate Manhood, divine in
suffering, defies its destiny of annihilation.
* * *
In every nook of the Australian wilderness
bleach the bones of Australian pioneers. The
dingo and the crow, sole witnesses of their
death-struggles, tore the flesh from the shrunken
limbs. Sun and rain disintegrated the poor
remnants, and left the ruined framework of a
man to shock the unthinking traveller, or
gradually to be dissipated in the mysterious
chemistry of the elements. And the man lived
and suffered—to what end? Who profited by
his days and nights of final agony, when the
sun scorched him as it crawled slowly round the
heavens, and the dews bit into his ague-stricken
body while he lay and watched the callous
stars? Myths and creeds fall helpless before
the problem of such a pain perpetually repeated
through interminable variations of wretched-
ness ; and the teleology which seeks to explain
it must assume as permitting and responsible
ruler not a benevolent God, but a gloating and
remorseless Devil.
• • #
In hundreds of instances the man who is lost
in the Australian waste disappears silently for
ever. If a ripple of wonder be felt when he is
no longer seen in his accustomed haunts, it
rapidly subsides, and the rest is mere oblivion.
There are so many homeless wanderers tramp-
ing across the continent that the here-and-there
one who drops out of the ranks is never missed.
Maybe tears are shed in far-away lands ; but
the bush keeps its secrets well, and rarely lets
slip a clue to the identity of its victims. Yet
ever and anon some ghastly relics are unearthed,
some piteous tale appears in the newspapers,
and the weird personality of the wilderness
shows itself as a horrid Minotaur, guarding a
labyrinth which exacts year by year its tribute
of fated human lives.
Such relics were recently found near the
Clarke River m North Queensland, about
miles as the crow flies from Charters Towers
ami such a tale is printed in the N.Q. Registry
of August 18. Even in sad Australian records
nothing more pathetic has been published. Tim
story opens with the statement of three m» n

on the back of which was written in pencil in
a good hand, the following diary. The man

Dear Sir and Friend,—For you have been a real friend
tome. Ido not know if this statement will ever reach
you, but I write it on the chance. What induced me to
come into this part of the country Ido not know. The
only way that I can account for it is that at times I am
pot altogether compos mentis. However, I left Sydney
by steamer for Cairns, and then went by train to Mareeba
stopping a day at the Barron Falls (which 1 did not think
much of). I then footed it to Herberton, and as the main
road was not passible owing to the floods, I took the
mountains, keeping near the heads of the Eastern waters
for a time ; then crossing to the heads of the Western
waters. I met with terrible rough country, interspersed
with bogs, and if not wet with rain daily, I was wet with
very long grass. In fact, I had a fearful time of it but I
might have got through all right but for getting bushed
I left Oak Park, on the Copperfleld River, for
Park. The track was difficult to follow ; I lost it but
kept in the same direction, till I found myself at the’slip,
rails (open) of a paddock. As there was a creek running
E through it, I expected to find some place on the creelT
and followed it for two days, but it was awful rou.ffi’
and for better walking I left it. Then sickness came
on, caused probably by exposure, for no matter how tou<ffi
a man may be he cannot be wet through night and day
for more than a month without feeling the ill-effects of it.
I pushed south for a day, then went down a watercourse
that ran EWN and S at first, finally settling down to
nearly east. I got seven little sprats at the head of this
creek and followed it in the hopes of getting some more
but it is very shallow and I have not succeeded. When I
left Oak Park (Feb. 19), I had with me three days’ tucker.
I believe that I have had no food at all now for more than
a week, and have lost all energy for saving myself. I
have camped to wait for death, hoping that it will not be
long coming; it is not painful, only weakness, but it seems
hard to die of starvation. limy be found yet before I
die, but I do not think that it is likely.
Does God answer prayer?
If so, how about "Give us each day our daily bread ?’
With all blessings and good wishes, I remain, dear friend
yours respectfully, B. S. Robinson.
Then, after an interval, follows another letter.
With what anguish was that interval filled !
For Mr. ,
——— Station,
Paroo River, N.S. Wales.
Two days have passed and I am still alive. How' long
it will last I know not; perhaps assistance may yet come,
but Ido not think it will. There is a marked tree near
my camp. This country is so rough that no one should
venture off a main road, for it is just impossible to go
straight in any direction. May God in his mercy grant
that assistance may soon come, or that there may be a
speedy termination to a life of misery.
A few more days have passed. I have shifted my camp
a mile or two daily. lam so weak that 1 can only make
a few' steps at a time, then cast a hopeless glance around,
and stagger on a few' steps more.
Strange that I do not come across any snakes, goanna,
or anything eatable, nothing but wild arrowroot, and not
much of that, as I cannot climb for it.
I have tried to keep my promise not to attempt suicide
again, but am very much afraid that the temptation may
be too great for me to resist. I might try to bleed to
death. I have lost both my knives, but p übaps my
scissors may be able to do the trick. I am afraid my
straps are hardly strong enough to hang me. However,
here is another sunset with prospects of more rain ; let
the morning decide ; anyhow, my fate is in God s hands.
What a long time it takes ro die of starvation.
I have vet strength enough to crawl to the creek for
water. The moon is now past its first quarter, so I sup-
pose it is about March 14 to 15. lam lying d nvn all day
and night long, thinking of food; no pain, except that
my bed might be s >fter. How much longer life will last
Ido not know', but it would be a great satisfaction to,
do so.
The moon is now in its last quarter, I am too weak to.
fetch myself more water from the creek, but will try to,
crawl down there and, small as the quantity of water is,
try to drown myself.
God have mercy on me. The date is about Mar. 27/94,
Born, June 26/40. Aged 54.

PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Nation of Tenants.
The serious and eminently dull Westralian
Assembly was shaken up, the other day, by a
motion, introduced by Leake, M.L.A., to the
effect that no more State lands should be sold
on any of the W.A. goldfields. Leake’s
motion was based, to a great extent, on the
awful fact that 216,000 acres of the Coolgardie
district were recently jobbed away to an Eng-
lish syndicate at 2s. 6d. an acre with exemption
from all taxes for 12 years ! Considering the
possibilities of Coolgardie and the adjacent
country, it may easily be discovered, 30 or 40
years hence, that the State has thus sold, for
miserable £27,000, an income of half-a-
million or so per annum, and lias bur-
dened the country with an annual tribute
of that amount to a gang of rack-renting
English landlords. In view of this un-
speakably bad bargain, and many more almost
equally foolish and nefarious, Leake desired
that goldfields-land should only be leased in the
future, instead of being sold outright, and he
was promptly suppressed by 16 votes against
eight. That hard-faced Tory, Premier Forrest,
gave a death-blow to the proposition by point-
ing out that it was one of the ineradicable
instincts of “ every Englishman ” to possess
the freehold of his own home ; and his colleague,
Makmiox, having backed up his dreary foolery,


, announcing that every Irishman felt the
aiue way, tire matter was settled. How every
Australian felt wasn’t even mentioned. This
lleged desire of every man to possess his own
freehold, and the fact that the leasehold system
-ould run counter to thebeautiful instinct in ques-
tion, and would, therefore, cause the population to
somewhei'e else where freeholds were obtain-
able, is not by any means a new argument. It
bus done good service everywhere, whenever it
lias been proposed to establish the leasehold
system and preserve the unearned increment
to the State, and it has lasted so long
that it is about time someone rose up
and denounced it as a miserable lie and
a n imposture. For, in truth, the alleged “ in-
stinct ”is a fraud in every sense. Not one
Englishman in 100 owns his own dwelling, or
ever hopes to do so. The sale of land does not
create a class of small freeholders, each living
in his own habitation ; it may have been in-
tended to do so, but its ultimate result is to
make a number of big landlords and an appalling
army of rack-rented tenants. In the Australian
cities something like 90 per cent, of the popu-
lation are rent-payers, and in the agricultural
districts the proportion of tenants is steadily
increasing. If it has any kind of good luck,
t j, e Hampton Plains syndicate may some day
be the absentee landlord of 30,000 or 40,000
tenants, but neither it nor all the other British
speculators, who own among them three or
four million acres of W.A. laud, will ever help
one single individual to gratify his natural
British or other instinct by living on his own
freehold. The land-sale policy is rapidly turn-
ing Australia into a nation of hard-driven lease-
holders, and this country’s highways are being
trodden hard by the large, flat feet of the agent
as he comes round every Monday morning to
collect the rent. The only definite result that
lias been achieved is that he collects the rent
for a private owner instead of for the Treasury,
and that doesn’t seem very much for any com-
munity to boast about.
Minimum Wage in Protected Industries.
The assembly of master bootmakers, in Melb.
last week, affords a crop of texts. The pro-
tected manufacturers who become haughty and
overbearing with success appear to forget that
their establishments are not purely private con-
cerns. To a certain definite extent the
State has a lien on the industries which
it establishes, even though its share in
floating the concern is but the Hey,
Presto !of a protective tax. Protection lays
obligations on the protected. The State guar-
antees its patronage, but not because it wants
to have a sweating system on the European
plan. Protection implies a local autonomy un-
hampered and undisgraced by pauper labor.
But do we find that the men advantaged by
Protection honestly endeavor to justify the pro-
tection ? Two speeches (Age report) by master
bootmakers in Melbourne :
Mr. Yager said he could see so many agreements had
been made that only division could result, and the manu-
facturers would only be playing halls and skittles with one
another. It was his intention to vo home and pay what-
ever he chose, without reference either to the employers’
or the men’s union.
Mr. J. BedgoOod stated he agreed with Mr. Yager’s re-
marks. As to his own firm, the members had made up
their minds that when work was resumed, if other makers
generally paid less than the 1892 statement, his firm
would at once tackle its wages-sheet and see ttiat it got
its work done at the same rates as others were paying.
Here are two men, established in business by
the vote of the people, bragging of their in-
dependence, and threatening to wreak the mis-
deeds of their brother ingrates on the por-
tion of the people unfortunately under their
control. The lesson is clear and plain. If
Protection does not protect the State from the
monopolist within as well as from the mono-
polist without, then the time has come for an
era of Free Heath. The chain of Protection is
complete In all but one link. That link is the
minimum wage. The day of the minimum
wage in Government and municipal contracts
is almost here. The next step is the minimum
wage for protected industries. With the boot-
trade in Victoria at the present moment the mini-
mum wage would be the solvent of the difficulty,
or, rather, the difficulty would never have arisen.
The master-bootmakers of Melbourne are,
fortunately, not all Yagers and Bedggoods
The very disunion existing in the employers’
ranks shows that there is a strong tendency to
concede the demands of the strikers. Not that
absolute justice necessarily rests with one side
in every dispute. The constant tendency to
trade disputes in “good times” as well as
“ bad times ” is exaggerated by the absence of a
standard to which arguments may be referred.
The minimum wage of an independent and
capable tribunal is that standard. It is
peculiarly and palpably appropriate to State-
protected industries. It would protect the
workers from the “ minimum of existence ”
below which men die, and it would assist
honest employers striving to build up a legiti-
mate trade.
Parliamentary Disintegration.
Payment of members, according to the Aus-
tralian Star, is fast demoralising the New
South Wales Assembly. Where there used to
be only two parties there are now a dozen or
more, and this, in the Star’s ppinion, is con-
clusive evidence of deterioration :
The House contains more parties and cliques than any
We remelu ber. There are Freetraders, Revenue-
tanfflsls, Protectionists, the Solidarity Party, the unat-
tached representatives of Labor, and the Country Party.
A'id amongst the Freetraders there are several cliques,
there are followers of Mr. Reid, followers of Sir Henry
iarkes, and followers of Mr. Wise. The first-named
advocate land and income taxation ; the last two are prac-
tically single-taxers, whilst the Parkesites are of the
opinion that no substantial alterations should he made in
the tariff, and the one thing Parliament ought to do is to
promote Federation. Can anybody name an Assembly
that contained so many parties?
Ihe Bulletin admits all the above, and is
gratified thereat. The present N.S.W. As-
sembly contains a dozen parties or more, and
this paper hopes to see the good work go on till
it contains three dozen at least. The House is
supposed to be a reflex of the people ; among
the thinking portion there are hardly two men
who hold exactly the same opinions upon all
matters ; therefore the Assembly should con-
tain almost as many parties as it does
members. If it doesn’t, then either the
people have given up thinking, or the
members are corrupt, or something else is serf
ously wrong. The trouble is that the supremely
idiotic system of Government by Party is based
on the equally idiotic assumption that the
Legislature and the nation should always be
divided into exactly two factions, and the
members of each faction should think absolutely
alike on every point. Which is just as foolish
as to suppose that there should only be two
sizes of boots in the community, and that the
man who doesn’t wear No. 6 shall necessarily
be exactly fitted with No. 10. If Ministers,
instead of being appointed in the present ram-
shackle fashion, were chosen for a fixed term
by vote of the people’s representatives, and
were not required to have the confidence of
any party, and their measures could be
thrown out or altered in any way without
the Ministry being thereby ejected from office,
and members could follow their own convictions
instead of being bull-dozed with the threat that,
if they don’t vote on strict party lines, their
own crowd will go out, or will fail to come in, and
all the rest of their policy will be sacrificed,
then it wouldn’t matter how many parties there
were in the Assembly. The paper which com-
plains that the multiplication of parties must
stop so that Government by Contradiction may
go on has got things upside-down. It is
Government by Contradiction that has to stop.
As to the other thing, the country wants as
many parties as it can honestly get.
The general ruck of Sydney barristers is
timidly moving towards the emancipation of
Briefless Lxttlewig, Esq., from the thraldom
of Horseleech Pettifogger, gent. one. As
yet, the Sydney barristers haven’t the col-
lective spunk to demand candidly that they
should be permitted to see clients personally
and without the intervention of a solicitor,
“ Because why ?” Because they want to “ get
all and give none.” Their present feeble
expression of opinion involves the absolute
amalgamation, on terms of equality, of all the
branches of the legal profession, and even
Briefless Littlewig, in his pigmy dignity,
could hardly stand Pettifogger’s right to
argue before the Full Court alongside of his
own mediocrity. Any average Attorney-
General, as one of the Devil’s Guild, naturally
weighs the prospect in prejudiced scales.
What is wanted is an Attorney-General imbued
with a deep scorn of his own profession, and
fired with a desire to serve the lay public
oidy, and send, if necessary, the whole legal
fraternity to its father, the Devil. The public
would probably hail, as a first instalment of
legal reform, the amalgamation of the branches
of the law-violating profession; but what it
hankers after is the abolition of the whole
“ biling ”of sharks as a protected guild. The
law, if passed, can be made inoperative by the
damnable inertia of legal etiquette. As ex-
ample : Queensland has its legal-practitioners’
law, whereby a barrister may act as a solicitor,
&c., by brass-plating himself as a “legal practi-
tioner,” and under this law the late solicitor
Mein was made a puisne Judge by his crony,
Sam Griffith, then Premier and now C. J. But
“legal practitioners” in Queensland—or, at
least, in Brisbane—are relentlessly “sent to
Coventry ” by the “ profession ” generally.
They are caste Ishmaels, their hands being
equally against other barristers and solicitors.
In the meantime, what Sydney wants is a
brainy, honest, “respectable” barrister who
will partner with a solicitor and give cheap
legal advice to all-comers on lines parallel to
those now remuneratively worked in the medi-
cal profession, some members of which give
“advice and medicine ” to callers at 2s. 6d. per
call. There’s a big pile in this idea.
A stereotyped reason for refusing Australian
wine-selling licences in New South Wales is
that supposed to be inherent in the formula —
“ Not required.” Assuming that our civilisa-
tion is sane and able to support its acts with
argument, where does the logic here appear ?
Is any applicant for a milk-vendor’s licence ever
refused on the utterly gratuitous assertion that
he or she, in his or her desired capacity, is
“not required?” Is any intending hawker
ever refused his licence solely on such an absurd
.pretence? Then, if not, why is this foolery
perpetrated in connection with Australian-wine
licences ? Is it to be laid down as a theorem
that a province, or a district, or a town is only
permitted to sustain the particular number of
Australian wine-sellers that some utterly irre-
sponsibleand meagrely-informed official declares
to be “required?” If so, who is going to
demonstrate the theorem? Who shall be
authorised to gauge and declare the cubic
contents of the local wine-drinking stomach ?
Who shall be clothed .with the power of either
setting bounds to the local vinous thirst, or of
conferring a monopoly upon a limited number
of licensed vendors ? On the one hand, the
State professes to nurture the great Australian
wine-producing industry, and certainly takes a
parentally corrective control of the operations
of viticulturists, besides appointing Boards to
deal penally with them under certain circum-
stances. On the other hand, any Bumble of
“ brief authority ” may impede the progress of
the industry by assuring the beak, who dis-
penses licences as if by inspiration, that this
or that wine-license is “not required”—thus
refusing revenue as well as hurting the in-
dustry. No one knows whether any license
is “not required.” Any statement to that
effect is merely a spurt of official presumption.
Grant the license in any and every case, so
long as the applicant is a respectable citizen,
and officialism cannot go far wrong. If it is
“ not required,” the licensee will speedily dis-
cover the fact and won’t bother the authorities
for a renewal.
There is a chorus of Parliamentary hairsplitters
who say that their .£3OO is not wages or salary,
but merely reimbursement of expenses. This
was Garrett’s introduction to the New South
Wales Bill in its early days, and the reimburse-
ment phrase was concocted to soothe Parkes,
who was a deadly foe to payment of members.
But no one then treated the expression as other
than a phrase, and its repetition in apparent
gravity by a number of men in the present
House proves how well-assured they are that
the world is gullible. Is not the payment
the average member receives his means of
living ? Does he not give his work in exchange ?
How would the reimbursement phrase apply to
a coal-lumper when he pockets his wages?
How does it fit a tailor who makes a suit of
clothes? There are wages for services, and that
is the principle the country had in mind when
it directed or sanctioned payment of members,
The man who contends that members should
receive no monetary consideration for Parlia-
mentary service is wrong-headed, but is not
contemptible; but the man who pockets .£6
a week for his Parliamentary work, but will
have it that this is not payment but merely
reimbursement, is either a tricky word-juggler
or a democratic fraud.

Sydney printers are on strike. The masters
wmh to reduce tm.e-wages from £3 to 12s.
for 48 hours; the men offer to take £2 l6s , ;
the masters refuse ; the men go out; and—the
vacant frames are filled immediately by the
multitude of comps, chronically unemployed.
It is the same sad, old story. Yet maybe the
ms are right to sacrifice themselves in struggle,
For, once set, precedent is inexorable ; and
wages once docked without resistance, ic rs
opportunity would be seized to dock them
again. The masters say that times are so hard
and competition is so keen that they cannot
get a fair profit on their work and pay the o
rate. But will the reduction help them . No
one whit; the public will get the full benefit ofiL
Nothing but competition has brought punteis
profits down to the bare subsistence-margin a
which they now stand ; and competition will
reduce them further till the same margin is
touched lower down the scale. The job quoted
at il now may be quoted at 19s. when wages
are lowered, but the Is taken from the com-
positor will go, not to the master, but to the
customer. This follows inevitably from the
present condition of things. And, as existing
charges are by no means unusually high, things
might very well have been left as they were,
But this is a time of retrenchment; and the
itch is attacking all employers in turn. All the
worse, in the long run, for the employers. Three
or four firms, to their credit be it said, accept
the men’s compromise. It remains to be seen
whether the public is unselfish enough to stand
by them.

“I am a staunch Freetrader. I hate the thing called
Protection, because it is mere blackmailing (loud cheers)
—it makes men put their hands into their pockets to
support other people's business.”—(Gibber by Canos

Bishop Camidge, of Bathurst (N.S.W.), is now
busy explaining his alleged utterances during
his recent trip to England. According to Dr.
Camidge he didn’t say what he was accused of
saying at all, and all the strictures passed upon
him were wholly unmerited. “ What I did
say,” he states, “was that if money were at
the present time judiciously invested in real
estate in Sydney, I believed in a few years, if
things went on improving as we expected, that
real estate would be worth four or five times as
much.” This was what shepherd Camidge,
according to his own showing, said to his
Yorkshire audience, and it is so exactly
the same thing as he was previously
accused of saying that his explanation
doesn’t seem to make any difference. Pastor
Camidge gets his tea and damper and
his episcopal gaiters out of this country, also
his mansion and the rest of the things necessary
to the perishing tabernacle of the flesh. And
in return he went “Home” and told his
audiences, m an indirect fashion, perhaps, but
with sufficient clearness, that Australia was in
a dreadfully bad way just now, and that
if any English capitalist there present
cared to take a mean advantage of our
necessities he could buy up property
in Sydney very much below its value. Then in
a few years the Australian would be glad to
buy it back again for four or five times the
price it was sold at, so that the foreign capi-
talist who put a million in the country would
take five millions out, and thus plunder Aus-
tralia of four millions for which he gave no
value whatever. In fact, Bishop Camidge,
on his own showing, was practically en-
couraging his British audience to loot, by a
morally - dishonest process, the country of
his adoption, because it happens to be in a
weak state and can’t defend itself. And this
isn’t by any means what Australia expects
from its Bishop, who is supposed to be a
heavenly-minded creature, and not a bagman
travelling in the interests of the British in-
vestor.
He has arisen in all his majesty of strength and
beauty. To Sydney be the honor and the glory,
for Sydney hath produced the phoenix. Not
the contemptible Anonymus who is bold in
solution of nut-galls, and who poniards behind
her back the soured mother of the girl to whom
he has promised a house-keeper’s billet for life
with board and lodging and drunken boots as
wages. But the fearless crusader who has
dared the shock in the open, and, before a
thrilled and awestruck world, has pumphandled
the mighty engine of the Statute Law and has
said unto his saddle-straddling mother-in-law—
“ Clear out of my house, or pay me that 10
weeks’ board and lodging you’ve had ! ” Alas !
that this welkin-rending challenge to Crea-
tion should waterspout only to the ebulli-
tion of effervescence ! The Statute Law, weak
and irresolute before the frowning personality
of the Gorgon, seeing in a vision the cold
goggle-eyes of its own hearthstone Fury, chewed
its swan-quill and postponed the matter to a
more convenient season. But the noisy fame
of the heroic son-in-law shall endure as a
mummy. Not Hampden, not Washington,
not Tell, not even Tommy Dodd and Samuel
Albert Rosa, were a combined rag-patch on
the Sydney son-in-law who worried his wife’s
ma right under the nose of District Court
dignity. The hero of all heroes he I—whose1 —whose
name, by the way, The Bulletin has forgotten.
Sic transit gloria Grundy !
Parson Henderson, of Melbourne, a newly-
imported sky-pilot from Dundee, preached
recently on the iniquity of Protection, its con-
spicuous sinfulness being that it is an utterly
selfish device, the purpose of which is to
“ boost” the community which adopts it without
caring what becomes of the rest of the world.
It is truly awful to think that Australia should
have gone to the expense of importing such a
simple-minded and unfinished person as Hen-
derson from the other end of the earth, when
an equally foolish individual could have been
found so much nearer. Of course, Protection is
selfish—utterly, beastly, disgustingly selfish.
So is Freetrade. The constant argument
advanced in favour of Freetrade is that it will
help “ our ” little community, and the rest of
the world may take care of itself. No one has
ever ventured to advocate either Freetrade or
Protection, or any other policy, on the sole
ground that it will do a lot of good to other
nations and leave our own just as it was, or
perhaps a little worse. Also, no one has
ever dared to advocate any ,creed with the
cordial assurance that it will save somebody
else’s soul and leave our own as badly off as
ever; every true pietist is engaged in a selfish
rush to save his own soul, and the other
fellow’s soul is a secondary consideration.
Also, no preacher has ever attempted to fill any
other preacher’s church instead of his own ; and
no country hasever maintained an army andnavy
for the express purpose of protecting some
other country and letting itself slide ; and no-
body on record has ever done anything that
wasn’t selfish—the man who does what the
world calls an unselfish action generally does it
for the selfish pleasure of feeling that he has
done it. Of course. Protection is selfish—
unutterably and brutally selfish. What else
did the unspeakable Henderson expect that it
or any other thing would be ?
Mrs. Annie Besant, when she brings forward
her proofs as to the truth of Spiritualism,
makes the usual mistake of relying on so-called
“miracles” as evidence. One of her state-
ments concerns a medium who, in the presence
of reliable witnesses, is slowly elevated by
some invisible force to the ceiling of a large
room, where, lying on nothing, he writes
messages on the plaster. Many people who
see this are doubtless converted on the
spot. The deceased Blavatsky is said
to have proved the truth of Theosophy
in a somewhat similar fashion. At an
impromptu picnic the party happened to
be a cup and saucer short, whereupon the
miracle-worker directed someone to dig beside
the roots of an adjacent tree, and there a cup
and saucer were found which exactly matched
the rest of the set. There things are remark-
able enough, no doubt, but they are just as use-
ful to prove the truth of Mormonism as to prove
anything else. If the balloon had been in-
vented and perfected by one man, who alone
knew the secret of aerial navigation, he
could have demonstrated just as logically
the errors of infantile baptism by going up
in his machine in the presence of a large
and moderately stupid assemblage. All that
was necessary would have been to tell his
audience with sufficient impressiveness that
adult baptism was the only correct thing, and
that he had a direct revelation to that effect,
and then go up in his big air-ship, whereupon a
very large section of his audience would have
regarded the matter as definitely settled. The
original discoverer of gunpowder might have
proved almost anything he liked by exploding a
cwt. of the new compound with a little pre-
liminary ceremony. No creed can be demon-
strated by a man floating in the air, any more
than it can by a man walking on a tight-rope
or boiling an egg in a hat without injury to the
hat.
Cheap Laborers in the vineyard.
We learn with sorrow, not surprise,
That preachers of Religion’s beauties,
Instead of pointing to the skies
And teaching sinners how to rise,
Are barracking for lower duties.
Instead of sticking to their creed
And Catechism, long or shorter,
These preachers, we regret to read.
Conservative in thought and deed,
Are striving hard to hatch and breed
Disciples for the fat importer.
The State protects the working man
And glories in his foes’ disaster ;
The shepherd doeth all he can,
By working on an Argus plan.
To serve his (stovepipe-hatted) Master.
Freetraders fill the foremost seats,
And beam upon their hired snorter ;
He owes to them the bread he eats,
The vegetables, beef and sweets,
For them alone his bosom beats—
Good servant of the fat importer !
The more the “ screw ” the shepherd draws
The more he serves the broad-cloth coat-er,
The more he cackles Cobden’s saws
And swears by Hingland’s tariff laws,
And vilifies the common voter.
A Christian loves his neighbor dear;
And, living in a tony quarter—
No nasty, humble dwellings near—
The parson’s neighbor (which is clear)
Sings ever in his holy ear
The sorrows of a fat importer.
We learn with sorrow, once again,
That saints who walk in golden fetters ~
And have a sordid point to gain
Are getting “ calls ” from Flinders-laue
To preach the gospel of the sweaters.
Their little minds distil delight
From arguments as weak as water.
God’s right is wrong and wrong is right
In these professing Christians’ sight,
So liars lead them on to fight
The battle of the fat importer.
The Census of N.S. Wales.
The Census of New South Wales (1891) issued by
Mr. T. A. Coghlan, Govt. Statistician, is an
interesting volume of 330 pages, which contains
more information than can be assimilated at short
notice. It is especially interesting in the early
chapters, where it deals with the primitive methods
adopted to round up and yard the population in
the Early Days; and in the concluding pages
where the writer deals with “Incidents of the
Census.” A lot of these incidents are remarkable
in their way. Some of them relate to the
Christian names of various unhappy inhabitants,
about the most astonishing of which is probably
“ Port Jackson.” A man named Fort Jackson

(General Poet Office next.)
Starts life with a heavy handicap, and the only
visible remedy for bis disease is to call himself
Peter Jackson. Other features of Mr. Coghlan’s
tale concern the samples of wild native humor
which turn up in the census papers. One woman,
for instance, returned her occupation as “ takes
washing,” and that of her son as “takes fits”—
one of the most sorrowful varieties of mirth which
could well be imagined. A person found sleeping
in a hollow tree put himself down as a “grazier”
on the ground that he lived on grass. A number
of citizens used bad language about their mothers-
in-law in their schedules, and others made un-
pleasant observations concerning their wives, one
of the latter being described as “ healthy, but bad-
tempered.” The occupations included such un-
obtrusively frank definitions as “ concubine ” and
“eunuch,” and others that were still more frank
and wholly unpublishable.
• * *
The religious statistics contained in the volume
are very elaborate, and show that there are no
less than 191 different creeds among the small
population of New South Wales, includ-
ing such weird stragglers as Non-Intrusion-
ists, Pessimists, Fatalists, Christian Saints,
“Gathered Unto Me,” and “Ecclesiastes VI.”
When one considers that all these sects, right down
to the “ Gathered unto Me,” is more or less firmly
convinced that all the rest of the community is
lost, this list becomes a very solemn thing to
reflect upon. A really surprising feature, and one
that upsets a lot of preconceived ideas, is that the
cranky, half-boiled little sects consist mostly of
men. Among the big and profoundly orthodox
creeds the men predominate just sufficiently—
considering that there are six men to five women
in the province—to show that the sexes are about
equally pious, but in the list of the small and
crazy churches males are three times as numerous
as females. Twenty-five dreadfully erratic con-
gregations count not one woman among their
adh-rents. The particularly violent unbelievers
are also mostly male, from which it would appear
that the he-cornstalk is more aggressive both in
his religion and in his want of a religion than the
she of the same race.
• • •
The “ Sickness and Infirmity of the People ” is
a gh istly chapter which any person of weak diges-
tion had better skip. One of its features is that
there are only two insane journalists in N.S.W.
an 1 only one tragedian in the Benevolent Asylum.
In the chapter dealing with “Habitations,” it tran-
•U'ires that about 4 per cent, of the population
dwe Is in tents, and 60 per cent, in houses where
the total accommodation is about three-quarters
<>f a room to each inhabitant. From this it would
appear that N.S.W., with million of people
and 198,000,000 acres, is already becoming some-
what overcrowded and short of sleeping space.
And just here it is worth noting that, in Mr.
Coghlan’s opinion, “they are generally the best
workers, and those whose standard of living is
highest, who are most involved in strikes and
combinations to maintain wages or increase them.”
Which is another way of saying that the worst
workers, and those whose style of living is most
like that of a hog in a sty, are the non-unionist
class. The section dealing with “Occupations,”
giving, as it does, the statistics up to the
end of the “Freetrade” epoch, shows some
rather awkward facts for those who have
t»een accustomed to claim that the manufacturing
industries of N.S.W. had gone far ahead of those
of Victoria, “artificially-fostered” by Protection.
The comparison of the number of factories and
the employes therein proves nothing, because a
factory, as defined by Victorian law, and the same
thing as defined by N.S.W. statute, are entirely
different. Taking the two census returns, how-
ever, Victoria, with a smaller population, has
about 14,000 more people employed in manufac-
tures than N.S. Wales.
• • •
These are a few scattered facts picked up at
random from the vast mass of information which
Mr. Coghlan has compiled. His volume is well-
printed, his facts are set forth in intelligible
langu ige, and almost every subject is illustrated
by diagrams which make the matter in question
as clear as anything dealing with statistics well
cm be. There are many things in it to
make the Australian feel proud, and many
more to make him feel miserably sick at
the stomach, and very much ashamed of
the great country to which he belongs. Also,
many of the figures—the lists of tent-dwellers, of
homeless persons who were enumerated in hollow-
trees and similar refuges, the unemployed citizens,
illiterates, persons dwelling in one-roomed habi-
tations, epileptics, cranks, persons who complain
pathetically in their schedules about their mothers-
in-law and their wives and their grandmothers
and about the drinking habits of the old man,
persons who put themselves down as “Ecclesiastes
Vi.” and the like —are solemn reading, and calcu-
lated to make any intelligent student feel that
this world is a fool of a place at the best. A
census-return, when it is read carefully, and
thoroughly digested, is an epitome of a country’s
misery, poverty, deformity, pauperism and general
ugliness, and is about the saddest thing outside
Gehenna.
If there is any truth in the yarn that 2000
Russian convicts have escaped from Siberia into
Corea, and 20,000 soldiers have gone in pursuit,
there are lively times in prospect for somebody,
and it is not easy to see where the fun will end.

hot column about the Rodondo wreck, while the
Telegraph had not a line. The Herald has
been “ down in the mouth ” ever since the Tele-
graph got in first with the Londonderry news;
now it has made up leeway.
Wild-Cat Column.
Now that the N.S. W. per cent, loan has gone
off with a rush at 99| it is pleasant to reflect that only a
year afro the same province borrowed £2,500,000 for 40
years at 4 per cent, and floated the loan at 97J nett I
During the 40 years of the loan’s currency that difference
of a miserable J per cent, will mean just £500,000 in gold
taken out of the country. And it is also interestirifr to
remember that a loan of two-and-a-half millions for 40
years at 4 per cent, will cost just four millions in interest
by the end of that period, and the original debt of two-
and-a-half millions will be still owing, and will be as
robust and as capable of earning another four millions in
interest as it ever was.
* • • *
N.S. Wales 4 per cent, debentures were sold in
Sydney, last week, at £lO5 10s. After that the possibility
of selling 3J per cent, stock in Sydney at par is almost
beyond question. The price of N.S.W. stock in London
is now only 20s. higher than the local quotation, and the
various commissions levied by the British financial shark,
and the cost of remitting interest to England, would
probably make Sydney the cheaper market of the two.

The shares (.£5 ss. paid up) of the B.N.Z. are
now offering at 12s. 6d. And only nine short years ago
they were over £27.
* * . *
The Colonial Bank of N.Z. pays its invariable
7 per cent. div. and has about £2500 to carry forward.
The Colonial has a paid-up capital of £400,000 and a
reserve of £55,000, and is one of the few Australasian
banks which has never gone broke, or reconstructed, or
suspended, or written off its reserve to meet losses, or
otherwise been in trouble. Which is about all that can
be said for any bank until a system of strict State-audit is
established. That good old item “Advances and all other
Debts due to the Bank ” covers such a multitude of sins
that no one can say anything really definite about any
bank save the bank that is already gone broke. There-
fore, it doesn’t follow that the Colonial of N.Z. is sound ;
it only follows that no one, up to date, suspects it of being
unsound.

Big rows are pending in England over black-
mailing by architects. The following popular condition
of contract is the lever for blackmail:—“The whole of the
work must be executed to the entire satisfaction of the
architect.” Who can ascertain beforehand, asks one
writer, except by specification, what is an architect’s
“satisfaction ”? It may be anything from perfection to
£lOO. And this is the condition used to justify the
famous eighth clause of the pastoralists’ agreement.

Editor Bulletin,—“ Spud ” complains in re
“ Xenos’” estimate of £1 10s. per ton for potatoes for
starch-making, that the price is too low, £2 15s. being the
average price in Sydney for last five years. Can “ Spud ”
say how many hundreds or thousands of tons of potatoes
have been thrown into Port Jackson during that time as
unsaleable ? Does he know that in the Crookwell district
(N.S.W.) hundreds of tons have been left to rot in the
pits, because they would not fetch enough to pay carriage
to Sydney? Potatoes are now selling there at 1/5 per
cwt. My idea was that the starch should be made as near
as possible to the place where the potatoes are grown
that the growers should co-operate to put up a central
starch-mill, just as butter-factories are put up, and should
send their surplus potatoes there to he treated. It would
prevent gluts in the market, and substitute a cheap, good
native product for a dear and sometimes rubbishy
imported one.—J.R.
♦ * • »
In Victoria the mint-deficit is decreasing year
by year, and this means that the large production of gold
enables the whole cost of coinage to be supported by the
miner. But the Westralian mint will find a different
state of affairs in the beginning, and the Government
reckons on a cost of £lO,OOO a year to the State till the
present production is very much larger. It would appear,
however, that this estimate is rather bigger than might be
reasonably calculated. It will be a good day for the
Coolgardie and Murchison miner when the mint (or the
assay office previously proposed in this column) is estab-
lished in Perth. At present, he is losing 3/- to 15/- per
oz. by reason of the distance of the market (mint) and the
“ nearness ” of the middleman.
• • * *
A certain bank had a customer heavily over-
drawn, and, as he was unable to offer any security, re-
quested him to form his business into a limited co. This
was done, and his creditors, being his relatives, took paid-
up shares for the amount of their claims, while the mer-
chant was allotted contributing shares, which he handed
to the bank as security. The directorate was actually the
merchant, and a heavy call was promptly made, which
the bank had to advance in order to keep its security
alive. Which shows how' the bank improved (?) its
security by transferring its claim from a man’s private
and trading estate to-his private estate only, supple-
mented by a portion only of his trading venture, fixed
and unnegotiable. Doubtless this appears on the balance-
sheet with-accrued interest as a good asset.
• • • •
An attorney who sheds cheques like leaves in
autumn, was badly left the other day. A holder of one
of his leaves got information when the settlement of a
case, which the solicitor had won, on behalf of a client,
would take place, and watching the payment of the
settlement cheque into the solicitor’s account, paid in his
own, which was promptly met. The client was duly paid
by cheque, and went to the bank, but “ when he got there
the cupboard was bare,” and he now theatens to bring
the gent, one before the Law Institute.
The same solicitor, when a client jibbed at a
stiff bill of costs, and elected to have it taxed, went to the
counsel in the case, paid his fees by cheque, took the
horsehair man’s receipt, to enable him to exhibit same to
taxing-master, and get the item passed—and then coolly
went to the bank, and stopped payment of the cheque 1

» • • •
Our friend Moses Discountesheim adroitly
wriggled out of a prospective heavy loss, the other day.
He had melted for a client a bill bearing the forged signa-
ture of a well-known firm of sharebrokers. The operator
had been discounting these forged bills for some time
with the kindly Moses, but had taken care never to have
more than one under discount at a time. When the
lender discovered the forgery he waited, correctly calcu-
lating that a fresh bill would be brought as usual for dis-
count, and the current one taken up with the proceeds.
In due course this happened, and when the new bill was
brought Moses promised to discount it, but excused him-
self for that day as the bill he held was in his safe at
home, and he would bring it in on the morrow, ma tear
freutid.
On the morrow Mr. Moses did not appear,
having been called unexpectedly away, but his clerk had
the cheque drawn out and signed, apparently reaily to
discount the latest bill. The discounter appeared and
was informed that Mr. Moses had left the cheque for the
last hill submitted, and it was to be handed over on the
original bill being taken up. This was a dilemma, as the
clerk would persist in carrying out the strict letter of his
instructions, viz., “that the bill under discount must he
taken up before the cheque could be handed over for the
last; ” and the cheque was flourished to emphasize this.
After wasting considerable persuasive eloquence
on the clerk, the operator withdrew, and presently re-
turned with another 60-percenter, who doubtless intended
to make a trifle, by lending the amount to take up the
current bill lor a few minutes. The needful was now
produced and the bill taken up, and the cheque, pro-
ceeds of the last forgery, waited for. The clerk unlocked
his cash-drawer, and drawing out, not the cheque, but
the bill, handed it to the expectant individual with the
remark, “ It was decided to decline the fresh business for
reasons best known to the introducer.” How the other
financier fared, history sayeth not, but this was emphati-
cally not one of “ Moses’ mistakes.”

Writes “Tenant ” : A solicitor, who has married
a propertied wife, insists now on every tenant taking out
a lease, for which document his professional charge is
four guineas. This is, of course, not blackmail, but only
the business-like proceeding of a gentleman (by Act of
Parliament). Times are so bad, however, that tenants
are throwing both property and leases on his hands.

Last week silver fell to 2s. 4-15/16d. per oz.
Tempora mutantur ! The poet Dryden once wrote to
his publisher : “ When the eighth book (of the ASueid) is
finished I expect £5O in good silver, not such as I have
had formerly. I am not obliged to take gold ; neither
will I." The silver-market now shows a slightly-upward

The register of a big printing co., just formed
up South, shows that all the shares are held by
clerks, agents, &c., but the actual holders are all very
bright lights in commercial and political circles. Before
the land and bank bursts everyone of ’em had his name
“writ large ” when he became a shareholder in any co. ;
but now, as one of them explains, “My bank doesn’t care
about my going into anything outside my business.” A
big and beautiful moral hangs thereby.
* * * *
During the last two years fire insurance co.’s in
Victoria have exercised such caution that it has become
almost impossible to overinsure either buildings or stocks.
Hence the absence of fires, and the overflowing coffers of
the offices. This happy state of affairs has, however,
lately attracted some half-dozen other London companies
to Victoria, and as they will all have to take extra risks to
get business, the usual weekly conflagrations—so much
missed laiterly—will be resumed at an early date. Just
you see!
• • • •
Five years ago Melbourne boasted some half-
dozen finance co.’s who “ did ” paper at 20 per cent, per
annum and paid decent divs. They’re all “gone" now,
but the many gentlemen who got on to the directorates
of some of them and gaily passed for discount the rocky
paper of their own private customers, thus getting out
“ at the finance co.’s” expense, are now busily hunting
around town to discount their own paper, to pay the
calls which the liquidators of these erstwhile convenient
institutions are making.

vacuum created in original capitals of between 20,000 and
100,000 dole, apiece.

Editor Bulletin, —There is a vacancy just now
for an M.P. game to get up and ask for a sworn return
from the banks, constructed and re-constructed, as to
their unclaimed balances. The trifling balances at credit
of deceased and other sorts of customers amount in
every bank to tens of thousands sterling. Why shouldn’t
those forgotten balances revert to the State every seventh
year? A short Act vesting all,unclaimed balances in the
Curator of Intestate Estates for the behoof of the State
is wanted worse than a lot of other things the public
howls for. As an old bank-clerk I know of several thou-
sands in one country branch bank belonging to people
who are still alive 1 Under the reconstruction and
liquidation schemes all such sums are most likely engulfed
according to law. Therefore, a vacancy exists for some
person to get up and move and “ keep on moving ” for a
return or a bill or some legal thumbscrew to make bankers
own up how much of other people’s money they are
keeping back from its owners or the State. It sometimes
happens people do ask about old balances, and then old
ledgers are hiked out of old voucher-rooms to be in-
spected, only to result in the discovery that during the
course of copying the list of unclaimed balances from a
filled ledger to a new one, as happens periodically, the
name “ Robinson ” has been transmuted into “Robert-
son,” or the ancient cognomen “Smyth ” into “ Smith.”
I’ve done it myself, so I know’.—Yours, Thin Man.

N.S.W. Savings Bank limit is .£2OO. Recently
a married couple had that amount banked in their joint
names, when an ingenious official told them that they
could bank to the extent of another £2OO by reversing
the order of their names. The next thing will be for a
single depositor to do likewise. When John Smith has
£2OO in the Bank, Smith John can start and lay by
another £2OO.

Ed. Bulletin, — You ask how a rival crowd can
possibly jump the Colo water-power when the Austra-
lasian Bights Purchase people have the lease of the
water? You see, the rival co. know that if they applied
openly for a water-lease their game would be known and
their attempt frustrated. They think they will get the
lease by a clause in their bill asking Parliament to
transfer the lease, already granted, to them, as those to
whom it was issued are not taking steps to have it fully
secured by passing a bill. Their contention is that as the
lease is issued, no matter to whom, another lease cannot
be granted, and that Parliament only can transfer that
lease on being satisfied of the bona fide» of the lessees.—
S.S.

Dear Bulletin,— By what right does N.S.W.
Department of Public Instruction compel its employes
to put a 2d. stamp on receipts for amounts less than £2 ?
If receipts are sent in without the stamp, twopence is
promptly deducted from the amount. Does the law of
the land apply only outside the Department ? or has the
latter been granted special power to raise a 2d. tax on all
amounts paid out under £2 ?— I Taxkd.

Published that already 20 Westralian companies
have been floated in London, and that of the capital
subscribed the vendors receive over a million. A million
w-hat? Cash? Hardly. Prohablypaid-upshares. These latter
are produced by printing-press, and a few hundreds of
thousands, more or less, don’t matter much. About one-
tenth of the money put up finds its way to the men who
pegged-out the ground, another tenth is put into the
mines, and the balance is swallowed up in the thousand
mysterious ways of a London boom.
• * » *
The point in the Londonderry North jumping-
case is simply this: Can a lease be forfeited before it is
granted? Or, rather, can the application for a lease be
cancelled on the ground of non-fulfilment of the labour
conditions of a lease ? Still plainer, does the application
for a lease carry with it the necessity to comply with
labour conditions? W. A. mining regulations say “yes" to
all these questions ; but the Act on which the regulations
are based is not clear, and the thing is —Do the regula-
tions go beyond the scope of the Act? W.A. Supreme
Court has been wrestling with the problem—the knottiest
ever submitted to it. The Londonderry extended claims
are at stake and, in the present condition of the market,
form a prize well worth fighting for.

• • • *
Story told of the first discovery of gold in Wes-
tralia. The Colonial Secretary was astonished one day to
receive from one of the Resident-Magistrates, in the
North, a telegraphic-message which ran, “ Boy took up a
stone to throw at a crow.” After puzzling over this
awhile the Col. Sec. replied, “ Well, what did the crow
do?” It was only on receipt of this that the excited
Magistrate learned that he had. forgotten to finish the
message with the words “ and saw gold in it.”

A party which lately went to Coolgardie travelled
overland to catch the steamer at Adelaide. They had
with them an outfit of mining tools, as much a necessity
of their vocation as a saw to a carpenter. At Melb.
they had to pay duty. At Adelaide they had to pay duty,
and when they finally landed in Albany they had to pay
up again 1
