Warden Finnerty recently ruled that Sunday
pegging of leases was legal, and granted an application
for ground alleged to have been pegged on Sunday. He
had, however, the good sense to state a case for the
opinion of the Attorney-General. If a man can be
punished under common-law for pursuing his usual avoca-
tion on Sunday, how on earth can the Sunday pegging
of a mine by a miner be legal ?

• • • *
The big shareholder in Mt. Drysdale, who
skipped out on the advice of two geological experts—one
from Queensland, the other from Victoria, lost upwards
of £20,000 by their advice. Geologists are the most un-
fortunate of all mining-men—they seldom make any
money for themselves in mines and frequently lose it for
other people. To pay fat fees and lose a big fortune is a
grim geological joke.

Already Mt. Drysdale gold-mine has paid up-
wards of £20,000 in divs. It was hawked around Sydney
last year, but no co. could be got together to work it.

Windmill Hill mine, Bendigo, which woke up
again a few days ago, has been a dreadful example of hope
deferred. For 29 years the shareholders kept on sinking,
boring and driving, and paid 30s. per share in calls, and all
they got in return was two 6d. divs. The third div. came
along the other day, -and, as a big reef has at last been
struck, it seems as if the sons of the original holders
would get their fathers’ money back with interest. A
Chinaman is not a pa'oh on the average Bendigo share-
holder for patience and persistence.

Two more missionaries are off from Sydney
to straighten up the Mulgrave River (N.Q.) blacks.
“The Bellenden-Kor mission ” is a standing joke
with white set tlers on the spot, the local aboriginal
fining worth about as much for “conversion”
purposes as a tree-climbing kangaroo. But the
mission does give food and shelter to a few
wretched natives, and they get so little charity up
North that it would be a pity if the play were
stopped.
Mr. Bert Levy’s sketch in this issue,
‘Two Sydney larrikins up to date,” shows the
Sydney tough in his newest form of development.
The very latest larrikin wears pearl buttons, after
the London coster fashion; and glaring, red
parrokeet boots, all silk and embroidery, and deco-
rated with rows of silver bells, which jangle
as he walks. He has his own photo, on the
toe of one boot and his donah’s on the other, and
one boot bears the inscription “May loves Billy,”
while the other is decorated with the legend
“ Billy loves May.” He pays i>3 or more for these
gorgeous decorations, and when he has got them
on he goes out and swears with a light heart.
Altogether, the Sydney larrikin is keeping pace
with the times.
Oliver Wendell Holmes is dead at 85-
dear old Oliver! His talent did not amount to
genius; but he made such a bright and cheery
noise in the world of letters that every book-
reader loved him. His life was singularly full,
complete, and successful ; and it closes as natur-
ally as a perfect day. Of his books, the Break-
fast-table and Tea-cup essays are best. All are
piquant; all charm by their grace of style and
unaffected fragrance of thought; all have a basis
of luminous intellect which is very pleasant to
rest upon. But the Autocrat is freshest and
strongest—Professor, Poet, and Tea-Cups are
sometimes repetitious, occasionally dull. The
Autocrat could not be the one, and would
disdain to be the other. Perhaps the similes
and illustrations —there are beautiful examples
of each class give Holmes’ books their
dainty and distinctive flavor. One can sip
and sip, and the palate never cloys. But
the witching draught must not be drunk to the
dregs. Holmes is generally in high spirits, and
one should come to him in high spirits. Yet he
can touch the minor chords —how tenderly the
Autocrat’s love-episode is treated ! And so he is
dead at 85.
Dean Macartney, of Melbourne, died las* 5
Monday, when well on in his 96th year. He was
born in 1799, so he narrowly missed having a
glimpse of three centuries ; and had been a clergy-
man of the Anglican Church for 72 years. His
clerical career commenced in ’22, when he became
curate of Banagher, and he came to Australia
in ’47, with Dr. Perry, first Bishop of Mel-
bourne. In 1852 he became Dean of Melbourne,
and in that capacity he was a picturesque
figure in the Victorian capital until the
day of his death. He grew so very old,
and yet remained so young, that at last
the idea of Dean Macartney ever dying at all
began to seem an absurdity. For the past ten
years or more his birthday has been a kind of
national event, to be commented on by the press
throughout Australia, and his progress towards
the end of his century has been a matter of
general interest. But, at last, the apparently
immortal dean died almost without any warning.
Melbourne will feel his loss keenly. If he never
did anything very striking he was at least a kindly
and honourable old man, an ornament to the
church, and an all-Australian institution. Also,
now that he is gone, many men of 70 and 80, who
formerly consoled themselves with the idea that
there was no need for them to die for many years
to come because Dean Macartney was still alive,
will suddenly begin to feel very aged indeed.
SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SriOVYS FOR COMING WEEK.

Bwou Variety & Burlesque Co.
“ Ma Mie Rosette ” is going strong at the
Lyceum. At the same time the public just begins
to realise that there is a serious deficiency in this
opera somewhere. The second act changes the
subject suddenly, just when something dreadfully
wicked seems as if it might be almost on the verge
of happening, and an idea is gaining ground that
the king needn’t have been interrupted quite so
unexpectedly. As it is he has caught Rosette’s
fainting form in his arms, and is just hoisting her
up a little so as to get his weight on the other leg
when the infuriated lover bursts in and breaks up
the tableau; consequently, the general desire to
know what the monarch will do under very em-
barrassing circumstances is left ungratified.
* * * *
After “The Shamrock and the Rose ” is
done with, which is expected to be on Friday
night, “Julius Caesar” will fill the aching void at
Sydney Majesty’s. The Roman drama is to be
staged with great magnificence, and “ thirty stal-
wart men ” are advertised for to be an infuriated
Latin mob with both hands full of tumult around
the bier of the butchered conqueror. Gov. Duff
and suite will attend on opening night. Gov.
Duff has now become a perfectly tame celebrity,
and will feed out of the hand. Of late he has
developed a taste for the legitimate drama, which
he really seems to enjoy in a certain Vjored and
dreary fashion that is especially his own.
# * *
The fact that Maggie Moore has been
coining money for a week or more at Sydney
Royal out of the “Octoroon,” is a sufficient proof
of that lady’s capacity to prosper on the most un-
promising materials. Nobody save Miss Moore
would venture to play the “Octoroon,’’nowadays.
That lady succeeded in making Zoe as interestingas
she ever has been, and Mr. Russell shambled before
the public eye as Jacob M‘Closky with about as
much success as that large, coarse deceiver admits
of. The rest of the drama consists principally of
Salem Soudder —a person whose unreality is
sufficiently proved by the want of that lonesome
initial which stands up like a mast on a wreck in
the middle of the name of every genuine Amur-
rikan —and Mr. Roberts did this part better than
anything he has yet attempted. The inevitable
“ Colleen Bawn,” “ Arrah-na-Pogue,” and “The
Days of ’49 ” will fill in the last week of Maggie
Moore’s season at the Royal.
* * * *
The theatrical carnival and costume-foot-
ball match happens at Sydney Association Ground
next Thursday, and promised, when last heard of,
to be even a bigger event than was generally ex-
pected. All the stage beauties now in Sydney
will help to dispense refreshments, and the chappie
who doesn’t spend his last copper like a manful
johnny at the shrine of his own special divinity is
unworthy of the name of dude. The Bulletin
suggests that a faithfully-executed cast of Ryley’s
vanished and lamented legs would make a good
sixpenny side-show.
* * * *
M‘Neill, the big, hairy, Scotch evangelist,
broke out in Sydney last Monday. The bigness
and hairiness of M‘Neill are the points which first
strike the beholder. His next most conspicuous
points are his aggressive confidence and his
whirling mode of delivery. Talmage, when he
was in Sydney, appeared to be the most wildly-
oocksure individual upon this earth, but he is a
feeble and dubious person compared to the
Highland caterah who now rages round Sydney in
his stead. When M‘Neill arrived, a large number
of local pastors assembled to welcome him, and,
when they had grovelled to him for 30 minutes
and compared him to Paul, the visitor admonished
them upon their shortcomings in a way which
made their hair almost stand on end. The gist of
his discourse was that the typical pastor is a poor,
washed-out being who is eaten up with his own
small dignity, and is petrified with etiquette
and smothered with precedent and red-tape.
M‘Neill’s average voice is a roar, and when
he made these statements at the very top of it
he broke up his audience completely. As a
humourist, however, the Rev. John is a failure.
His one attempt at a joke so far has been a dreary
witticism about “the beautiful harbour,” and for
the sake of that witticism he ought to be taken up
Johnson’s Bay in a boat, and anchored off the
Glebe Island slaughter-house until he repents and
swears off humour for the rest of his hairy exist-
ence.
* * * *
Abell and Klaer’s European Circus has
done most excellent business ever since its opening
night at Sydney. Though some people may take
exception to its not being “ horsey ” enough for
Australian taste, those who think that way must,
judging from results, be in a “ strong minority.”
The show has, to date of writing, played packed
business in all its 13 performances, including
matinees, and on Saturday last the doors had to
be closed before the advertised hour of commence-
ment in the face of many would - be patrons.
The leading equine favorite is the creamy, Nellie,
who does all the ordinary trick-pony performances,
besides a few feats of her own—and does them
apparently because she likes to, for it is next to
impossible to catch her trainer dictating to the
clever creature. On Tuesday a Govt. House party,
including Lady and the Misses Duff, patronised
the show and were greeted by a full and fashion-
able house. The programme was changed on
Wednesday, when a number of acceptable innova-
tions were introduced.

Plant the poet underground !
Upon the simple wooden cross, that throws
A thought of Heaven o’er the grassy mound,
And softens sunshine where the daisy grows,
Carve with a kindly knife—
“ He wrote for ‘Sundry Shows,’
But death came in the midst of life:
He went to see * Morocco Bound,’
Then withered like a rose.”
« # *
The most humorous items in the produc-
tion of “Morocco Bound,” at Melb. Princess’,
were certain rampagious ducks. These low-oomedy
birds appeared in place of the swans that graced
the Squire’s fish-pond at Sydney, and they broke
the monotony of the first act by frequently
scrambling out of the tank and waddling up
stage with wet feet. The ducks were thoroughly
successful in their efforts to amuse on Saturday.
If they are sacrificed to professional jealousy, and
out out of the cast for being funny behind Elton’s
back, the entertainment will lose all its animal
magnetism. One remembers the ducks with
gratitude. Otherwise, Melbourne is nob likely to
see any points that Sydney failed to discover in
the gaudy dullness of “ Morocco Bound.” It
appears to be a mass of slack-baked dialogue
covered by a thin coating of story. A few in-
genious lyrics and glimpses of female underwear
are connected with this indigestible talk, and a
poor attempt at a variety-show crowns the dump-
ling.
# * *
Judging from a book of song-words,
“ Morocco Bound ” originally contained more
music and less conversation than now. In the
present company there is no singer, male or
female, but especially female, who can be called a
sparkling vocalist. In short, the measure of
success for a show of this description depends
upon the artists who undertake to pull it
through, and these artists are, with three or
four exceptions, not up to the particular
kind of work required of them. Neither
are the exceptions very startling. Wilfred
Shine, a good comedian, is nob mercurial;
whilst Elton, mercurial as ever, can’t warble any
better than of yore. The amusing young johnnie
person is merely a glorified reminiscence of many
idiotic dudes in British and Australian melo-
drama ; and although Miss Edith Courtenay gets
much applause for her burlesque imitation of a
sassiety maiden trying to skirt-dance, it seems
probable that an intelligent amateur could bur-
lesque herself in much the same way. _ As for the
tame baritone, and several other disappointing
performers, they chiefly serve to check the action
of the piece.
** * *
“ The Irishman,” at Melbourne Royal, is
a fairly cheerful assortment of characters and
incidents selected from Boucicault’s Irish plays.
An overwhelming bhoy, in the usual drab-caped
overcoat, periodically chokes the miserable pro-
cess-server in the square black hat. Also, he cir-
cumvents the blaggardly nephew who sets the
Law upon the young master and cherishes evil
designs against that noble pathriot’s colleen. A
praste, a furious squire, a conscientious officer,
and a faithful Norah, likewise a warm-hearted
Molly, are included in the cast, and the scenery is
old and reliable. “ The Lancashire Lass ” will suc-
ceed “The Irishman.” Maggie Moore and Co.
are expected at the Royal shortly, when business
will doubtless hum. Meanwhile, the Taylor-
Carrington season is chiefly prosperous on Satur-
day nights.
** * *

(13th), at popular prices, with a variety-citwi-
comic-opera bill. Offenbach’s “M. Choufleur,”
otherwise “ Mr. Shrewsbury at Home,” is the
opera, and Miss Marie Elster has been engaged
to star in this promising experiment. Little
Percy Grainger pianoforted with the Exhibition
Concert Company on Saturday last, to the delight
of a good attendance ; and the Alexandra show
has accomplished its destiny by shutting up.
At Poverty Point.
Wilson Barrett has made a great hit with his adaptation
of Hall Caine’s novel, “The Manxman.” It is said to be
the strongest drama he has starred in since “ Silver
King.”
“ Mr. Shrewsbury at Home,” a liberal translation by
Lance Lenton of Offenbach’s “M. Choufleur restera chez
lui Id,” will be produced at Melb. Bijou as soon as the
new variety show can find room for it. “Mr. Shrews-
bury ” was tried at the Exhibition a couple of years ago,
and went merrily.
Courtneidge—he is travelling around the U.K. with fair
Miss Fortescue’s eo. and doing well—sends some indignant
remarks in a private letter upon “Go Bang,” one of the
latest London atrocities. The central figure in “Go
Bang” is an Eastern potentate, who implores one man
after another to hear a certain "spicy” story, “so very
blue,” commencing with “There was a young man of
Madras—” The joke seems to lie in everybody answering
“Ohl”—a gentle insinuation that the doggerel is very
old as well as very blue. At last the awful heathen gets
hold of a hunch of girls, and shrieks—“ There was a young
man of Madras, who—always had eggs for his tea.”
Whereupon the boss-girl winks gaily at the stalls and says,
“ Ah, that’s not the way I heard it told before." Apart
from this drivel, “Go Bang” is not considered a
brilliant success. The rest of the dialogue contains very
little Hinglish wit and humor, and even the young man
of Madras begins to pall upon sassiety after the first hour
or so.
The boy-reporter again: “Whereon the captain at
once marries Miss Warren, and others follow suit.” From
an Age criticism of “The Bush King.”
The Age’s London correspondent, speaking of Plain
Bill Holloway’s venture at Terry’s Theatre: “Mr. W. J.
Holloway has engaged an excellent company, and, with
rare self-denial for an actor-manager, does not appear in
either of the pieces which he produces.” “ Rare self-
denial ! ” Not at all. Bill is simply studying his own
interests with much more intelligence and discrimination
than he ever displayed in Australia.
The lank youth named Dorrington who shed a feeble
light over the Melb. Princess’s stage during the recent
B. and B. season is a son of those great theatrical stars,
Mr. and Mrs. W. H. Kendal.
Although Mrs. Brough farewelled at Melb. last week,
she will not go to England at once, the B. and B. Co.
having arranged a season at Hobart during the opening
weeks of the Exhibition.
“ The Bush King ” was produced at Melb. Royal under
the distinguished patronage of Lord Hopetoun, Chief
Justice Madden, the omnipresent Zox, and bookmaker
Alf. Josephs. The connection between Alf. and His Ex-
cellency is closer than might be supposed. Mrs. A.
Josephs—who, by the way, is a maternal aunt to the
author of “ The Bush King ” aforesaid—recently slipped
downstairs and broke some small bones in her nose, allee
same as Lady Hopetoun.
Tommy Hudson, who coined money at Adelaide Bijou,
sailed for India on Oct. 3, taking almost his entire co.,
including Shine. Tommy always makes a small fortune
in India, but says this is his last trip, as he means to settle
down hereafter in Adelaide, where he owns much property.
“The Phrenologist,” a comedy by Mr. John W.
M'Dougall, reaches a low literary level, but is well put
together, and has considerable vivacity and entrain.
Brightly acted, it would probably make a hit; for the
average Australian audience is frequently condemned to
laugh at much inferior humour.
Mrs. Mayor Yates comes definitely on a lecturing tour
to Australia. Piloted by R.S.S. ?
Mrs. Palmer’s benefit-concert resulted in a net balance
of £250, which amount was presented to her last week at
an afternoon-tea given by Mayor and Mayoress Snowden.
The Melb. Lyric Club proposes giving the sassiety
maidens, who can neither act nor sing, a chance to display
their nether shapeliness and expensive lingerie, by pre-
senting a ballet in their next essay, “ Marjorie.”
George Musgrove’s youngest sister, “Tripper,” is a
good amateur actress, and made a successful appearance
at the Austral Salon, the other day, when she played in
“ Twenty Minutes Under an Umbrella.”
The Melb. musical world has been startled by the news
that Mine. Christian has decided to become a nun. For
sometime past Madame has been in indifferent health,
and for months she was an inmate of St. Vincent’s
Hospital, where the Sisters of Charity nursed her with
exceeding care. She is now in Sydney, and will probably
enter conventual life as a novice of St. Vincent’s. The
Other surprise of the week is the departure for Sydney
and elsewhere, en route to Cairo, of Herr Benno Scherek,
who is attached to the Sapio-Urso combination.
Surgeon O’Hara, as a witness in the De Alba lift*
accident damages case, explained the facial injuries
received by plaintiff, and said that they were the most
dreadful he had ever witnessed. They would have killed
95 per cent, of people. His fee was 500 guineas. It was
no tinkering work, and he had received 1000 guineas for
a less difficult night’s work. George O’Neill, doctor of
dental surgery, described the injuries to Madame De
Alba’s teeth and jaws. He thought his fee of 150 guineas
more than reasonable. Dr. O’Hara, by the way, is
regarded as the champion Victorian witness. Kx.-<jr. —
Purves, Q. 0.: “ How much would you have charged had
she died next morning?” “When a patient is not in a
position to pay I would not prostitute the profession by
charging a small fee.”

Thus the London correspondent of S.A. Advertiser:
“ The ladies who play the nude figures in the ‘ living
pictures,’ now so popular at all the music-halls, do not
really show so much as Lady Henry Somerset supposes.
Their arms and legs are covered with flesh-colored and
invisible silk ‘tights,’ and the busts, &c., are papier-
mach6 imitations. Lady Henry unfortunately did not
realise this, and after a visit to the Empire or the ‘ Pav.'
(she doesn’t say which) wrote a scream of horror to the
Times. As a result the halls have been fuller than ever,
and more nude pictures are in preparation.”
Fillis, the circus man, says the weather has robbed him
of £BOOO. Something in this. Whenever an exceptionally
severe drought was on in Victorian country districts,
the advent of Fillis’s circus almost invariably broke it up,
and Fillis, too, as it turns out.
The notice of Madame Christian’s last appearance in
Melbourne makes a musical amateur think of her first
appearance in Sydney as a young woman not much over
20, very simply dressed, and with not a sign of make-up.
It was June 13th, 1873, in the old, Masonic Hall, at the
first of Arabella Goddard’s concerts, of which R. S. Smythe
was manager. The tickets were half-a-guinea ; and the
assisting vocalists were “Mrs. Smythe, Miss Christian,
and Mr. Andrew Fairfax.” The contralto was quite
unknown, and the Herald gave her just two encouraging
lines. For sixteen years from that night, not only over
all the Australias, but in South Africa and England, the
Much-Travelled never tired of sounding the praises of
“ the favourite contralto.” Every sub-editor and musical
critic knew the burden of his song. Tub Bulletin
always maintained that Smythe discovered Madame
Christian: he so persistently boomed her that his life
might be divided into two periods—before and after the
Christian era. Not only did he always include “ the
charming contralto” in his concert companies ; but some-
times he did most outrageous things. Once, in Maoriland,
he yoked her with the Rev. Charles Clark ; another lime,
in Melbourne Opera House, he arranged for her' to sine
the National Anathema at one of Archibald Forbes’ lec-
tures. If Smythe would only give us the history of those
17 years, it would be quite as entertaining as the most
interesting chapter in Daudet’s “Femme d’Artistes.”
New song, “ The Bogus Man ’’—who takes a theatre.
Expect the news that Williamson has concluded terms

While the theatre-dog is unlucky, the theatre cat is un-
killable, even after she rubs against Hamlet’s leg during
the soliloquy.
Sara Bernhardt is no lover of diamonds. She says that
they kill the best expression of the face, put out the fire
of the eyes, pale the tints of the ears, make the finest
teeth look like porcelain. “ I would wear,” she says,
“beads or Egyptian coins, but diamonds—no, no, no.”
Patchett Martin tells an English paper how he once
took an Australian actor to see Irving as Romeo. In the
middle of the hush that followed one of Irving’s best
apostrophes, Martin’s friend dug him in the ribs and ex-
claimed in a voice heard all over the theatre : “ Oh, that’s
appalling 1 You should just see me in the balcony-scene.”

A first attempt to form a Country party
in the Vic. Assembly was not very promising.
After 31 members had assembled in a committee-
room, and a chairman had touched upon the eternal
subject of rural industries, Alfred Deakin came
along as an uninvited guest to implore the
party not to form itself at all, Deakin having
peacefully withdrawn, Francis Longmore in-
timated that he couldn’t think of sitting in the
same party or same room with Staughton, the
hard-shell Tory. Exit Longmore. Then Murray,
of Warrnambool, stated that he had undergone
conversion since Patterson’s Parliament broke up,
and was now a Labor member. The flight of
Murray ensued, and the party finally decided to
adjourn its policy for the present.

“ IMITATION
is tiie
SINCE REST FLAT! ERY.”
The articles most imitated are
CLEMENTS TONIC and
FLETCHER’S PILLS.
WHY?
BECAUSE they have the best reputation and
are most in demand by the public, consequently,
every dishonest trader tries to trade on their fame
and renown.
It is really wonderful that the Australian people
are so slow to see the truth as regards the. un-
scrupulous quacks and charlatans who prey on
the credulity of the Australian public. Scarcely a
week ever passes but what the press exposes the
barefaced and shameful practices of some of th se
harpies, yet, in face of all they seem to thrive, and
the paper which hounds them down most thrives
fattest on their advertisements and cracks cham-
pagne over each balance-sheet. Clements Tonic
and Fletcher’s Pills have never yet stooped to
deception and misrepresentation. They are
scientific therapeutical agents, and evidence as to
their value can be found in every district, and
such being the case it is unnecessary to further
extol their virtues.

Parkes has been lecturing at Parkes
(N.S.W.) on—Parkes.
A certain eminent titled Australian Q.O.
began life as a “ buttons.”
West Australia, Victoria, and S.A, have
now all got natives as Premiers.
In the new Vic. Assembly there are 3
Andersons, 2 Isaacs’s, 2 Williams’s, 2 Turners and
3 Smiths.
S. M. .Herald now al ways alludes to Admiral
Bowden-Smith as “His Excellency the Naval
Commander-in Chief.”
The lady who works the oracle at the chief
Sydney spiritualistic seances is named Mellon.
A melon among pumpkins.
Already S.A. Agent-General Playford
sighs to return to Adelaide, owing to the continued
ill-health of his wife, who loathes the English
climate.
Bent, ex-M.P. for Brighton, and ex-
Speaker of Vic. Assembly, was the fattest man
in his constituency. His successor, Moule, is one
of the thinnest.
Lord Fingall, lately at Coolgardie, is
nephew of the Redemptorist missionary, Hon. and
Rev, Father Plunkett, who visited Australia some
little time back.
Street, the Footscray (Vic.) councillor, who
retired in the recent election in favor of Labor-
member Hancock, is the living double of S.A.
Premier Kingston.
Dean Marryat, who for a time is supreme
in S.A. Anglican circles, is a nephew of Capt.
Marryat, the novelist, and is probably the fattest
cleric in Australia.
“Miss Adey Brunei,” the Melbourne
reciter, is a niece of Sir William Jenner, Queen
Vic. ’s doctor. He married a Miss Adey, who was
Miss Brunei’s aunt.
Why does the authoress of “The Yellow
Aster,” who is one of the apostles of the New
Woman, allow her name to remain unchanged ?
Why isn’t she now Mrs. Womanton Caffyn ?
The first service which Vic.’s Bart.,
Clarke, rendered to his bleeding country after
being sworn in last week was to fall back on the
front Govt, bench into a sound, snoresome snooze.
W. H. Masters, founder of Melbourne’s
telephone system, who died t’other day, is the
third director of the Evening Standard who has
departed this life. William Wilson and Currie
were the others.
Both the present Vic. Minister of Defence,
Sargood, and the late Minister, R. Reid, are soft-
goods merchants. What is the connection be-
tween gunpowder and grenadine, bullets and
blankets, fire and flannels, shooting and shoddy ?
Seldom has there been a worse misnomer
than the pending Queensland “Judges Disqualifi-
cation Bill.” The Bill does not disqualify anv
Judge; it disqualifies a barrister who desires to
appear in the court over which his father presides.
Premier Kingston has added another to
his list of enemies. First Baker, then Symon,
Downer, and now Chief Justice Way. The latter,
by the way, has just made a most injudicious
attack on the Premier from the Supreme Court
bench.
Cuthbert, new Vic. Solicitor - General,
belongs to the well-known legal firm of Cuthbert,
Hamilton and Wynne, Strange to say, partner
Wynne was Postmaster-General in the Patterson
Government just routed by Cuthbert’s leader,
George Turner.

Of the new Vic. Ministry the Premier,
Attorney-General, Chief Secretary, Minister of
Lands, and Minister of Works, are natives. With
Sargood and Cuthbert in the team, however, it
will be difficult for these young Australians to
keep pace with their Liberal aspirations.
A lately-deceased Victorian plutocrat was,
during his life-time, credited with the largest
natural family in the province. The text of the
will discloses no terms for their provision, which
might be accepted as rather unnatural were it not
known that he provided for them long ago.
A son of ex-Governor Bowen, of Banana-
land, is steward of the estates of Robert Herbert,
erst Colonial Secretary of the same province.
Herbert is rich, and this young gentleman is heir-
designate to his estates. There is a romance
attached to the Bowen-Herbert friendship.
A. G. Taylor is writing in Sydney Truth
some excellent satirical articles on “John Bull’s
Calf, or the Land of the Kangaroo,” and signing
same “ Max O’Rell.” Sam Cook, general manager
of S.M. Herald, actually believed the other day
that they did come from the pen of Max O’Rell.
In S.A. Assembly, the other day, Solomon,
M.P., attacking Premier Kingston in re some
statement which he doubted, quoted Tennyson’s
verdict that “A lie which is half a truth is a
harder matter to fight.” “ Well,” retorted King-
ston, “ that is 50 per cent, above your average.”
Dr. Renwick, of Sydney, now brass-plates
himself “Sir Arthur Renwick, M.D.,” but the
public are not aware by any means that he was
bebaubled chiefly by reason of his medical prowess.
On the contrary, it strikes the world that his
knightly status is mostly as an Exhibition-boss.
Vic, Customs Minister Best has, or had,
his father as a non-clerical in the depart-
ment over which he presides. He has a brother a
letter-carrier in Melb. G.P.0., and he himself
was a clerk in the office of his father-in-law (the
late G. D. Langridge), in whose chair he now sits
at the Customs House.
Judge Hamilton, of Vic, County Court,
remarkable for incisiveness and absence of cere-
mony, recognising a frequent visitor to his court
in the witness-box, accosted him with, “Well,
Jorkins, you here again ?” “Yes, your Honor,”
replied defendant Jorkins, “I do not like to pay
away money without consulting you.”
Richardson, late Minister of Yic. Rail*
way.*, had only one arm, Williams, his successor)
has but one eye.
While Justice Williams was summing-up
in the big railway-case and squirting verbal vitriol
over Speight, Richard walked out, and turned
round looking the Judge earnestly in the face.
Next day Mr. Williams’ son-associate handed a
note from the Bench to Speight’s solicitors, telling
Richard that he was not to do it again.
Dear Bulletin, —Miss Muffy Chirnside
as was is a brick. The Yankee millionairess
squanders thousands to marry the worn-out, dissi-
pated, pauper-holder of a title. Miss Chirnside
sacrifices thousands to avoid such cattle, and
marries a son of the soil without title. She
deserves to be the mother of Australia’s first
President.—V.
Some Melb. University constituents want
to get rid of a professor or two. So at a recent
meeting of Council there were references to an
imaginary University, where one professor in-
dulges in “ senile witticisms,” and another in
“inebriated twaddle.” The sensible plan would
be to prescribe terms of five years instead of life-
appointments, as now.
Lord Brassey visits Australia early next
year in the old Sunbeam. Mrs. Brassey No. 2is
young and handsome. In India, last January,
when a travelling Australian visited the yacht,
Brassey presented him thus : “ Mr. Brigaloo, of
Australia, my dear. Mr. Brigaloo visited the
yacht when we were in Sydney some years ago.”
Awkward for Mrs. Brassey No. 2.
Explorer Carr-Boyd has christened two
peaks off Murchison way the Argus and Austra-
lasian in compliment, as he reports, to Wilson
and Mackinnon, and these journals now refer to
him as “ the courageous and competent bush-
traveller.” This is a forgiving world. Seven years
ago, Carr-Boyd demanded .£5OOO libel-damages
from the Argus, and the case went into court.
Gov. Hopetoun had a birthday on the day
that Turner’s new Government was born and the
Patterson gang went out. The young man was
35, though he only looks 20, save when his hat is
off. Patterson wished him many happy returns
of the day, to which His Ex. murmured abstrac-
tedly, “The same to you!” Patterson retailed
this afterwards and said, “ I wonder whether he
meant it?”

An Argus criticism of “ Rolf Boldre-
wood’s” latest work The sketches of life in
‘The Summer Isles of Eden’ are full of the
purple light of the Southern seas, and may be
compared favourably with Mr. Stevenson’s more
elaborate, more sentimental, and more artificial
pictures of the same world. ‘ Rolf Boldrewood ’
has succeeded in making a very entertaining
book.” That’s so—he made it. Which must be
all very pleasing to Louis Becke.
Minister Garrard has accepted an invita-
tion to eat a dinner cooked by the girls of the
Parramatta Industrial School. Mr. Garrard is a
sound, practical cook, and has for years made
personal study of the best ways of producing the
most nutritious food. He often lands home with
a bag of some fancy meal for breakfast use, and
spends an hour or so making a sample dish. Of
all things farinaceous he knows the qualities, and
of sound, digestible food generally he can talk with
scientific experience.
The other day there was mighty jubilation
in Sydney over the departure of a number of
military persons to India, whither they were going
to learn their trade. One of them is an ingenuous
youth who joined the English navy as a midship-
man, or something of that sort, in ’63—31 years
ago—so that he cannot now be much below 50.
It is hardly fair that N.S. Wales should have to
pay for his instruction at that age. If he doesn’t
already know his business, it is too late to learn it.
The day after the general elections a silly
rumor was rife in Melb. that ex-Speaker Bent had
suicided. A sub-editor of one of the evening
“pypers” telephoning to the House, was answered
byTom in person. This duinb-foundered the scribe
fora second, and Tom enquired simply, “What
do you want?” “Oh,” replied the newspaper-
man, “I wished to know who are the candidates
for the Speakership ? ” “ Dunno,” replied Tommy,
as he rung off. A good “ bluff.”
Bell, pioneer and solitary settler on Sun-
day Island, in the Kermadecs, landed in Auckland
(M.L.) from the cruiser Rapid the other day.
With his Kermadeo mail-bag slung on his shoul-
der he stepped on to Queen-street wharf bare-
footed. When skipper Fairchild, of the Govt,
steamer Hinemoa, tried to bluff him off the
island, Bell threatened to bombard him. Bell has
a famdy of three children ; two of his daughters,
who are in Maoriland, are said to be as strong as
three average men.
The supporters of a certain Melb. news-
paper-proprietor, beaten at the Victorian general
election, were arranging a banquet to console him
for his defeat. The poor little man actually sug-
gested to a member of committee that he would
appreciate it a great deal more if they would
devote the money to paying his election expenses.
As a result, it is likely he will get neither banquet
nor money. .ZEsop’s fable over again : the dog
with the bone and the reflection in the water —
dog’s name being Toby.
-It is estimated, by those likely to know,
that S. A. Premier Kingston, Q.C., sacrifices, at
least, ,£2OOO a year by neglecting his profession
for politics. During the last 15 months he has
kept so assiduously to his Ministerial work that
he has barely been as many times inside his
private office, and his late partner has severed
connection with him, because of the consistent
manner in which he puts State matters before
professional ones. “Some day I’ll strike it rich
in mining,” he says, for he is a plucky speculator.

The writer of the lately-published book
Ihe Lives of Twelve Bad Men ; original studies
of eminent scoundrels,” includes Ned Kelly, the
bushranger, in his Valhalla of villains. He makes
a great mistake about Kelly’s hanging. He draws
a picture of Ned cowering just before his execu-
tion, and having to be supported to the gallows.
This certainly does not agree with the testimony
of those who were present to see the bushranger
die. They say that Kelly “ pegged out ” with un-
flinching courage, manifesting neither desperate
bravado nor craven fear.
Tawhaio, the defunct Maori king, left
behind him a harem of 37 wives. To prevent the
aged king, in his peregrinations among his sub-
jects, adding to the list of favorites, the sternest
and most forbidding-looking of his wahines, by
common consent, accompanied his majesty. She
sat beside Tawhaio in the buggy during his
rounds, and effectually frowned upon the chiefs
who, according to immemorial custom, pressed
their brown-skinned maidens upon his Nibs. Thus
the string of concubines who occupied the royal
whare was kept within the number stated. 3
The inventor of the impressionist-school
of painting is said to have stumbled upon it qnite
promiscuously. He was accustomed to work in a
pair of canvas pants. One day he accidentally
sat down on a palette covered with miscellaneous
paints. Being a lazy man, and an exhibition
being at hand, he cut the result of this accident
out of his pants, framed it, and sent it along. ]>
was received with rapture, and purchased by th ( .
local National Gallery trus f ees. Ever since then
the impressionist-school has been steadily grow*
Amongst the visitors to Sydney block last
week was the octogenarian Wm. Richman Watt
a contemporary of Wentworth’s, and member for
Carcoar in the first N.S.W. Parliament under the
extended franchise. Mr. Watt’s homestead is at
Bumbaldry, near Grenfell, where the Watt family
were pioneers before the solitude of the neighbor-
liood was broken by the “ Wedtiin ” rush in ’6G
Prior to that, Mr. Watt prepared to build a house
at the old spot, and had all the materials upon the
ground, but the rush altered his plans. The
“material ” lies there still unused, and Mr. Walt
rests content with the hospitable old homestead.
Among things not generally known is the
fact that Q. Govt, offered John Robb .£lOO 000 to
square bis =£262,000 deviations claim over the
Cairns railway. Robb refused. The Govt, was
“careful ” in the appointment of arbitrators, with
the result that the contractor recovered .£2O 000
at a cost of =£26,000 in costs. Robb made’the
usual mistake of not laying to his book. As long
as he kept to railway-contracting he made money
(£.124,000 on the Cairns contract alone), and in
’B9 he had a credit-balance of nearly half-a-
million; but when he started squatting, sugar-
planting and land-speculating he lost band-over-
fisb
Mrs. Macalister, who died lately in Bris-
bane at an advanced age, was a connecting link
between the Queensland of to-day and that of the
pre-Separation period. Her husband was a poli-
tician of narrow, unsympathetic, but restlessly
active proclivities, who managed, against great
odds, to grasp the Premiership. He left no per-
manent impression, for he was constitutionally
incapable of nourishing large ideas. The part lie
took in the fight for Queensland autonomy was
all provided for him, and it was his mere restless-
ness which made him notable. The premiership
he practically bartered for the billet of Agent-
General.
Dear Bulletin, —Your recent par. re Mr.
George Anderson, Master of Melb. Mint, recalls
to the writer the good old pre-ballot times when
Mr. Anderson was one of the three candidates for
Glasgow. I question if any other candidate in
the United Kingdom was “barracked’’ more by
the Catholics, who made his annual address to his
constituents, in the City Hall, an occasion not yet
forgotten by Mr. Anderson. Mr. A. received his
appointment from Gladstone, who took that
means of getting rid of a member who had an
unpleasant manner of asking questions which it
did not always suit the Liberal Government to
answer.—Yours, An Elector of the Time.
When the Redmonds were lecturing in
Maoriland, a well-known Auckland Irishman took
them in, boarded and lodged them on a royal
scale, and drove them around in bis trap to see all
the available scenery. A few years later, the
Parnellite disruption came, and "their late host
heard with profound disgust of the attitude of the
Redmonds in the matter. Whereupon he wrote
to the elder of the pair something to this effect:
“ Sorr,—When you were out here I had great
pleasure in driving yon round to see the country.
If yon come out here again I’ll have great pleasure
in driving yon over an embankment, and breaking
your neck. Yours, etc. .”
The combative Speight has moved for a
new trial against Melbourne Age Syme. He
wants to rechristen the latter “Doomsday David.”
A poetical termination to the suit should be the
mutual bankruptcy of both the litigants, with the
bailiffs in the Age office and Speight ploughing
the free and bounding billows in the direction of
Argentina. In the meantime ;

Aristocratic Enjoyment for Democrats.
Since tobacco was first introduced in Europe, or
at least in England, about three hundred years
ago, its consumption has ever been increasing,
like coffee and tea. Up to the last century a pipe
was a luxury within reach only of the so-called
“ better classe,” as it was so expensive that tor a
shilling people only got in tobacco the weight of
that coin in silver. Times have undoubtedly
changed for the better in this respect, as, in our
days, any tobacconist will give you a packet con-
taining eight of Frossard’s Cavours for a similar
piece of money. These cigars are mild and
aromatic, and are certainly the best value that has
ever been brought to Australia. *

Crick is playing for the leadership of
N.S.W. Opposition —and with Dibbs’ consent.
Hindle’s “three bad men,” by the way,
all got back to N.S.W. Parliament. St. Hindle
didn’t.
Election expenses : M‘Cracken, £1500;
Deakin, <£soo. Last election (1891) : Deakin,
£4O; Morris, .£4O.
If, as N.S.W. Premier Reid says, pay-
ment of members is on its trial, then all demo-
cratic institutions are on their trial.
Under the new S.A. progressive taxes the
S A. co., the great absentee British land-syndicate,
finds its taxes increased by over £2OOO a year.
No head of expense in Australia has grown
so rapidly as the N.S.W. military vote. Thirty
years ago it was £30,000; to-day, it is close on
£400,000.
The two last Premiers of N.S. Wales—
Dibbs and Parkes—are self-confessedly ignorant
of the land question, though one has been in the
House twenty and the other forty years.
Adrian Knox, M.P. (N.S.W.): “No
person who does not follow some occupation for
his livelihood should be allowed to be a member
of Parliament.” A Labor-member : “What about
Henry Parkes ? ”
Almost all nations were represented by
bunting in the Sydney Eightffiour procession—
with the exception of Australia, The Southern
Cross was nowhere to be seen in the demonstra-
tion of flags (and advts.).
Cables say that the Imperial authorities
have consented to the appointment of Chief
Justice Way as S.A. Acting - Governor after
Kintore’s departure. Which means that South
Australia is to have her own Way.
Young man Kirton, who voted with the
Patterson Government, and was rejected at Bal-
larat, in a recent speech : “ I will not be driven.”
“No,” yelled a well-known carpenter; “you’re
like a soft nail —you won't be driven.”
“ Bedrock ” : The real, effective Vic.
Cabinet would be Premier, Syme; Treasurer,
Hancock; Railways, Speight; Customs, Murray
Smith ; Attorney-General, Marshall Lyle; Educa-
tion, Zox ; Lands, Hirsch ; Works, W. Gaunson.
The Q. Parliamentary Labor-party was
against the now-collapsed shearers’ strike. Local
leaders, however, were for it, and were eventually
rewarded by having to call off the strike, acknow-
ledge failure, and see their orders disobeyed by
their followers.
The plausible C, J. Moran, whom the
Cooigardie men were fools enough to return to

Govt, supporter, and backs the Forrest crowd at

a Sydney D.T. leader-writer :
From its many harbor strongholds, predatory cruisers

The Age handles the land-tax very gin-
gerly. It gives the Government a programme:
Tariff Revision, Railway, Electoral, Parlia-
mentary and Banking Reform, but conveniently
forgets the tax on unimproved land values. David

Re Adrian Knox’s woefully unsuccessful
attempt to abolish payment of members in N.S.W.,
the only wonder is that such persons as some of
those who supported him dared rise in their
places and speak on the very evening of the day
when they had pouched every man his £25 cheque.
The spleen-swollen features of Backbone
Patterson were a matter of comment at the swear-
ing-in of Vic. Parliament last week. The few
peevish strictures he attempted to cloak with a
srocodile leer, resembled the squeaking of a dis-
appointed rodent attacking a leaden corpse-casket.
Abbott, M.P. (N.S.W.): “Garrard and
Cameron managed to attend to their Parlia-
mentary duties when there was no payment of
members and when they were employed at manual
labor. Yes, they “managed.” Garrard (loq.) ;
“I wouldn’t ask a dog to go through what I
experienced then.”
It has been estimated, in view of approach-
ing income-tax, that the bulk of N.S.W. lawyers
and doctors will oppose the Bill, and that the
apposition will be fiercest among those of the
lowest incomes. Men in these professions, whose
incomes barely reach the exempt figures, are
expected to fight the Bill tooth and nail.
Graham Berry’s unanimous selection as
Speaker of Vic. Assembly is a recognition of the
Liberal veteran’s long and arduous public life. A
point generously overlooked by the House is the
question of his physical fitness for the post. Will
the long night sittings overtask the old man’s
great age, and fast increasing bodily feebleness?
In Victoria the Jews form less than one-
two hundredth of the population—and more than
one-twelfth of the Legislature. In other words,
the Chosen have got sixteen times more than their
“proper” share of representation. Truly the seed
of Abraham is irrepressible and the time is steadily
corning nearer when Cohen shall own the earth.
It must have made Max Hirsch think hard
when he saw that Frank Madden had positively
refused, on behalf of the conservatives, to let him
on the committee of the proposed Melb. Reform
Club. Max was prime Freetrade barracker at
elections and supplied all the brains used in the
conservative campaign. He is a sucked orange.
Throw him away.
Remarkable that, although every Victorian
Crown Minister procures an elaborate portfolio
and travelling-bag, at State expense, lettered
“ Commissioner of ,” he never leaves it behind
for his successor. Consequently, every change of
Ministry means a junior civil-servant’s salary in
portfolios alone. And these are the men who talk
of “rigid economy !”
The S.A. beer excise was first collected on
the night of Tuesday, Sept. 25, yet, at 2d. per
gallon, the Treasurer hopes to realise .£30,000 by
the end of the financial year, the average annual
consumption per head being reckoned at about
14 gals. The hotelkeepers are making an attempt
to squeeze about double the amount of the extra
beer and spirit duties out of the drinking public.
When the Victorian Parliament assembled
last week the greatness of Patterson’s fall was
A SABBATH SCENE IN ANCIENT EGYPT.
(From sculptures—by The Bulletin’s own Egyptologist).
Here you see Amen-Hoteph taking a bottle out of Cheops’ Pyramid, where he had
secreted it on the previous day.
NOTE: -Tickel-en-Rhibb, Mr. Hoteph’s wife’s mother, had been elected Lady Mayor of
Thebes, and had suppressed all clubs. In fact, for a period of 2000 years the bulwarks
of Egyptian liberty were shaken to their foundation by drastic Sabbatarian legislation.
Tickel was finally mummified, and a reaction set in.
realised for the first time. His following on the
Opposition benches numbered exactly six. The
Opposition corner —otherwise the independent
oppositionists —were 26 strong, but the lonesome
half-dozen afore-mentioned were all that remained
to represent the alleged Foreign-trade reaction in
Victoria.
Re selling State railways to “ private
enterprise.” Bagehot, admitting that there
would be no necessity for a second Chamber if the
Commons were perfect, points out that the latter
has, or then had, 200 members for railways.
Moral: When the State does not control the rail-
ways the railway companies (as in America)
control the State. He who controls locomotion
controls the nation.
The law of One Man one Vote, passed
three years ago by a “cronk” Vic. Assembly,
which knew quite well what was going to happen,
lies buried tor the present in the Leg. Council.
The new Labor Party will have it resurrected,
and a row will ensue. Sargood, who seems
destined to bring the Turner Government to ruin,
is a Some Men Six Votes kind of person. That’s
where his Liberalism comes in.
The most interested, and sometimes the
most excited, auditors in Q. Parliament are the
Labor - party’s women folks. Labor - leader
Glassey’s daughter is one of the most regular
attendants, and appears to regard all her father’s
caustic criticisms of the crooked Nelson Govern-
ment as so many huge jokes. “Society” ladies
stand no show against the labor ladies, who dis-
respectfully elbow them into the back row.
Nominee M. ! sL.C. make bad censors of
M.’sL.A. When they reproach members of As-
sembly with having worked underhandedly and
overhandedly for seats they direct public attention
to their own exertions for seats in the Council.
The average M.L.A. is not a high-minded indi-
vidual in his canvass, but the average M.L.C.’s
labored process of securing Ministerial favor is,
while more secret, not a bit more elevated. And,
on another topic. Members of the N.S.W.
Council sneeringly denounced Assembly members
the other day for their anxious grabbing at Public
Works Committee billets, but when their own
turn came avaricious indecency was as scandalously
exhibited.
Australian Star, stigmatising ex-Chief
Justice Lilley as the Judge whom Windeyer van-
quished, denounces him as an agitator who proves
by his attitude to the recently-carried coercion
bill that he does not want the reign of sound law
in Queensland. That is a myopic view of Lilley’s
relation to the question raised. Lilley’s objection
to the new “coercion” law is that he considers
the old laws, if enforced, sufficient for the occasion.
A wealth-tax in Victoria is impossible just
now, apart from the question of “ paper ” liabili-
ties, which numberless affluent persons would
crawl behind and use as shelters from the tax-
collector. Its imposition would open up the awful
question of reconstructed bank deposit values. A
man credited with .£lO,OOO worth of deposits at
current market rates might maintain that he was
justified in valuing them at next to nothing. A
delicate matter to bring into Court.
Within the last two years 11 lives
have been lost in the Ipswich and Bundamba
colleries. Boodlewraith is not troubled thereat.
Within the last 2J years there have been
not fewer than 12 murders in the Mackay dis-
trict, and the perpetrators are still at large.
Boodlewraith is not troubled thereat. But imme-
diately woolsheds are burnt down, out comes the
Northern Fat Man with a Peace Preservation
Bill worthy of the direst days of Buckshot Forster.
So far, a title has been anything but a
Mascotte in Australasian politics. Stout has not
yet recovered from his bebaublement, and John
Downer is out —not in. MTlwraith’s star is on
the down grade ; Parkes seems pretty well snuffed
out; Dibbs howls at the outer gate; Patterson is
bust; and Berry in the Vic. general election dared
not use his title on his election-bills, but had to
appeal to the electors as plain Graham Berry.
And these examples by no means exhaust the
list.
Kingston’s Land and Income-tax proposals
are nearly as drastic as the present Maoriland
imposts. S.A. Govt, proposes to double the
present £d. tax on unimproved land-values com-
mencing at £5000; income-tax of 4|d. on all
incomes derived from personal exertion up to
<£looo ; fid. to Is. above .£lOOO ; additional tax of
20 p.c. on unimproved land-values owned by
absentees. The Govt, has the solid support of the
Labor-members, and claims a majority in both
Assembly and Council.
In 1883 the people of Maoriland drank
only half as much spirits per head as in 1878, only
a little more than one-third as much wine, and
less than one-third as much imported beer, while
the demand for “ colonial ” beer had fallen off
about 20 per cent. Taking him in all his various
thirsts, the Maorilander only drinks half as much
as he did 16 years ago. Which shows either
that anti-liquor legislation makes the people give
up drink, or else that when people give up drink
they take to passing anti-liquor legislation.
Melb. Akgus lately gave way to the depravity
of letters signed “Worker,” “Workingman,”
“Worker No. 2,” etc., etc., in which the deluded
toiler and moiler pathetically pleads for political
guidance, and displays the self-abasement of a
conscientious jackass in such sentences as this ;
“We have no knowledge as to which is the right
side to take in the coming election,” “ We look to
you as the best judge to decide for us.” This
kind of “working man,” if he is not an office
spook, ought to have a keeper to appeal to.
Alex. Brown, M.L.C. (N.S.W.), holds an
“ office of profit ” as Chairman of Hunter River
Water Board, and sticks like a limpet to his
M.L.C.-ship. The Constitution Act, while for-
bidding a man to hold any but a Cabinet office
with a seat in the Assembly, is silent about the
Council. An M.L.C. might be a railway-porter
so far as the law is concerned. The Foreign-trade
M.L.A. who “ drew attention ’ to Brown—Brown
belongs to the Dibbs faction—is alleged to hold a
similar “office of profit ” himself, by the yvay.
The German opinion of the Costa Rica
Packet business is that the Dutch Government
erred only in being too lenient ! The haughty
Australian, it appears, wants to boss the Malay
archipelago as well as Oceania, “and, if Sydney
could have its way,” opines the Cologne Gazette,
“even the German flag would fly no longer in
New Guinea or the Marshalls.” The Dutchmen
are counselled to scout the .notion of compensa-
tion, and force the matter to arbitration, “ the
result of which cannot be doubted.” Apparently
not—if sauerkraut is to have a finger in the pie 1
Ninian Melville, after fourteen consecu-
tive years in N.S.W. Parliament and four years in
the well-paid billet of chairman of committees, has
bankrupted—liabilities dllBoo, assets .£2OO. His
schedule describes him simply as an unemployed
person residing in a cheap Sydney suburb. Mel-
viJle had a profitable undertaking business onca
upon a time, but he left it for politics, and then
politics left him, and in his old age he goes tha
way of politicians in general. The man who can
get a living out of corpses should stick to corpses.
In the long run, they crush ' than
live voters.
N. Premier Reid’s little explanation .
“ I have always opposed payment of members and
shall continue to do so until it is proved that it
produces a better House. After the Act was
passed in ’B9, I paid .£550 back into the Treasury,
but when those members who had supported the
system and those who had opposed it had been
before the electors, I felt that to refuse payment
any longer would seem like an attempt to acquire
praise for a sort of fictitious credit over one’s
fellows.” Which, being interpreted into the
vulgate, meaneth : “ After the Act was passed in
’B9, I paid £550 back into the Treasury, but when
I—ahem ! got married,” &c., &c. Why doesn’t
George own up like a man—a married man?
The unspeakable greed of the Northen
planters provokes them to the most suicidal policy,
as witness the decimation of their obedient bond-
slaves, the Polynesians. Witness also the steri-
lisation through too frequent cropping of some of
the fairest sugar-lands on the Mary. Witness
also this—the latest : A cane-disease is breaking
out in the Burnett and Isis districts. The cane
begins to run to a gummy liquid which it tends to
secrete instead of sugar. The disease appears to
have originated in the Woongarra district, and is
said to have arisen from the too frequent planting
of cane in the one soil. Northern planters will
crop cane 15 years from the one field, and then
look paralysed when the exhausted alluvial cries,
“ Hold, enough !”
Said a business man, recently (after busi-
ness hours): “We have travelled far since
Shakespeare’s time. He depicted the spirit that
wanted flesh in payment of debt. Shylock is
dead, and the world in these days of bankruptcy-
laws, big secure life - policies, and Married
Women’s Property Acts, to say nothing of effec-
tual nuptial and post-nuptial settlements, no
longer allows payment in flesh. It first limited
the creditor to the skin, then the singlet. After-
wards the creditor fought for the right to levy on
the waistcoat. Then he struggled for the debtor’s
coat; to-day he is making a last despairing clutch
at his overcoat,” The moral conveyed in this
parable is that commercial transactions are very
nearly down to the naked bedrock of confidence.
Dear Bulletin, —Can you tell me why
Eight Hours’ day was not considered a “ public ”
holiday for the N.S.W. law courts? The Judges
refused to adjourn cases over Monday, Oct. Ist,
on the ground that they did not recognise
Demonstration • day as “a holiday for Us.”
Why? The birthdays of Queen Victoria of
England, and Albert Edward, Prince of Wales,
are considered fitting occasions for closing the
law-courts all over Australia. They are holidays
by Act of Parliament. Demonstration-day—the
people’s day—is only a holiday by proclamation,
and may be disregarded at will by the people’s
highly-paid servants —or masters ? Isn’t any
“democratic” representative going to call atten-
tion to this piece of insolence ? Or is Demon-
stration-day going to be merely a holiday for the
“lowah ordabs, you know ?”— Democrat.
Vic. Attorney-General Isaacs must fight
for re-election at Bogong against Ferguson, ex-
member for the Ovens district. I. Isaacs ran his
little brother Johnny for the latter constituency
and ran Ferguson out, wherefore that irate
follower of Patterson has betaken himself to
Bogong out of pure cussedness. It is a droll busi-
ness altogether, this hauling of brother Johnny
into politics. The original Isaacs, who carries
most of the family brains in his own personal
head, is likely to find Johnny a serious handicap
as time goes on, although the fraternal alliance
will give him the weight of a dual voter in the
House. The Attorney-General will count as two
on a division —self and Johnny. Meanwhile, he
has nothing to fear from Ferguson’s opposition,
except a little expense. Fergy is on a wild goose-
chase, with a reasonable chance of losing his £5O
deposit.
Editor Bulletin,— “ No venality about
Speight.” No. But Speight had something about
him that put away the millions just as easily.
Goodfellowship. Speight went about in triumphal
tours putting gilt on every M.P.’s gingerbread.
He was even the good fellow with such as C. E.
Jones and Mason. They asked for stone and
took the cake. “ The money was there to spend,’'
say you. There were Parliamentary appropria-
tions, and Speight exceeded everyone of them.
He is no construction manager, but I would take
him on again for the traffic. He is a Napoleon
for knowing the names of his men, and is popular
with them to idolatry. I would put over him a
man who doesn’t know anything about railways—
say Weigall, Vic. Curator of Intestate Estates.
Weigall is a man who has developed economy to a
vice, and a Speight-Weigall combination would
make the railways.—F.
The Bulletin is not in a position to pro-
nounce on the merits of the subjoined case, but it
is a ghastly thing that so many civil-servants and
ex-civil-servants, all over Australia, can truthfully
utter similar wails of despair :
Editor Bulletin, —“Civil-servants always vote Conser-
vative, they also vote Freetrade.” Although a C.S.
(believing him a Protectionist and Democrat) I voted for
Dibbs. Because I did not see fit to dishonor myself, in a
way I cannot here specify, I incurred the enmity of a
high official, who sent in several false reports to the
“chief,” with the result that when retrenchment came 1
had to go. I held a responsible position, and had the
confidence alike of the public and every superior officer I
had ever served under. 1 worked an average of three
hours per day overtime for years (without extra pay) in
the interests of the service. I was on permanent staff,
and yet dozens of temporary officers were retained, and
one was appointed to my position. Although I had
high legal opinion that the C.S. Act had been violated in
my case, and there were many aggravating circumstances
surrounding it which increased the hardship, the Dibbs
Government declined either a reconsideration or an
investigation into my charges of malicious misrepresenta-
tion against the officer indicated. What matters how
O. vote after that? They seemingly have no rights to
conserve.— Ex-Civil- Servant.

The ghoulish humor of sexual murders is
phosphorescently presented in the latest Melb.
wife-butchery case, where, as also in the recent
life-bashiug performance upon his paramour by a
negro at Port Melbourne, the little home-nest of
the principals was ornamented with gaily-colored
prints of “The Springtide of Love.” One more
illustration of the fact that True Love—the boiling,
passionate, red-hot lava of the amative animal—
leads to more murders than any other known
cause—drink included! On the tombstone of
every felon who murders his wife might be written
truthfully —“ He loved too largely.” Extremes
meet. The alpine penumbras of Love infringe
momentarily on the border-line of Hate, and the
electric crash of opposing clouds loosens the
lightning of unpremeditated murder. No wife
should be satisfied that her husband truly loves
her until he starts to saw her throat, or trepan
her skull with a meat-axe.
• • •
Mrs. Wash, murdered by her husband in
Melb. last week, was an Adelaide schoolmate of
Martha Needle, now under sentence of death.
Mrs. Wash, at the time of the Needle poisoning
discovery, told a friend that the condemned
woman, as a child, had a penchant for toxicology
and continually bought poison to “ fix-up ” cats
and dogs. Which seems to place the unfortunate
Martha in the light of a poisoning maniac. By the
strange workings of Fate, one woman was
butchered last week, while her schoolmate lies in
gaol awaiting a violent death on the scaffold.
* * *
Maggie Moore, at the end of the “ Struck
Oil ” season at Sydney Royal, gave a matinee for
the local charities. The Benevolent Asylum
authorities marched such of the recipients of their
doles as would accept tickets a mile through the
most crowded streets from the Asylum to the
theatre. Naturally, all who had a rag of self-
respect left declined with or without thanks.
The man or woman who would submit to such a
degradation for 2£d. a day, or its equivalent in
food, is lost beyond redemption.

Editor Bulletin, —“The much-discussed
Ocean-street (Sydney) cable-tram has to earn
£lB every day to pay interest on construction.”
Yes, and £l4 to pay wages of gripinen and con-
ductors, £lO for renewal of cables, and probably £lO
more for coal and engine-room expenses. Say
£52 ; besides miscellaneous expenses, wages of
clerks and other workers, which should bring the
total up to £6O. The engines were expected to
have power enough to allow an extension of the
line, but it seems they are no more than enough
for the present length. The whole is a monument
of engineering ignorance. The cable-train system
is itself a blunder except for straight lines with
fairly uniform slopes. The Ocean-street route has
three ascents, two descents, and nine curves in 2J
miles. The consequent frictional resistance is
excessive, and the wear and tear in proportion.
The adoption of the faulty grip is the least of the
blunders perpetrated.—Tangent.
* * «
Since Speight won his farthing damages
from David Syme, several hundred of the latter
gentleman’s admirers have written him enthu-
siastic letters of congratulation and enclosed a
bright, new farthing apiece, with an intimation
that they are prepared to see David through, and
a request that he would dump down the micro-
scopic coin before Speight and get rid of him for
ever. Considering that it isn’t the farthing
damages but the £40,000 costs that weigh on
Syme’s mind, this is a fine example of polished
irony. Think of the feelings of a man with a
£40,000 legal bill in one hand, and a wildly con-
gratulatory letter and a farthing in the other.
• • *
A juryman’s statement that the Speight v.
Syme verdict was the result of a compromise
makes the matter a little worse than it looked on
paper. As regards one libel—viz., that Speight
had increased the average cost of each employe
nearly 50 per cent.—eight jurors found that it was
substantially true, when it was proved to have
been a substantial untruth, founded on reckless
miscalculation. Yet the three dissentients, who
were quite clear as to the merits of this particular
issue, agreed to meet the Syme faction by allow-
ing that the error was “fair comment.” The
influence of a certain bank-prosecution can be
dimly perceived in this kindly way of regarding
false figures.
Some curiosity has been caused in certain
circles by the quashing of a charge laid against a
well-known and handsome lady of having stolen
a valuable diamond-ring. It appears that the
ring was taken about 18 months ago from a lady
lying unconscious in illness, and was the object of
an unavailing police-search on the premises of the
defendant. Recently, in the exercise of another
search-warrant for goods belonging to another
lady, what the loser claims as the missing ring
was found among a quantity of other jewellery in
a box in defendant’s house, and was taken away
by the detective and identified by the owner. An
information was sworn, but at the police court
the case was adjourned from week to week on
plea of defendant’s illness, till the week before last,
when it disappeared altogether from the charge-
sheet, no intimation of the proceedings appearing

Another gem from the fluent pen of Melb.
Herald’s own delightful cable-fakir. It is about
a man murdering a woman at Southampton
(Eng.):—
Something, however, occurred to cause a disagreement
between them, with the result that the man suddenly
attacked his companion with a razor, which he drew from
his pocket.
Seizing the woman by the hair, he slashed savagely at
the unfortunate creature’s throat, with such effect that
he killed her on the spot.
“ Which he drew from his pocket.” “Seizing the
woman by the hair.” Booh !

m m m
A Maoriland schoolmaster married his
pupil-teacher, who, however, went up at a later
period to pass her exam. This was all well
enough, but the master subsequently applied for
the customary State allowance of .£lO for coaching
her, and the inspector wanted to know if the
department should pay a man for “ instructing ”
his wife? Why shouldn’t it ? The fact that the
woman was married is no affair of the State’s.
• • •
A poor woman was lately brought to
Maryborough (Vic.) hospital, bleeding internally
through miscarriage. The doctor in charge
refused to admit her, as she had no “order ” and
the hospital was “not intended for such cases.”
Then a constable appealed to the Ladies’ Bene-
volent Society ; but they could do nothing, as the
woman’s character was not good—which refusal
should forever damn the benevolent ladies (with
good characters), who aren’t women. Finally, after
being carted about for four hours, the wretched
creature was taken to gaol. At the gate of nearly
every State-subsidised hospital in Australia lies a
corpse killed by “regulations.”

From proceedings of the Wanganui hos-
pital and Charitable Aid Board :
Milk. —The chairman said that in the past the Board
had been paying 3d. per quart for fresh milk, and he did
not see why, if skimmed milk was good enough for mem-
bers of the Board, they should feed the inmates of the
Jubilee Horae on fresh milk. After a brief discussion the
matter was left in abeyance.
A few inches above, the same chairman—a rich
ex-publican —figures as having sent, with a wreath,
a most affecting letter to the father of a hospital
doctor recently deceased, in which it was piously
hoped that the “ Almighty might strengthen him
to bear his sad bereavement with fortitude.” A
unanimous vote of thanks was accorded to the
kindly and considerate Bung.
* # *
Another point, not generally known, about
the N.S.W. civil service superannuation fund.
Awhile back a lady-teacher applied, on account of
ill-health, for two months’ leave, which the
authorities granted her, without pay. When,
after resuming work, she got her next cheque,
there was deducted from it the 4 per cent, super-
annuation on not only that month’s salary, but
also a like percentage for the two months previous
that she had been away on sick-leave, and for
which she had received no remuneration ! It was
explained to her that payment into the fund must
be continuous ; failing which, contributors cannot,
in any future time, claim a penny of compensation
from the wild-cat insurance department.
• * *
Wages at the new Sydney market excava-
tions —for the hire of a man, horse and cart, and
to cart daily a minimum of 11 loads to Pyrmont
quarries, more than a mile away, 11s. 6d.
Further, it is stated that some of the carters are
not working “ on their own,” but for a large
carrier from whom they rent horses and carts.
And the Sydney cabmen now are having but a poor
time. Lately said one of their number, a sober
and industrious man, to the present writer: “I
have been a cab-driver for much over 20 years,
and have brought up a large family decently, but
I have still three children at home. For the
last three years I have earned, on an average,
just £2 4s. 9d. per week, out of which I have had
to keep myself and family and two horses. lam
now old, and despair of being able to continue.
There is nothing for it but the poorhouse, as I had
to mortgage my turn-out for £25 at 40 per cent,
when the bad times came on. Look at me to-day.
I have been out since this morning at nine o’clock,
and it is now 10.30 p.m. All I have earned is two
shillings.”

# * *
According to a Sydney Telegraph cor-
respondent, one lady who last year was a pupil of
a leading light in N.S.W. Art Society, exhibiting
II pictures, this year bobs up as a judge of other
people’s work, and shows 12 pictures—the maxi-
mum permitted. Seems a singular thing that
when a R. A. or Salon artist can only paint one or
two good pictures in a year, a N.S.W. ditto
should get through 12—all good enough to hang,
and all good enough to crowd out other artists'
work altogether.

A few of the Rue Australians have taken
unto themselves in marriage the local Paraguayan
maiden, and one of the troubles of the settlement
is that the Australian female contingent rigidly
boycott these Guarani girls, whom they regard as
social inferiors. The principle of true mateship is
hardly on speaking terms with itself now in Rue
Australia.

It is not generally known that a pupil once
expelled from a N.S.W. State-school cannot after-
wards be enrolled in any other public-school in
the province. But it is only in very rare and
extreme cases that expulsion is resorted to.

Dear Bulletin, —“ Spagnoletti’s ” com-
miseration of uniformed nurses is three-parts
wasted, because it’s for the uniform three-parts of
them take to the calling. Woman—more emotion-
ally ostentatious than man—is credited with the
kinder heart; purring more gracefully round a
sick-bed she is assumed to be the gentler nurse.
Every man who has been both he and she-tended
knows better ; but, while this venerable gag sur-
vives, the stoniest-hearted Jezebel that ever
cursed the race will hanker after the reputation—
and the garments—of the “ministering angel.”

Another example of the dreadfully bad
luck which attends on churches and churchgoers.
Twenty-three years ago, when the church at
Koroit (Vic.) was built, some workman left a large
screw-driver lying on a rafter. It lay there un-
disturbed till a recent Sunday evening, when an
owl got into the building during service and in its
terrified flutterings knocked over the screw-driver,
which fell point-downwards on the head of Mr.
Alfred Skilbeck, and nearly killed him. Con-
sidering that the screw-driver had 23 years to fall
in, and that there were six days in the week when
it might have fallen without hurting any one, and
SPEIGHT v. SYME-A CONUNDRUM.
If Syme writes libels, and Speight takes ire,
And Speight proves truthful, but Syme a liar,
Though Syme still wins—and Speight has lost,
Yet Speight has the honors : who pays the cost?
The following answers are barred :—The Govern-

SPRING TIME
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
M IS r*r e
(a.e fond affair-
Spring is here, and it is sweet
Low to lie at lady’s feet,
Lingering, pouting lips to taste,
Close to press the yielding waist.
Men, beware,
lt is rare
- That she be as fond as fair.
In the half-light of the eye,
. When the shades your sight deceive,
As the western radiance dies
Eyes are looking into eyes.
Then take care
' How you fare
Lest she catch you unaware.
’Neath the wilga’s drooping shade,
Where the lover’s rest is made,
In the dim, seductive park,
Forms,are flitting after dark ;
; Then A is,
/ For a kiss,
Men would barter future bliss.
Girls, when burning whispers sound,
Bend your glances to the ground,
Answer curtly—better naught,
Half are lies, and half are sport;
Men are snakes,
h Marriage takes
All the bliss from bridal cakes.
Ah ! ’tis useless. Girls are girls,
Youth and love’s hot madness whirls
Fever-heat within the heart;
Love, and mind not after-smart;
. Kiss and play
While you may.
Death will catch you all some day.
Cobar, 18th August, 1894.

Some of the mail-boats are now as badly
rushed as are the ordinary coasters between the
East and Albany. So rushed, indeed, that the
overplus of third-class passengers are crammed
into the second—which, before this innovation, was
rather more popular than the saloon. The conse-
quence is that the food is now disgraceful, the
attendance the same, and, as for the passengers,
the only places now not crowded are the bath-
rooms.
# # *
A “Reform” Club is being started in
Melbourne to rope-in rampageous young Tory
patriots, at a guinea-a-head. The Reform Club
of Sydney, alas, is now dead. Its chief reason for
existence was to enjoy the opportunity of hearing
old Jack Robertson swear. When dear old Jack
died and stopped swearing—or, anyhow, when be
stopped swearing and died—the club died, too.
Those of its members whom grog had not quite
killed have been slowly dying ever since. It was
the “ booziest ” club in Australian annals.
# * «
Pathos from the island-hell of New
Caledonia. A stowaway was found concealed in
a cask aboard the barque G. M. Tucker, and he
was promptly landed—poor devil! —at Noumea.
lie had swum off to the ship in the company of
another convict who sank “ with his irons on ”
fortunate lihere! The sea was kinder than the
sea-skipper. The maritime novelist is still search-
ing for a typical skipper who, obedient to an un-
breakable principle, will assist any convict stow-
away to escape from any law-factored inferno.
* * *
Lord Sherborne, elderly and rich, lately
married Miss De Stern, also elderly and rich, in
London. After going through the marriage cere-
mony, the elderly couple separated and set up
separate establishments, the gentleman returning
to his bachelor quarters in St. James’. If more
couples arranged their affairs in like manner there
would be less work for Judge Windeyer.

A Sydney Woman’s Christian Temperance
unionist has made the horrific suggestion that the
union should capture the hotels by rental thereof,
and use the bars for the sale of unintoxicating
liquors. The teetotallers must first invent a new
drink with a grip in it.
Give us bluestone rum, petroleum,
Every sort of poisoned oleum,
Kerosene, sulphuric acid,
Rather than their ohillgut placid;
Give us fusel, oil of methyl,
Rather than the drinks of Bethel.
* • *
The first case of hydrophobia ever recorded
in Australia is reported from Adelaide. The
patient, who kept a hop-beer shop, having become
violent, was arrested by the police, from one of
whom he bit half a finger. Hydrophobia is about
the only old-world horror which Australia has
hitherto escaped, and now that it seems to have
really arrived, after a number of false alarms, our
civilisation may be said to be just about complete.
Let us hope that, after all, the case is only one of
acute mania.

The ordinary style of P.U. shearing notice
is just like this : “ Bobberyboo, Billygolong and
Snapitup have finished shearing under verbal
agreement. Wantaguy, Jaokarooby and Kidney-
fat are shearing short-handed under P.U. agree-
ment.” Short-sighted loyalty to a stupid senti-
ment ! Which loyalty, by the way, has reft
asunder the Pastoralists’ Association, as will be
seen after shearing.
« * •
Evangelist M‘Neill has firmly resolved
that during his Sydney mission he will not permit
anyone in the audience to ask for prayers on be-
half of relatives. Some of the Smellbourne
requests were bad enough, but it has been left to
an individual up North who had been through the
divorce-court to plead for prayers for “ my first
wife.”

The latest N.S.W. embezzlement presents
some unique features. The young man— he got
three years—had never drunk a glass of strong
drink, or backed a horse, or stepped on a race-
course in his life. All the loot was spent in
making home comfortable for motherland pre-
senting her with such articles as a iB6O piano. He
gave parties at home and led his friends to believe
that he was in receipt of £3OO per annum at least;
whereas, though “ financial manager” of a ware-
house, he only got 30s. a week.

N.S.W. metropolitan postal officials com-
plain of having suffered three years of a trying
religious atmosphere. The Post and Telegraph
Christian Association was nursed by Kidd, and
when that son of light departed preacher Joe
Cook took the baby in his arms. “Why,” ask
the officials, “ have we been afflicted with the
pious members of two administrations? O for
the days of Dan O’Connor !”
* * *
The Coombe-Martin (Q.) shooting-case was
probably remarkable for the tallest lying and
deepest swearing on record. For quaint, original
oaths and fathomless, unspeakable profanity the
evidence tendered beat the record. Even the
Judge commented thereon, and the defending
counsel feebly apologised for the weird and lurid
epithets of his witnesses by assuring his Honor
that the objectionable vocables were only “ terms
of endearment.”
• • *
Victorian general elections have been
robbed of one of their most interesting features,
viz. —tomatoes. This vegetable ripens (and decays)
about Feb. or March, and was most useful for
election-purposes in days gone by. Now that the
election date, has been changed to Sept, voters
have, in their 1 Armouries, nothing but the time-
honored egg* with an occasional packet of flour
thrown in, The tomato’s place is, indeed, hard
to fllh A well-seasoned tomato always fell with a
gratifying splash which is entirely absent from
the general run of missiles.
• • m
The effects of the explosion of an air-
eylinder at the sewage works, Port Melbourne,
one day last week, were peculiar. The men work-
ing in the shaft where the explosion took place
were carried nearly to the surface by the rush of
air, and, as the power subsided, were gently
lowered again, each revolving on his own axis like
a catherine-wheel, and striking the bottom without
sustaining a cent’s worth of damages. One of the
miners told a Bulletin man that the sensation
was rather pleasant, and inferred that as a mar-
ried man he hoped never to be blown up with
worse effect.
