THE LAW OF SELF-PRESERVATION.
The Law is very old, and not
The kind of person girls can wheedle ;
A spouse at home the Law has got—
A bad look-out for Martha Needle.
Fair Martha’s husband on the “ shelf ”
Reminds the Law to mind himself;
No pity for the pouting sinner
Hath he who trusts his daily life
Into the keeping of a wife,
And drinks her coffee after dinner.
The Law’s a Man, just bear in mind,
His mother’s son and not her daughter,
And hence the Law is disinclined
To sympathise with husband-slaughter—
No crime could be more black and base ;
But Justice, in the Needle case,
A weight of hidden meaning carries.
The Law, by breaking Martha’s neck,
Is putting—well, a little check
Upon the lady whom he marries.
THE FOURTH ESTATE.
Some of the results of delirium were the results of in-
telligence passing into the fourth stage of desire, a region
where the denizens were of an exceedingly unpleasing
character. That was why people suffering from delirium
tremens always saw the same objects. They were real
things existing on the Fourth Plane.— Mrs. Besant.
That cypress, Florentine,
Now cheerfully restore ;
For ne’er did hell of thine
House denizens unpleasant
As these of Mistress Besant,
In Bolgia Number Four.
True snakes and crawly things,
Not phantoms dodg’d of yore,
Cavort around in rings
That storey ’neath the basement,
For quill-compelling race meant
Who sport the sign of Four.
“ Abandon hope of nips ”
Burns red on brazen door.
With crack’d and parching lips
That shriek delirium tremens,
Each head as big as three men’s,
The Press cram Number Four.

appear on MS. No liability is undertaken re
contributions voluntarily submitted, and in no
case will MS. be returned unless stamps ac-
company.

of soda. There is as much difference between chloride < f
lime and carbonate of soda as between chalk and cheese
A. Horton: The Australian term “bail-up” is derived
from bailing-up as applied to cows. When a man is
“ bailed-up ” bv bushrangers he is to be milked of 1 i <
money. The American “hold-up,” similarly used, has
quite another origin. It means “ hold up your hands,"
so that you can’t draw a hip-pocket revolver.
The Present Season.
Many persons may not be aware that the season
now upon us, with all its wealth of youthful ver-
dure and floral embellishment, commonly known
as “ beautiful Spring,” has its perils as well as its
delights. Experience reveals the disquieting fact
that the human system is subject to vicissi-
tudes brought about by climatic influences
peculiar to Spring-tide. While nature is assum-
ing her most attractive features, and delighting
the senses, there are opposing influences at work
which frequently, in a secret and insidious way,
go far to counteract the joyous sensations n still-
ing from the renewed beauties of the vernal re-
vival. Too often the impaired action of some
vital organ, probably the kidneys, produces a
vitiated condition of the blood, attended by boils
and abscesses, and many painful symptoms, which
render it impossible to enjoy life. The liver, al o,
if not actually congested, becomes sluggish and
inactive, causing a number of other disagreeable
and dangerous complications owing to the in-
corporation of bile with the blood.
Now is the time when active measures should be
taken to remove these troublesome indications.
The most effectual agent hitherto discovered for
this purpose is undoubtedly Warner’s Safe Cure,
which, aided.by a judicious use of Warner’s Safe
Pills, has established itself as the most successfu ,
and, in fact, the only reliable antidote to all ir-
regularities connected with the kidneys and liver,
affections of the urinary organs, and an impure
condition of the blood.
The following testimonial from a well-known
lady-philanthropist will well illustrate the power
and value of Warner’s Safe Cure; “Eirene,”
Ohatsworth-road, Prahran, near Melbourne, Vic-
toria, 20th April, 1894. —I can speak of Warner’s
Safe Cure in the very highest terms, for I believe
I should not now be living save for its use. I
speak of it and recommend it to all my friends,
and I am very sure a patient and continuous t s •
would in many cases have saved life. I was dy n t
from Bright’s Disease, brought on by the shock of
operations for tumor, and was so ill when I began
it a nurse had to lift and turn me in bed. I had
been bed-ridden for many months from acute
pains in my limbs; one knee was so contracted
from agony of pain I could not stand at all. I
was suffering from gravel and many complications,
and fully thought I was dying when I dismissed
all doctors and began the Safe Cure in April,
1887. I am perfectly well now, and have not
needed to use any for several years.—(Signed)
Georgiana S. Morris.

My Dear Moorabinda—
“Exhibition Hall” (the old noisy York-street
skating-rink, where the Art show was staged this
year) had about as many people as you could
scatter with one flourish of a pepper-caster the
other eve, when Mr. Nicholas Shiels rose among
the lovely frames and delivered a technical lecture
upon “ Etching.” To a rank outsider, Mr. Shiels*
explanations wanted explaining. The only thing
I seized in a sea of darksome allusions to “copper
biting ” and “dry point” was when Mr. S. con-
fessed that the charm of etching was its uncer-
tainty, which is, no doubt, why the French make
a feminine noun of it. Comic relief (from a
gallery point of view) was given to the proceedings
by the appearance of an individual with three
pink roses in his coat, who had once been a presi-
dent of the Society, and who rose to discuss the
lecture in a wild and rambling speech, whereat
several men and women laughed, but to me the
spectacle seemed hardly a mirthful circumstance.
By the way, the artists present revenged them-
selves upon the “ evening-dress ” aspect of things
at the “ smoke-oh ” by sporting coats of many
colors, and a general air as if the Art Society had
run to seed was noticeable all round. The fuzzy-
wuzzy coiffure of a he-artist on my near side, for
example, looked as if he had held a butting-match
with a rabbit-proof fence before coming out. Mr.
Bloxsome presided in a Carnot necktie and a dis-
tinctly judicial manner. If I ever had to explain
myself to the Art Society’s brand-new president,
I am sure I should say “ Your Worship.”
A fete in aid of some hungry church or other
came off at Toxteth Park the other day, under
active vice-regal patronage, Lady Duff doing the
show from door to door, which was all the vice-
regal party did do, for not even a penny pin-
cushion found its way to Government House.
Her Excellency viewed the cobbled cosies and the
puckered slippers with every mark of approbation,
but came out of the fiery ordeal quite purse-whole.
To go to a “sale of gifts ” without your purse is
equivalent to going to a dinner-party without
your teeth.
When charity dances cease, philanthropiojfolk
raise the wind with bazaars. The fair in aid of
Auburn hospital, which runs for a week from to-
day (Saturday), is to be perfectly stage-managed.
Sydney Town Hall is the place of action; the
Acting-Governoress is president over the 16 stalls,
the banditti are to be in nurses’ uniform, and I
am told that the disputed Geneva cross which
gives Dr. Vandeleur Kelly so much trouble won’t
be allowed to appear even on the bath-buns.
It is to be hoped that when the Hospitals ball
comes off in ’95, a little more consideration will
be given to the naming of the rendezvous. It is
occasionally possible to be a little too apropos. A
maiden was making dance-engagements at the
ball hereinbefore alluded to, and when he had
signed the contract there came the usual, “And
where shall I find you?” “ Oh, this is my haunt,”
said the maiden, glancing upwards at the sign,
"The Benevolent Asylum !”
Sydney Philharmonic Society finished up their
festival on Saturday evening with “The Messiah.”
Madame Sapio, Belle Cole, Mr. W. Walshe and
Magrath, were the soloists; the chorus and
orchestra of the society went well and strong
throughout, and Wiegand made thrilling, if spas-
modic, appearances at the organ. Signor Hazon
conducted. People who come under the beat of
bis baton say the magnetic maestro has a most
unexpected way of beating time, and that strange
vocalists obey him with difficulty. Talking of
the instrumental part of the show, I would like to
tell that young lady in a white gown and a celes-
tial nose, who played the fiddle immediately
behind leader Allpress, that no less an authority
than the gifted Madame Urso was quite taken
with her bowing.
Madame Sapio (in pale-blue brocade with pink
rosebuds) certainly took the vocal palm. Madame
Belle Cole, in black with a point-lace tucker, was
by no means so successful. The contralto’s ren-
dering of “ He was Despised ” seemed to me as
grotesquely mournful as a dingo howling in the
wilderness. Mr. Walshe took the tenor part on
very short notice. I like Mr. Walshe in oratorio.
His vocal manner is so stately that you never
imagine for one moment that he is trying to wink
at a pretty little angel.
Charles Magrath was in resonant voice for the
growling numbers. He is very over-aweing in
oratorio, and always looks the part. As Elijah
he was almost a character-study, barring the omis-
sion of the prophet’s historical bald-head, and his
awful scowl in the “ Messiah ” suggested that he
was endeavoring to impersonate the incensed
deity. When the chorus shouted that unto them
a “son had been born,” he no doubt took quite a
personal interest in the matter, for precisely the
same thing has just happened (in England) to Mr.
Magrath. Some of the sacred remarks of the
chorus aforesaid were weirdly split up to fit the
music, it seemed to me. For example, sopranos,
contraltos, tenors and basses separately reiterated
“ All we like sheep ” until it really almost sounded
like an ad. for Australian mutton.
A pleasant little ceremony occurred in the in-
terval, when Mayor Manning, in a flowery speech,
presented the badge of the P.S. to Camilla Urso,
Sapio, and Belle Cole. I thought the presentation
far more effective than the eleventh-hour handing
over to Lady Halß, at the Redfern Railway
Station, of a silver lyre-bird with a long, unman-
ageable tail. This work of art, you remember,
was got “on tick ” from a local handicraftsman,
who was only prevented with the greatest diffi-
culty from presenting his little bill to Norman-
herself.
The Town Hall fairly bulged with people,
topped off by Gov. Duff in white kid gloves, and
with Lady Duff in rainbow silk, a daughter or
two and Mr. Pennyman in his suite. General and
Mrs. Hutton, Mr. James Fairfax and Mayor and
Mayoress Manning scintillated in the front
row of patronage; and Madame Urso, in a gown
smothered in black jet, and squired by Benno
Scherek, who had a miniature skull for bouton-
niere, also had her feet on the sacred vice-regal
carpet.
Apropos of patronage parties. The unrepre-
sentative wife of a representative man must suffer'
violent agonies when hubby trots out after it’s all
oyer with Lady Snooks hanging affectionately on
his arm, and leaving his lawful wife to show her-
self to her carriage. When sex-equality is firmly
established there will have to be an escort pro-
vided for Lady Oracle when Sir Oracle’s strong
left arm is booked to another lady for the evening.
I didn’t detect much novelty amongst the
choristeresses on the platform. One fair ’cellist
in a white gown and a scarlet four-cornered
waist-belt could have gone to any bal masqud
as the ace of diamonds; and one of the soprani
rather reversed the usual order of things by
glaring at the audience through flashing opera-
glasses. That wouldn’t be a bad idea for the
chorus of the next Gaiety Co.
Do you know that there is a language attached
to the way a well-known, silvery-haired musician
of Melbourne wears his eye glass string ? When
it hangs straight down his blushing cheek he is
“quiet to handle,” as the horse-dealers say, and
could be trusted to chaperone Venus her-very-self.
When a subdued feeling of friskiness comes o’er
him, the cord is stuck out of harm’s way behind
the waxed portion of his mustivey ; and when he
is particularly dangerous he tucks the string out
of the road behind his right ear.
Maidens whose pet Johnnies look at life through
window-panes will please accept this intimation.
For, though these profoundly interesting signs are
only visible on one individual, they may be the
key-note to the moral slant of all eye-glassed
Johnnydom, for aught I know. Somebody should
write a piece on the language of eye-glass strings.
It js certain that one of these serviceable threads
in juxtaposition with Vincent’s moustache would
quite spoil the osculations for Rosette.
While talking of music I had fully meant to say
that there is a rumour of a return to mournful
Gregorian chantings (like the groans of the
wounded in the battle of Prague) by the R.C.
authorities in Australia, acting under Papal
orders. But fancy the Romish services without
the standard masses which give good-natured
operatic artistes (Sapio sang like an angel in St.
Patrick’s, Sydney, last Sunday) the chance to
bring down the house on the seventh day !
They tell me that a league of ungallant Sydney
youths has formulated a dark plot to banish the
women from the dummies of the King-st. cable-
tram, by means of the very rankest brands of
tobacco. It is most amusing to see how pertina-
ciously woman does stick to the dummy. She
evidently intends to ride in front, there and else-
where, every time.
The same moral difference that distinguishes, or
used to distinguish, the Melbourne from the Syd-
ney hansom cab also extends to the cable-trams of
the two cities. In Melbourne, as the tram
dummies were monopolised (like the hansoms)
from the start by golden-haired ladies with
elaborately-clocked stockings, they are considered,
even to this day, a more or less frisky mode of
transit for the daughters of Eve. But in Sydney,
the Darling Pointer steps off the dummy in the
very core of King-street with an absolutely uncon-
scious air, or goes flying down hill (four of her
make a fashionable figurehead on the little cable-
gripper) with all the fluttering appearance of
Mary Ann on a merry-go-round.
“Ma Mie Rosette” continues to impress its
virtuous lesson at Sydney Lyceum. May
Pollard’s legs, clad in tights the plaintive hue of
the lean part of boiled pork, are picturesque
enough to support the opera single-handed, so to
speak. Marietta Nash, who accentuates her like-
ness to a Dutch doll by painting her cheeks bright
red, is another pleasing item; and Lauri’s legs
twinkle all over the show. I think nature in-
tended Mr. Lauri for a coryphee—he never seems
at his best unless he is gyrating in a cutty-sark.
Howard Vernon reminded me of a mixture of
Amelia’s unfortunate young man and “Hop’s”
idea of a social-purity official. By the way, people
notice that Tapley is allowed more scope when he
frantically hugs his dear Rosette than ever Nellie
S. vouchsafed to the fascinating Ryley. Is it done
to make little Mrs. T. sit gnashing her bridal
teeth in the darkness of the auditorium ?
Miss Chirnside (Mrs. Everard Browne) has
given her friends several surprises even in the
short interval since her disclosure that she did not
go to Molesworth Greene’s place for her honey-
moon, as arranged. The last is the postpone-
ment of her trip to England, on which she and
her husband were to start on Saturday.
A sign of the times is that a lady, once rich and
extremely well-known in society, last week opened
a pretty little tea-shop in Hunter-street. She
hopes to obtain the patronage of her friends,
by whom she has been accorded an encouraging
send-off. Which makes two “ society ” tea-shops
in Hunter-street.
Three “dissenting” clergymen visited Man-
darin Tart’s restaurant recently and ate to the
tune of 18d. each. On being presented with the
usual cash-desk docket each refused to accept on
the ground that they understood that “ Mr. Tart”
always invited clergymen to “partake.”
Hilda Spong, I hear, is going in bald-headed for
her “art,” and no shirk. She applied for an
order to do the lunatic-asylums the other day to
study the gibberings therein, and one of her recent
outings was a trip to the Sydney Zoo to hear a
hyena laugh ! Her next part ought to be per-
fectly lively if she works all these tips in !
What a number of shelfy-looking women on the
Block are now wearing grey with pink fal-de-rals!
A worse chromatic choice, under the circum-
stances, would be impossible. I thought every
woman knew that as white is the uniform of the
debutante, so pink-and-grey is the trade-mark of
an old maid ? Any unappreciated maiden of
debateable juvenility who goes abroad in the
festive colors of a rosy cockatoo might just as well
add beaded mittens and a poodle on a ribbon to
her walking-costume. When her dressmaker sug-
gests pink and grey—a combination supposed to
lighten faded complexions and give an air of early
spring to the last rose of summer, so to speak—
the wisest thing a woman can do is to join the
she-Suffrage League at once and make a sudden
discovery of the worthlessness of Man.
Yours faithfully,
SAPPHO SMITH.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Southern racing-boom commences on Satur-
day at Caulfield. Local interest in the game which, one
time, provided Melbourne with six solid weeks of madness
would have been fainter this year than last but for a
threatened scooping of the pool by Sydney invaders. So
many “ morals ” are said to be coming, if they haven’t
already arrived, from beyond the Murray, that Victoria
shows a disposition to barrack for its own side, and is
more than usually eager to skin the stranger within its
gates. Guineas Day, at Caulfield, however, is often
very bad for everybody except the “books." For one
thing, a deluge is apt to occur about the middle
of October, and then the proverbial rank-outsider
comes sludging first past the post amidst the shouts of
damp Israelites. The Caulfield course, too, seems to
favour surprises, by giving moderate old animals an undue
pull over the crack heavy-weights. And there are pre-
meditated surprises sprung upon the public by cunning
asses who can’t find it in their hearts to win a race on the
first day of a meeting, but must needs try to ‘‘take the
measure ” of something that is merely having an idiotic
“ liker ” at something else.
The Caul field Stakes may supply a rough out-
line idea as to which horses are likely to distinguish them-
selves presently. Patron, Paris, Brockleigh, Carnage,
Fortunatus, Capstan, Isaac, Ruenalf, Nobleman, Tarcoola,
Malvolio, Loyalty, and others, are entered for this nine-
furlong race, and if many of them toe the mark the
gallop will be worth looking at, anyway. Should Dream-
land show up for the Guineas, a red-hot favorite for the
V.R.O. Derby will probably eventuate.
The two Ballarat stables represented by Dream-
land in the Derby and Capstan in the Caulfield Cup, are
said to have some more good ones behind them. In other
quarters, Steadfast, Fortunatus, Bruin, Tullamore and
Straightflre are talked about by people who ought to
know. Whether the local animals will hold their own
against the Sydney-Newcastle contingent remains to be
seen.
• • • •
Dreamland is re-introduced into the Derby
betting, and there is the usual talk of a large stable
commission. Is it likely that Scobie would adyise his
owners to back him while, barring one Yorkshire gallop,
he is utterly in the dark as to the colt’s capabilities 7 A
line has yet to be obtained through Shotbolt’s and others’
running at Caulfield. This flash in the pan may be
due to the tactics of the “artless bookie.’* Remember
Autonomy two seasons ago ! Was he not reported as
backed for tons by the stable, and yet Camoola repre-
sented them—and won ?
* * * •
Capstan again is reported to have cleaned out
all Alec. Taylor’s lot, and is consequently backed pretty
freely for Caulfield Cup. If, during the Melb. month, a
good winner is to be sprung from Ballarat district it may,
after all, be Shofbolt.
♦ ♦ • e
Toorak Handicap looks, on paper, a certainty
for Eumarrah. If upset, ’twill be by Parramatta—being
fit at present.
• * * •
Moorite keeps well, and Caulfield Guineas to-day
(Sat.) looks a gift for him, though he can be made much
better by Derby day-d la Strathmore, he may win the
double.
• • • *
The field for the Caulfield, alias the Graveyard,
Stakes will doubtless contain the usual percentage of
candidates labelled N.O.J. Ruenalf (Flaneur spelt back-
wards, by the way)should win, but in his absence Forward
or Euroka will about do the trick.

Sternchaser may run well, but he will be handier
in the Melb. Cup, where, on his best behavior, he should

of this horse yet, and it must be remembered that his
owner is in luck—which is a great factor in racing.

Launceston ought to have a chance for Caulfield
Cup. Last season, with 7st. 121 h., he ran second to Lady
Trenton (6st. 121 b.) in A.J.C. Handicap, 1J mile, in 2.37 J
—a good go. Now he has the same weight over the same
distance, and she has to give him 101 b., so that he meets
her on 241 b. better terms than he did last year. Then,
again, he ran third to Donation (to whom he conceded
61b.) in the V.R.O. Handicap in the fastest time on record.

Gov. Glasgow (M.L.), whose ancestor raced and

and the Bench may fine them the £2O and send them to
gaol as well.

Whakawatea was about the fastest two-year-old
Maoriland ever produced, Carbine and Maxim thrown in,
but he was shipped and dumped about to race-meetings
all over the islands and spoilt. It was only the late Wally
Clifford’s riding on Stepniak that beat him in the Welcome
Stakes at Christchurch. However, a season or so after-
wards he beat Stead’s horse in a mile and a-quarter race
at Wellington. People this side think him only a sprinter.

• * * •
With the revival in Australian pedestrianism
comes a discussion as to who is really champion. Samuels,
the Q. aboriginal, has sadly deteriorated, and, beyond a
doubt, M'Qarrigal is not nearly so fast as when, almost
six years ago, he won the Botany Gold Cup and a big
Carrington Handicap in the same week. Taking late
public form as a guide, there is no doubt that the cham-
pion sprinter is Larry Marsh, the aboriginal from the
Clartnoe River, N.S.W. (whose portrait, from a photo, by
Edsn, of Sydney, is printed in this week's Bullbtin). He
la 2S years old, 6ft. Sin. high, and in running costume
weighs about 9 stone, but possesses the thews and sinew*
of a 12st. man, devoid of lumber. In shape, he is tha
beau ideal of a sprinter, and his style is easy and graceful
In intelligence, he is far above most of the aboriginal
runners seen in Sydney, and, living more steadily, should
soon eclipse his former performances. His brother Jack
Marsh, lately showed in Victoria that he is little below
Larry in pace, and, being two years younger and some,
what bigger, may yet equal, if not surpass, his elder
brother as a sprinter.
• • • •
Though Larry Marsh, the aboriginal, is now
generally recognised as Australia’s champion runner yet
many think that at any distance between 120 and 44#
yards he is not equal to “Wally” Williams, the well-
known Botany and Carrington performer. Nearly eight
years ago, the writer asked Hutchens, the English cham-
pion, his opinion of the Australian runners. The “ Put.
ney Pet” replied that, leaving aside the aboriginal, for
whom no laws held, the native-born were more adapted for
medium-distance running than sprinting. A sprinter not
only had to get fit, but keep on year after year so as to
develope muscle and strength. Robust men, not liable to
get overtrained, were the best, and Hutchens pointed
out how the best performers at Sheffield were compara-
lively old men. He also instanced the American Myers
who, while unapproached at medium distances, was never
equal to the first-class amateurs at sprinting. Prom his
length of stride and spare physique, the future Aus-
tralian should beat the world at races from a quarter to a
mile. Hutchens, however, said one young runner was of
the robust stamp required for Sheffield sprinting. This
was “Wally” Williams, then comparatively a novice.
Time has verified the English champion’s opinion in one
particular. The strong and shapely Williams soon got to
the top of the tree, and was among the best half-dozen
ever seen at Carrington or Botany. And he alone seems
to have retained his old form. Walter H. Williams was
born at Dubbo, N.S.W., 27 years ago. He stands 5 feet
9| inches, and, in running costume and properly fit, weighs
list. 41b. He has got into twenty finals.

Evangelist M‘Neil’s strong plank in Adelaide
was the accursed totalisator which he denounced in the
name of the Lord as an abomination. Now, brothers
M‘Neil and Burke made £122 3s. 9d. net profit on their
one week’s Adelaide show, and if they had done as tha
totalisator authorities do they would have publicly turned
over the “ fraction," £22 3s. 9d., to the charities. How-
ever, being very good men indeed, perhaps they turned it
over privately.

The Council of Churches, sitting at Melbourne
the other day, announced that it recognised that the spirit
of gambling was a national evil, and that immediate steps
should be taken for the suppression of the evil. There-
fore, the Council deliberately determined that sermons
shodld be preached against “ this great danger to the
public and religious life of the people.” This is rough on
the bookies—just before the Cup, tool Seriously, the
belief of the average parson in the efficacy of sermons is a
stupendous thing.

law, but also money won or said to have been won by any
person at athletic sports or other games or exercises!

• * * *
A young Tasmanian, who fancies himself with
his hands, was in Sydney during the great J. L. Sullivan’s
visit and looked him up at his hotel, introducing himself
as an admirer and humble imitator. After, preliminaries,
the young worshipper invited the illustrious one and Ash-
ton to take a drink. Each accepted, and with a wink to
the presiding Hebe, said he would have “the same as
usual.” Which proved to be a large bottle of fizz apiece.
The adoring one took shandygaff himself and paid without
a wink, but reflected on the penalties attached to knowing
the salt of the earth.

• • * »
Why the totalisator should supersede Nebuchad-
nezzar Solomons, the bookmaker, in every Australian
province;—
1. Because the tote lays the highest odds.
2. Because it never climbs over the nearest fence when
the time comes to pay out.
3. Because it never needs to lay short odds so as to buy
a diamond pin and a 36-carat watch and chain.
4. Because the tote is indifferent as to the ultimate
winner, and never needs to dose up the favorite in the
interests of the “bag.”
5. Because the machine doesn’t own and run racehorses
6. Because the percentage profits go to the clubs and,
through increased stakes, hack to the public.
7. Because wherever it has been tried the machine has
purified racing and protected the legitimate owner.
8. Because it has led to the extinction of the turf
middleman, the “bookie,” and diverted hundreds of
pounds from his pocket to the charities by handing its
fractions to benevolent institutions.
Adelaide. E. M. Watts.
* * ♦ •
Molly Darling, who got home in a race at
Timaru (M.L.), the other day, went out unbacked on the
tote, and investors got their money back, less 10 per cent.
The owner had wired money to the tote-shops in other
parts, and as the machine paid nothing some of them
refused to pay. However, as they were not supposed to
do “ tote ” business, and as the money was sent them to
“ put on,” and “ nix ” was put on, but “put in” their
pockets, they have to pay the £l5 limit and say nothing.
But how wild the owner was on finding he had been too
clever, and that if he had put £1 on his mare on the
course he would have scooped the lot!

Coolgardie is not the sole attraction for
Easterners. Some unsavoury layers of odds have just
gone over to Westralia to lay the “ doubles ” on the
Melbourne Cup. They have not a fiver between them,
but that won’t matter, as their natural brass will work the
oracle. After fixing up a tidy book (no tick) they must,
of course, “ run over to see the Cup, you know.” But
the lambs will see them no more.

An Oamaru (M.L.) man has patented a bike
that has to be propelled with both hands and feet, and
can go at the rate of 40 miles an hour. Two have been
constructed at Christchurch, but in the trial one steered
an erratic course-put down to the jerking niexpert
However, its speed is undoubted. Further particulars of
this 40-imle-iti-60-min. machine will be awaited with
interest. ,
Mr. Albert Sharp, the discoverer of the
new Cannalin remedies for piles, announces that,
in future, there will be a single charge of one
pound for the six weeks course of treatment.
This will avoid delay in serious cases from remote
parts of the colonies, as a pound-note can be sent
where postal-order is not available. Applications
must be accompanied by a full description of the
case, and be addressed plainly to 257 George-st.,
Sydney, N.S.W.
Few readers of “A Queenslander s Travel-
Notes,” by A. G. Stephens, will endorse the
author’s application to himself of Matthew
Arnold’s scoff at the traveller-in-vain, for, in this
account of a trip to Europe vid the States and
Canada, Mr. Stephens has supplemented a sharp
eye and a sinewy judgment with a live pen. it
he has “ trotted ” his globe, there are no signs of
the incidental “joggling” about this easy chronicle
of impressions reproduced with a humor that
“goes of itself.” Mr, Stephens can say chunky
things in a feathery way, and expresses himself,
when he chooses, with an epigrammatic felicity
which may fairly condone his unholy affection tor
the pun that walketh in darkness. The philosophy
of the missionary-question, for instance, could
hardly be more neatly nutshelled than in the
observation—touching the Chinaman that At
centuries of heredity can’t be eradicated m the
twinkle of a missionary’s eye!” The Scottish
idiosyncrasy, too, is shot flying with the assertion
that Sandy alone succeeds in serving God ana
Mammon ! The book, in short, abounds with
shrewd criticism —has a fair share of grotesquenes
and “good-’uns”—and may be kodaked as dis-
tinctly re-readable.

As an indication of the belief in the
revival of prosperity in N.S. Wales, it may be
noted that the well-known ophthalmic opticians,
Messrs. Wood, who have been established in
Liverpool for over 70 years, and in Melbourne for
20, and whose reputation for the scientific testing
of eyesight is world-wide, have opened an estab-
lishment at 299 George-street, Sydney, with the
intention of making that city the centre of their
Australian operations. Mr. W. Wood, who was
originally connected with their Lord-st._ house,
but who has more recently been located in Mel-
bourne, is charged with the superintendence of
the new establishment.

Editor Bulletin,— “ Not Much ” substi-
tutes fiction for fact. My statement was that
when a man or a community pays interest nothing
is received in exchange, which is a simple fact.
“Not Much” thinks we need not trouble about
our national debts. By and bye we shall be in
the position of the man who sold everything at &
loss and was only able to make a living by the
multiplicity of his transactions. This is mere
fiction I prefer to stick to the facts as indicated
on page 18 of The Bulletin ,of Sept. 22, which
are that the national debts of the world have
increased nearly five-fold in the last half century,
and we know that the private indebtedness has
also enormously increased. “Not Much evi-
dently thinks that by constant borrowing we can
all become rich enough to do without work if only
somebody else will provide the income for us. ihe
cow and calf story touches quite another ques-
tion. As to whether the average individual or
the average nation is such a fool as to give some-
thing for nothing, I am sorry to say each is.-
J.
MEDWAY DAY.
Wool and Produce Reports.
M'Burnby, Wallis & Co., report'The weekly wool
auctions opened on Monday to a Urge attendance of
buyers representing all the manufacturing centres of the
world. The market is in a state of uncertainty, and
bidding was of a very half-hearted character. Judging
by sales made, prices are considerably lower than last
season, but we have hopes they may harden somewhat as
the sales progress. We would again impress upon owners
the great importance of submitting to the auctioneer s
advice. He is on the spot and has the best knowledge of
current values and when to meet the market. Excessive
reserves spoil the sale of a clip.

Under plea of economy, the dual billets of
Chief Commissioner of Police (Tas.) and P.M.
[Hobart) are wrapped together. Mr. Shaw, with
prosecuting evidence at his fingers’ ends, will, as
magistrate, adjudicate upon it! Justice !
The late Ross Cox, Melb. journalist, was
the son of a Dublin clergyman, and reached Aus-
tralia as scripture-reader on a convict-ship, a post
ne got by influence for, like most parsons’ sons, he
Knew about as much of theology as he did of gold-
ligging. Luckily, there were but two of his own
alleged faith on board and neither of them
‘bigoted,” so he got through until the boat
reached Hobart. There, he was sent for by the
Bishop, who had been informed that young
Cox wished for ordination. “ What is your
detailed arrangement of the 39 articles ?” asked
chat dignitary. “Dunno,” replied Ross; and he
repeated the answer to a dozen similar questions.
“ My young friend,’’said the Bishop at last, “I
lon’t know how you obtained your appointment,
out you certainly cannot be ordained here.” So
Ross made for the Vic. goldfields, and ultimately
drifted into journalism. He was the author of
several circumstantial hoaxes, one of which was a
description of a telegraphic tasting-machine, with
a.ll the prominent politicians’and professors’names
used with a liberty that made the public swallow
the article as solemn fact. At another time he
described how the Shah bought two infant slaves,
male and female, and, like the historic Irishman
in the gondola yarn, let “nature take its course,”
watching developments meanwhile. The cool
audacity with which names were used, and the
apparent technical and scientific knowledge of the
writer, made Cox’s hoaxes go down with nine-
tenths of nevvspaper readers, most of whom pro-
bably believe in them to the present day.
I am Tired!
Sufferers from indigestion and liver complaint are
subject to a continued feeling of drowsiness,
headache, tired aching- limbs, and a general
inclination to languor. These symptoms are due
primarily to a deficient secretion of bile, the
natural cathartic of the liver, which, when secreted
in insufficient quantities, permits the coarse and
fibrous particles of the food and other waste and
poisonous matter from all parts of the system to
become impacted in the duodenum and lower
intestines, creating a very dangerous condition
which requires instant and proper treatment.
An experience of five years with Clements Tonic
has proved, this remedy a specific for such com-
plaints, and its universal success has resulted in
thousands of imitations being forced on the
market; not because they possess any therapeutic
value, for they are purely speculative ventures.
Sufferers who desire to get relief must insist on
having Clements Tonic. It acts scientifically, has
proved its efficacy in thousands of cases as in
that of Mr. W. S. H. Dunlop, Berry, N.S.W.,
who writes on April 7th, 1893 About five or six
weeks ago I got a stroke of the sun which ran
through me from the crown of my head to my
foot. I was completely shook and broken up ;
one bottle of your Clements Tonic completely
cured me, and I am as strong as ever, in fact,
better than I was before. I have also used
Clements Tonic for influenza. About eighteen
months ago I had three attacks of that disease,
and after all other remedies failed, Clements Tonic
cured me, and I consider it has done all you profess
it to do. If you think the above will benefit others,
you are at liberty to use it.—Yours faithfully,
W. S. H. Dunlop, Berry, N.S.W.
Dear Bulletin, — He a par in “ Wild Oat”
column of 25tli Aug. A friend writing from out
back sends me this to show you how the system is
worked : “Some fellow in Sydney has been
advertising that he had large sums to lend cheap
on good security. Some farmers here thinking it
a good chance, wrote to him ; in consequence of
this the advertiser’s representative came up to
personally interview them. The first thing he
required was an examination fee of so much per
cent, on the amount to be borrowed; this, of
course, had to be paid down. He then examined
the farmers’ proposed securities and reported to
head-quarters, and, as you may imagine, the
report was unfavorable. A great number of
people about here fell in, one for .£2O, one for .£lO,
one for £lO, one for .£6; and others, making a total
of nearly <£2)o, were bitten. One farmer offered
the representative a bonus if he negotiated a loan
for him, but refused to pay an examination fee.
Needless to say, he heard no more of his applica-
tion.” —Yours, &c., A.G.R.
Curiously enough, the Oarruthers Land
Bill proposes to prohibit the taking up of N.S. VV.
“homestead selections” or “settlement leases”
by any “person who is not a natural-born or
naturalised subject of Her Majesty.” Under
such a law, a Hindoo or a Hongkongee would be
able to become a homestead selector, while a
German, Danish, Swedish, &c., newcomer—who
might have been all his days an agriculturist, and
who is generally a settler to be desired—would be
debarred, until he first qualified for naturalisation
by five years’ residence within the province. It
should be sufficient to limit the prohibition of
selection to aliens against whom it has been
already expedient to pass restrictive legislation,
adding, however, to the isolated Chinee, the
Asiatic, the Papuan, the Polynesian, and the
general variety of Hackman. Further, it is high
time that a more liberal naturalisation law was
passed in N.S.W. Only, let the above-indicated
anti-alien legislation be clearly defined and strin-
gently administered as a first precaution.
The proprietors of “Morocco Bound’’
have had a series of extraordinary misadventures
ever since they bought the piece in London. Dick
Kenna, one of them, missed the boat from London,
had to rush down to Brindisi, take ship to Port
Said, and just managed tocatch his own vessel there.
On arrival in Sydney he bought a bull-dog, and
took him up home to Bathurst in order to have him
ready for the first night. He was an inoffensive
dog and was left in custody of a lady relative of
the proprietor. The owner of a Newfoundland
dog next door, thinking to have a lark, pulled
down some palings and let his dog through to the
bull-dog. The Newfoundland never returned—
Bully killed him outright. Then another bull-dog
came round and put his head through the palings.
Bully seized him and held on for so long that a
law-suit ensued. Mean while most of the principals
arrived in Sydney, and then the conductor died.
W. Shine, the Spoofah Bey of the cast, arrived
later and had his boxes lost at Adelaide, mean-
while going on in the piece for the first few per-
formances in a borrowed frock-coat and top-hat.
A few days before the opening, the scenic artist
fell ill and, owing to his illness, there was no
dress-rehearsal. The first night was a series of
complicated disasters. After a week the bulldog
was run over by a tram and chopped up. As he
had no understudy the part had to be cut out.
Then Billy Elton and Joe M’Mahon, driving home
from the races, had their dogcart smashed up,
the horse injured, and themselves nearly killed.
One of the swans that swam near the backcloth in
the first act —after several times attempting to
?i°D ie OWI \ footlights to protest against the
‘Pas de Cinq,” and having to be “hooshed ” back
by the principals—eventually died of shame.
Before any further catastrophes had time to occur
the season providentially ended.
“It is with sorrow and surprise I have
learned, since coming to this colony, that men
who claim to be Liberals are yet the advocates of
the High Tariff. They call it a policy of Protec-
tion, whereas, in reality, it is a policy of attack.”
-—Drivel from the Rev. Henderson, Unitarian
Church, Melbourne.
Dear Bulletin, —-Can you rouse up the
controversial scientists ? It is now asserted that
the planet Mars has no atmosphere. Proctor long
ago declared the Moon to be “a dead world ” and
sans atmosphere. Now, consider the sum of pre-
sen t_ human knowledge as regards the transmission
of light. Our naked eyes see the Moon as a
luminous disc and Mars as a reddish star. If
there is no atmosphere around either, what is
there? What carries the rays of light that belong
to the solar system? Is it ether? Or what?—
Yours, The D.
When the Shearers’ Accommodation Bill
was before the Maoriland Parliament, Hogg-,
M.H.R.. moved an amendment that shearers
should be supplied with proper food, which
amendment was thrown out by 44 votes against
11. Therefore, it seems that a four to one
majority is of opinion that shearers should not
have proper food.
Debs, president of the American Railway
Union strikers, when lately asked by a State Com-
mission if he believed in strikes when they caused
innocent parties to suffer, said: “I believe in
strikes to resist oppression, no matter on whom
the suffering falls.” “Do you believe in Govern-
ment ownership of railroads?” “Yes; it is
preferable to railroad ownership of the Govern-
ment.”

Spring Show.
Good Washers All.
These have always been a leading line, and the
present season is no exception to its predecessors.
Possibly you don’t know it, but they had a wet
summer in Europe, and consequently large quan-
tities of the season’s goods were left on hand, and
manufacturers were triad to quit at a low price for
SPOT CASH. And .
SPOT GASH DID IT.
Our buyers toured the manufacturing districts,
and, CASH IN HAND, had the pick of the
most beautiful of the goods made for the European
summer trade. This splendid operation will have
a tremendous effect on the section concerned, and
in natural course upon the selling prices. The
cost of a dress will be less, while the assurance
will remain that the gown exhibits every fashion-
able characteristic. So marked is the difference
in many favorite lines that a dress and a-half will
cost no mure than a single one under any other
circumstances. It will be well to bear these facts
in mind when contemplating the purchase of
Washing Materials.
Anthony Hordern & Sons,
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS,

A NEW CARDINAL.
In connection with the cablegram which reached
Sydney the other day to the effect that Monsignor
Satolli, the Papel delegate to the United States of
America, is to be created a Cardinal, we publish a
portrait of that eminent ecclesiastical dignitary.
Monsignor Satolli, who is an Italian, was sent to
America by the Pope to settle the differences of
opinion existing among the Roman Catholic
prelates on the schools question and the rights of
jurisdiction. His “ mission ” has been very
successful, and he has been welcomed in many
places in the States as a distinguished visitor. In
July last, Monsignor Satolli arrived at Waterbury,
and was entertained by clergy and laymen. A
reception was given in his honor, and, with the
Mayor and other prominent residents, he visited
the factory of the Waterbury Watch Company.
He ■expressed his surprise and pleasure at seeing
the Waterbury Watch Company’s wonderful
machinery, and the almost perfect system that
pervades the great building. Monsignor Satolli
was presented with a Waterbury watch of one of
the new ecclesiastical patterns, and he was
delighted with the unique design, as well as the
thoughtfulness that prompted the gift. In return,
the Prince of the Church sent his photo, with his
autograph.

Special Attention paid to Painless Operating.
Husbands and fathers are warned against
a kind of family art-gallery, purporting to be a
journal of spring and summer fashions, just issued
by Anthony Hordern and Sons. The pictures of
gowns, hats, and etceteras are so numerous and
seductive that this dangerous production will be
worse than a bomb in the bank-account.
John Howe, for whom is claimed the title
of ‘ ringer of Queensland shearers,” represents
his tally for the last eight years as 82,400, which,
at ■£ l per 100, comes to .£824. Howe worked
only about five months out of each year, however.
The fact is instructive, inasmuch as it enables the
public to see both what a first-class man actually
earned, and what it was possible for him to have
earned at shearing had work been constantly
available.
A Queensland correspondent: “ When
you see telegrams in the daily press announcing
‘ Another shed burnt by unionists !’‘shed ’doesn’t
mean always the costly corrugated iron or sawn-
timber buildings of Riverina, but simply posts set
in the ground and roofed (save the mark !) with
boughs from the nearest scrub. And yet insur-
ance co.’s, until recently, insured these wrecks
with the wool contained therein. In fact, a few
co.’s still do so.
“ The settled policy of New South Wales,”
as it is the fashion to call Freetrade, has
amongst its absurdities the kerosene duty im-
posed by a Ministry which included Cob-
den Club medallist Parkes, which duty was
acquiesced in by Cobden Club medallist
Reid. At present prices, the duty is about
240 per cent., and was imposed for the benefit of
a company which distils a middling sort of
oil from shale. In Victoria there is no kerosene
duty, and in Melbourne gas is cheaper and better,
though coal is dearer than in Sydney. Thus that
huge monopoly, the Australian Gaslight and Coke
Co. of Sydney, is the real gainer by the kerosene
duty, while the kerosene shale is more profitably
used for gas than for oil-making.
The City Mutual Fire Insurance Co., Ltd.,
whose head offices are at 95 Pitt-street, Sydney,
combines low rates with prompt payments. Its
subscribed capital is =£150,000, and it is entirely
an Australian institution, all funds being invested
in New South Wales, while a cash-bonus is paid
to policy-holders every year. The chairman of
directors is the Hon, John See (late Colonial
Treasurer), and his colleagues are the Hon.
John T. Toohey, M.L.C., James P. Garvan,
Esq., Patrick Hogan, Esq., M.P., Patrick
Stanley, Esq., J.P., and John Donelan, Esq.
The head offices have lately been altered and
renovated in such a way as to give increased
accommodation for clerks and the public, while
the board-room and manager’s room have been
considerably enlarged. The interior decoration
has been renewed in a remarkably artistic manner.
The ceiling of the main office is divided into two
bays by a girder. Each bay has been formed into
two square panels, enclosing an octagonal panel of
light buff, surrounded by a border of ornament in
light blue. At each corner is a panel bearing the
arms of the company in raised ornament on a
terra-cotta ground, relieved by a sage - green
border. The intermediate panels, forming the
continuation of the border, are of a pale blue,
with white ornament. The walls of the office are
painted in oil a delicate steel-grey, with a frieze
of pale straw color enriched by stencilled orna-
ment in terra-cotta; being divided from the ceil-
ing by a gold cornice. The board-room and
manager’s office are decorated in a much lighter
style than the main office. The walls of
the board - room are papered with a hand-
some gold paper, with heavy frieze; and
with dado and rail of heavy Japanese leather
paper of splendid design. The general effect of
the renovations is to give largely increased facili-
ties for the conduct of the company’s rapidly
increasing business.*

Talmage has dashed across the Australian
sky like a patent Yankee meteor, a nine-days
wonder of which the tenth day finds no trace. He
denounces drink, of course, but only for the reason
that he has found a better substitute. “When I
feel out of sorts,’’ he told a startled interviewer,
' w h6n gorgons and hydras and chimaeras come
gibbering along the nerve-tracks of this wonderful
body of mine, and variegated serpents curl and
twist along the corridors of this magnificent brain,
it is then that I call for Mrs. T. to unlock her
little cupboard and take down from the top shelf
that great modern restorative, infinitely preferable
to alcohol—the nectar of the eternal, Nature’s own
panacea, Marshall’s Tonic Nervine.
When Isaac A. Isaacs quitted the Patter-
son Government because of the clumsy efforts
made by his colleagues to stop the trial of Matthew
-Davies, he showed himself the one really far-
sighted man in a miserably stupid Cabinet. At
that time Patterson had a steadily-increasing
majority, and the country at large didn’t seem to
care a straw about the Davies prosecution, and, so
far as appearances went, the Solicitor-General was
throwing away his salary and his prospects in a
hopeless cause. But Isaac A. Isaacs was the only one
of them all who knew when to come in out of the
wet. Public enthusiasm was long in developing,
but when the time arrived it burst forth so
strongly that if Isaacs had had 14 brothers they
could all have got into Parliament on the strength
of his reputation. As he hadn’t 14 brothers, the
grateful community rushed in other Hebrews
instead, and the present is the most aggressively
Jewish Legislature that ever existed in’Victoria.

When Alf. Outtrim told his Maryborough
(Vic.) constituents that he was in favor of a State
Bank, an elector rose up and asked him if he
thought a State Bank, managed by the old Parlia-
mentary crowd, would be a safe place for any-
body’s money. Then Outtrim made a hurried
mental review of Bent and Patterson and Shiels
and O’Loghlen, and reluctantly acknowledged
that he didn’t feel quite sure it would.
Dear Bulletin —Let me enter for the tightest
place. Last summer I was working in a tunnel
by myself. Standing outside my forge and partly
sunk in the ground was a full-sized whip-bucket
which I used to quench my drills in. One day I
had finished sharping and was standing over the
cask smoking when my pipe dropped out of my
mouth into the water. I groped about with the
tongs awhile but could not find it; so, as there
was but two feet or so of water in the cask, I slid
in head-first and felt for the pipe with my hands.
I soon found it, but to my horror when I tried to
get out again I couldn’t. This was bad enough,
but when I had been there for perhaps an hour a
fearful thunder-storm came on and big hailstones
began to descend. After the hail came the rain
in torrents, and the cask began to fill. I gave up
all hope of keeping my head above water, and
commenced to pray, when two men who had been
shooting came rushing to my forge for shelter ;
they saw my legs sticking out of the cask and
thought I had suicided, but my yells soon un-
deceived them, and they hauled me out just as
the water had risen to my mouth. Wasn’t that a
tight place ? — Hatter.

OTICE OF FORECLOSURE UNDER REAL PROPERTY ACT.
In the matter of the application of The Right Honorable Sir Charles Wentworth Dilke, mortgagee
under memorandum of mortgage from Thomas Smith Richardson, No. 185,327, dated Ist Sep-
tember, 1891, over the whole of the land comprised in Certificate of Title, vol. 100, folio 199.
WHEREAS the abovenamed mortgagee has made application for an order for foreclosure of the
mortgage above referred to, accompanied by such evidence of default for six months and other facts, as
required by section 113 of the Real Property Act: Notice is hereby given, pursuant to the said Act,
that the land comprised within the said mortgage, as hereunder particularised, is offered and will
remain open for sale until the 12th November, 1894, which date has been appointed as the time upon or
after which I, the Registrar-General, will issue to the applicant an order for foreclosure, which will
exclude all claim by the abovenamed mortgagor, and of any person claiming through or under
him, unless in the interval a sufficient amount has been realised by the sale of such land to satisfy
the principal and interest moneys due, and all costs and expenses occasioned by such proceedings, and
interest up to the time of such sale.
A plan and further particulars may be obtained by application at the Land Titles Office, Elizabeth-
street, Sydney.
Dated at Sydney this 28th day of September, 1894, at the Registrar-General’s Department.
CHARLES PIN HEY, Registrar-General.

There is a deep-green garden where white-robed
Love walks free, . .
On grass no frost lies hard on, in sound of Austral
Clothed’ by the moon in whiteness, barred with
broad bars of jet,
Framed in the purple brightness of Nights star-
spangled net,
Where Heaven to Earth draws nearer, with ear
that almost hears,
With keener gaze and clearer Love reads the
running years, .
White Love among the roses, a low-voiced woman
dwells,
And views as dim day closes those lone star-

A LULLABY SONG.
Patterson, Patterson, snuffed like a candle ;
Now that the voters have put out your light,
Say, why on earth were you decked with a
“ handle? ”
Answer that question, Victorian knight.
Is it explained by your pitiful plight?
Patterson, Patterson, one time so cheerful,
Travelling round with your pestilent “ blow,”
What can you say to the multitude sneerful —
Why did your Sovereign “ honor ” you so?
Thousands of scoffers are wanting to know.
Patterson, Patterson, think of Mat Davies,
Likewise of Bingy, who burst with the boom—
See how they stew in their titular gravies,
Lasting derision their merited doom ;
Think of their fate as you glare in the gloom.
Patterson, Patterson, king of a season,
Tested and found a political blight,
Can you invent a respectable reason
Why you should cling to the title of “ knight ? ”
Has it a value in anyone’s sight ?
Patterson, Patterson, cast-iron Tory,
Proof against ridicule’s sharp-pointed knife.
Yours was the pride and the honor and glory
Of sending that cable to York and his wife,
Wherefore the butcher was branded for life.
AND THIS IS ONE OF THEM.
There are some truths that ought to be blown
through a million speaking trumpets every hour;
that ought to be printed in big type on the front
page of every newspaper; that ought to be painted
■on every signboard at the crossroads ; that ought
to be taught in every school. And this is one of
them : There is no such thing on earth as a tonic
medicine.
People talk of “ tonics ” and doctors talk of
“ tonics.” Pshaw ! When a country is discovered
in which no food stuffs are ever raised or imported,
and in which the men, women, and children are
all well and hearty, we may conclude they subsist
on some sort of “tonic.” The mischief this
delusion about tonics has done is beyond calcula-
tion. It leads the sick to lean on broken reeds,
to expect relief from a source from which it is
simply impossible that relief can come, to waste
money in buying “tonics,” and precious time in
waiting for these alleged “tonics” to work
miracles.
Mr. Thomas Foster, of 15, Chatham Place,
Adelaide Street, Hull, in an account of a recent
illness, says, among other things, this: “I then
tried stomach tonics, but they did me no good.”
Suppose we have his whole story, which is short,
and make our comment on it afterwards. He says :
“ Up to the month of June, 1891, I was strong and
healthy. At that time I fell into a low, weak
condition. I felt languid and heavy, and was
always tired. I had a foul taste in the mouth,
and a dreadful pain in the chest and sides after
eating, whilst my stomach was like a burning Ure.
I was much troubled with wind, which Seemed to
roll all over me, and I had a constant belching and
rising in my throat. I was in agony day and
night, and for hours I walked about the room
rubbing my chest in the effort to obtain relief.
I lost a deal of sleep, and felt worse tired in the
morning than when I went to bed. Gradually I
became weaker and weaker until I had hard work
to follow my employment, for / was in misery all
the time.
“ I went to a doctor, who sounded me and gave
me medicines, but I got no relief, and after taking
his medicines for a month, I left off going to him.
I then tried stomach tonics and other medicines but
nothing did me any good. In this state I con-
tinued week after week, growing more feeble all
the while. I felt that if I did not soon find a
remedy I should be done for altogether.
“In October, 1891, a book was left at my house
telling of a medicine called Mother Seigel’s Cura-
tive Syrup, and describing a case like mine that
had been cured by it. As I had often heard this
medicine well spoken of, I made up my mind to
try it, and got a bottle from Mr. Cousins, in
Anlaby Road. After I had taken two doses, I felt
grateful relief, and before I had quite finished the
bottle I was completely cured, and have since been
in the best of health.
“ I thank God that this medicine was ever made
known to me. Otherwise I should have been in my
grave before now. I will answer anyone who may
write concerning the facts here set forth. Yours
truly (Signed), Thomas Foster, 15, Chatham Place,
Alelaide Street, Hull, March 24th, 1892.”
Now let us see. The symptoms of Mr. Foster’s
complaint are easily recognisable. He suffered
from indigestion and dyspepsia. The medicines
administered by his physician or purchased by
himself proved useless because they were not
addressed to the disease with which he was actually
afflicted, but possibly to one or more of its symp-
toms. To abolish any existing evil it is always
causes we must work at —never mere conse-
quences.
The “ stomach tonics ” which Mr. Foster hoped
might relieve him may have done so for a moment
on precisely the same principle that a sharp
application of whip and spur wakes up a tired
horse, not by giving him strength, but by rousing
his reserved nervous force, with a deeper reaction
to follow. That’s how it ever was and will be.
Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup restored this
gentleman to real and genuine health by cleansing
his system of the poison of disease, by removing
the obstacles and enabling the stomach to retain
and digest food. A very simple thing, yet how
hard to accomplish, oh ! my masters.
The remedy does it, however, as is testified by
a host of witnesses all over the world—witnesses
who say more in praise of its merits than you
would have time to read.
Encore the Sydney College of Music.
“The Sydney College of Music, 1894,” developes
fresh beauties the more it is examined. As stated
in the official pamphlet, its objects are “To
advance the art and science of music, to improve
the professional and technical education of musical
students, to provide for the delivery of lectures on
musical subjects, to ascertain by means of public
examinations the persons who have acquired pro-
ficiency in music .... and to grant to such
persons diplomas or certificates of proficiency, and
other awards and distinctions proportioned to
their respective attainments, and also to confer
honorary distinctions upon persons who have ren-
dered important service to music. ” The Bulletin
intends to apply for an honorary distinction on
the ground that the exposure of this transparent
piece of musical empiricism is an “ important
service to music. ” If the Council desires to improve
the professional and technical education of musical
students, why have they shut out the leading
professional musicians from their ranks ?
Further on in the pamphlet it appears that the
Council have appointed themselves for life.
“Members,” runs the regulation with regard to
membership, “ of the College (that is, common or
garden members, outside the Council) shall enjoy
such privileges as the College Council shall from
time to time confer on them(!)” And again—
“ The College Council may from time to time
admit persons of Distinguished Positions or
Attainments, or such as have rendered eminent
service to the College, to be honorary members
thereof.” Eminent service to the College, mind
you, not eminent service to musical art. There is
nowhere any mention of any election of Council
members or any rule giving power to elect, or any-
thing compelling the Council to retire from any
cause whatever. No, it seems that they have
elected themselves a perpetual Council, to sit en
permanence , to confer privileges from time to time
on persons paying a guinea, or Persons of Dis-
tinguished Position who have rendered eminent
services to the College or who have enough capital
letters after their names; but no one is to be
given any voting power or voice in the manage-
ment, election of managers, or, indeed, anything.
It would appear to be true that, while masquera-
ding in the garb of Public Duty, these gentlemen
are pursuing a policy of Energetic Private Enter-
prise on the strictly limited principle.
The word college, in its general acceptation,
means a place where something is taught, some
instruction given. Not so in this case. ; This
college does not propose to teach anybody any-
thing, probably for very good reasons. It only
proposes to examine. It will not teach in class.
Its council are all private teachers. One rule says
—“ Members of the college will have the privilege
of entering candidates at reduced fees.” That is
to say, they will have power to enter their own
pupils and friends at reduced fees. It is vain to
search through the book for any provision for a
thoroughly competent and independent board of
examiners. There is no such provision. True,
under the head “ examiners ” this occurs —“ From
time to time, as found expedient, resident and
visiting musicians will be invited to adjudicate.”
It seems plausible, but are the talented foreign
and native artists who have been shut out of this
close corporation likely to adjudicate? Hardly.
And so, as visiting artists are few and far between,
the council will be compelled to adjudicate them-
selves. Indeed, one of the councillors, Mr.
Delany, has already been appointed “Chairman of
the Board of Examiners.” If it is necessary to have
such things as diplomas, etc., then no power but
the State through the Department of Education
should administer the letters of the alphabet to
the patients, and they should appoint the Board
of Examiners from among the Competent.
In the name of Art, which includes common
sense, is this how young Australia is to be musi-
cally straight-jacketed? What originality can
possibly come out of such a scheme as this?
English is written in big letters all over it.
English teachers are to carry on th'e good old
exploded English system of teaching harmony and
counterpoint, and brand the victims with the same
old brands. And When hordes of people are let
loose with A.S.C.M. and L.S.C.M. hanging onto
them like a tin-pot to a dog’s tail, how will musical
art be helped ? A set of bumptious imbeciles will
be created whose love for alphabetical decoration
will far exceed their love for Art.
If Australians are to avoid becoming objects of
ridicule to the musical world they will rise and
tramp heavily on their dull British institution.
John Bull is not musical. Even the Royal College
of Music (of which this seems to be a feeble imita-
tion) has not succeeded in doing anything as yet
but distribute capital letters. We must not allow
the stream of Australian music to be headed off
towards dull hymn tunes, snuffly anthems, and
the ecclesiastical music of the Established Church.
We want that science, that sweetness, that depth,
that vigor, that originality, the secret of which is
held by the continental peoples. Founding their
schools upon the achievements of the past, in
direct succession from Bach, Mozart, Gluck,
Beethoven, Chopin, Rossini, Mosoheles, Verdi,
Gounod, Auber and Halevy, they pass on to us
the best traditions of musical art; and it is from
them, and not from England, that we will learn
how to enclose the Australian sunshine, the tones
and atmosphere of Australia, in our music.
In short, any attempt to shut out the foreign
musicians, resident in Sydney, from-participation
in the shaping of Australia’s musical destiny will
recoil on the impudent heads of those who
attempt it. We want the best we can get from
all nations to join in shaping our growth, and,
above all, we want to avoid the portentous dull-
ness and somnolency of musical John Bullism.
“The Sydney Musical College, 1894,” is an
appropriate name, for it will have no existence to
speak of in 1895.

George Anson once had a harrowing experience. Late
one night, in the bar of the George, St. Kilda, Anson was
accosted by a slightly inebriated dude with, “ Hallo,
Elton, old man. How are you, Billy?” Anson got on
his dignity, and exclaimed, “My name’s not Elton. I
am George Anson.” To which answered the Johnny,
“ Oh, that’s all right, Billy. You don’t have me. I’m
up to your little joke.” Anson got mad. “All right,”
said he, “I’ll soon show you that I’m Anson,” and pro-
ceeded to have that weird fit he used to give in “Dr.
Bill,” during the progress of which he rolled the dude all
over the floor, and broke furniture with him, and damaged
him generally. The dude, however, came up all smiling,
simply said, “You’re a d d funny fellow, Billy,” and
stood drinks for the crowd. When parting at the door,
the inebriated one fell on Anson’s neck, saying, “Good
night Elton, old man; you’re a clever fellow, but that
chap Anson is a blanky idiot 1” The “boys” laughed
hilariously, but Anson’s language was unfit for publics-
tlon. .
At one of the Sapio-Urso concerts, at Wellington (M.L.),
an intoxicated person wandered through the stage-
entrance, and along various passages, until at last he
landed at the open door Of the dressing-room,
where he proceeded to dance a hilarious jig. They
couldn’t scream without disturbing Sapio and Scherek,
who were in the middle of a duet on the stage, so they
banged the door and held it on the inside, while the
visitor howled terms of endearment at them from without.
The two gentlemen on the stage heard the riot, and
vaguely understood that some unauthorised person was
trying to kiss their wives during their absence. But
their sense of duty to the audience was paramount, and
they finished their duet in a state of awful suspense, yet
without slurring a single note. Then they bowed to the
audience, and rushing behind the scenes they threw the
intruder down stairs in 16 pieces.
A really unique aspirant for music-hall honors turned
up in a recent Melb. law-case. She was the daughter of
an up-country school-teacher, and ran away to Melbourne
to be a swinging beauty at Frank Clark’s show. Writing
to a member of the co. to meet her at the station, she
said: “ You will know me by my freckles.” That girl
knew her sphere; the variety business would certainly
knock spots off her.
Ructions at London Lyric, resulting in the secession of
May Yohe. Florence St. John takes her place as Little
Christopher Columbus, whilst Geraldine Ulraar now plays
Guinevere Block in the new and revised version. It plays
too long at present, and Lounen must have a lot of his
twaddle cut out. It is remarkable, says The Bulletin’s
London correspondent, what a run so poor a piece has
had. Johnny Sheridan has been one of the causes, for he
is inimitable as the second Mrs. Block.
“ Little Jack Sheppard,” just revived at London
Gaiety, is going very dottily indeed, and proves once
more that the old order has completely changed, remarks
Tub Bulletin’s London correspondent. Teddy Royce
and Charles Danby are the principal “ mirth provokers,
but they must find the work pretty hard.
“ Little Jack Sheppard ” is not quite ten years old, but
out of the first Gaiety cast five performers-Fred Leslie,
David James, Miss Wadman, Harriet Coveney and Emily
Duncan—are dead and gone, and Nellie Farren has become
a physical wreck.
A pulpit orator in America recently took upon himself
to denounce the plays produced by Mrs. Brown-Potter
and “Curly,” and as the pair walked out of the church
with somewhat pardonable indignation, he proceeded to
inveigh against them personally. The question that
strikes one is what the devil they were there to do . The
goody-goody game?

Miss Millie Collier, leading lady at Melb. Alexandra, is
the eldest daughter of the late G. W. Collier, who, years
ago, supported the late Walter Montgomery, under Cop-
pin’s management, at the old Royal.
Rich man Edward Terry had, apparently, no more than
a fat salary interest in the unsuccessful production of the
“King Kodak” burlesque. Manager Abud was lately
sued for 164 on account of costumes supplied for “ King
Kodak.” and although he tried to shift the debt along to a
mysterious Burlesque Comedy Syndicate, the plaintiff
got judgment with costs.
The old building in Queen-st., Melb., which, 50 years
ago, was John Thomas Smith’s Queen’s Theatre, is to let.
For many years it was a coachbuilder’s.
A traveller who, by great persistence and much diplo-
macy, got admission to the Chapel of the Cathedral of
Shoa (Abyssinia), found the walls of that sanctuary—pro-
bably the most profoundly holy place in all Africa—hung
round with glaring oleographs representing the British
leg-show females in various stages of undress. Yet the
Abyssinian prays there in a devout spirit, and the un-
clothed coryphee doesn’t take his attention off heavenly
things.
A Parisian music-hall has got something like a sensa-
tion. The curtain goes up and reveals a pretty woman in
bed in an elegantly-appointed bed-room. A clock strikes
ten, whereupon a neat waiting-maid comes in, awakens
her mistress, and tells her it is time to get up and git.
Then she slowly arises in an embroidered nisrht-dress with
great quantities of lace upon it, and dresses herself in
garments that are a poem and a dream of loveliness,
before the eyes of an enraptured audience. For some un-
fathomable reason very few men go to this show', but the
women roll up in multitudes to sigh over the beautifully-
appointed bedroom, and the silk stockings and expensive
linen and cambric underclothing, and all the other items
of the wardrobe. The lady who forms the centre of this
show doesn’t dance or do anything else after she is
dressed ; she sings one or two snatches of song while her
great masses of hair are being brushed with an ivory
brush, but apart from this she merely wakes up and
dresses herself and then goes away.
Dining at a country hotel, W. S. Gilbert found himself
at table with three cycling clergymen. Cards being
exchanged, the parsons found who their companion was,
and one asked, jocularly, “ how he felt in such grave and
reverend company.” “I feel like a lion in a den of
Daniels,” replied W.S.G.
Waxworks Kreitmeyer originally came to Australia for
a London scientific society to study the marsupials of the
country, but on arriving in Melb. he was bitten by gold-
fever and threw up his billet for a tin-dish and a trip to
Ballarat.
Leon Caron owes a deal of his advancement to Madame
Camillo Urso, who met the young violinist when in his
teens, and was struck with his playing. It w’as the late
Charles Horsley who advised him to come to Australia,
which he did in ’76. Meeting him again in Melbourne,
Madame Urso appointed him her chief of orchestra, and
since then his career has been uniformly fortunate.
Alice Dunning Lingard, the loveliest woman who ever
trod the Australian stage, is touring Great Britain with
H. B. Conway, in Oscar Wilde’s play, “A Woman of No
Importance.” ■ •
Maud Hobson, said to have been born at St. Kilda
(Melb.), is taking “ A Gaiety Girl” to America. She plays
the lead herself.
Bernhardt tells an interviewer that every woman should
retire from the stage at 50. But that is just when a ballet
girl is beginning to know her business.
The stage locomotive always puffs vigorously while
travelling at top speed, whereas the real article does so
only while starting.
Bland Holt’s horses, those he uses in “Prodigal
Daughter,” won first prize at Adelaide show.
A new song, doubtless destined to have a tremendous
sale, is “A Song to yEgir.” The words, music, and
design for the cover are all by the young German Kaiser.
In style it is said to resemble “ The Watch on the Rhine.”
Recent “ living pictures ” at the Empire Theatre, Lon-
don, included a gory picture of three of Her Majesty’s
troops, crouching behind a cluster of Maxim guns and
vanquishing the Matabele, who waved assegais at them
from afar. At least, the surviving Matabele were making
a demonstration with these crude weapons, but most of
the miserable “ rebels ” lay in a heap of slain. This pic-
ture was labelled “ British Pluck,” and the audiences
took it quite seriously. Doubtless it is still stirring the
souls of the populace. A tableau of that description,
accompanied by. “ Rule Britannia” from a first-class
orchestra, is just what a Londoner likes, especially if he
stands flve-feet-nothing and serves in a draper’s shop.

A Chicago paper publishes an article entitled “Actresses’
Legs.” It discusses at length the legs of Mary Anderson,
Mrs. Langtry, Mrs. Brown-Potter, Sara Bernhardt, Jane
Hading, Ada Rehan, Ellen Terry, and others. Most of
the legs are illustrated.
Olga Nethersole has signed with Augustin Daly for a
tour in America, and a London season. Does this portend
the retirement of Rehan?
One of Fred. Leslie’s jokes is still remembered at
Tussaud’s waxworks. He took up his position in a
crooked and hump-backed attitude along with a wax
group, assumed an expression which even the worst
murderer in the Chamber of Horrors could not rival for
brutality, and remained motionless. Gradually a crowd
gathered round the dreadful object and speculated as to
the crimes it must have committed. When the crowd
was at its thickest the exhibit straightened itself up and
walked away, leaving two women in hysterics and half-a-
dozen bucoiic visitors bolting for the door.
A well-known ex-circus proprietor recently joined the
Salvation Army. One day an old employ &of his met him
and said: “Well, sir, who are you working for now?”
The ex-ringmaster said: “I’m working for God, my
friend!” “Well,” replied the other, “you’ll have the
best of Him, I’ll bet.”

open three nights a week, averaged weekly during a five-
months’ season only £47. Yet in the Co. were Mr. and
Mrs. Rogers, Mr. and Mrs. Charles Young (later Mrs.
Herman Vezin), and some of the Howson family.
The Maoriland Tablet announces the appearance of the
Devil to a Masonic lodge : “ He had the features of a man
from 35 to 38 years of age ; tall in stature ; without heard
or moustache ; rather thin than fat, but in no degree
bony; his face refined and distinguished ; an indescribable
melancholy in his look; a nervous smile curving the
corners of his lips. He was naked—in skin, white tinged
with rose ; marvellously well shaped, like a statue ol
Apollo.” This item is inserted in the “shows” column
principally because the Devil had nothing on.

Said Richelieu : “No man can speak five

“A Golden Shanty,” by Bulletin writers,
is one of the most striking products of nascent
Australian literature. Its contents are gems of
native lustre, glowing with no borrowed brilliancy.
Their breezy humour, their weird pathos, spring
alike from Southern soil, under a Southern sky,
and their promise is sui generis, unique and
unapproachable. The second edition now ready ;
post-free to any Australasian address. Is. 3d. :
foreign, Is. fid.
Critics, and Other Refletions.
PEAK Bulletin,— Your critic, “ Walter Clay,”
should know that there are pilots and pilots—
river pilots, “Channel” pilots, deep-sea pilots.
Jn England, where Tennyson wrote, the most
common variety is the Channel pilot. He meets
you at the mouth of the Mersey, or the Humber,
or the Thames, and takes you perhaps as far as
the Lizard, when you set sail across blue water on
your own account. And the bar is just the spot
where he comes aboard. Here the obscure
river specimen leaves, and the deep-sea man
takes his place. Surely “Walter Clay” remem-
bers the yarn of the Soilly pilot who knew “all
the rocks in the Channel,” and said as the vessel
struck, “That’s one!” Why didn’t he “turn
home” when he got to the bar? Tome, it has
always seemed appropriate that a pilot should
come to guide the poor trembling soul
over the Infinite into its Aidenn. “ Poets don’t
know everything ’’—true. Neither does Walter.
Or he would not tell you that so much of the best
poetic thought—Milton’s? Wordsworth’s? Ros-
setti's ? —has been spoiled by the tyranny of
sonnet-construction, and therefore the sonnet must
go Why? To a poet it is as easy to build a
sonnet as it is for Parkes to rhyme “ doggie ” and
“loggie.” The sonnet-form has never cramped
genius. On the contrary, as the perfection
of melodious brevity, it has enabled it
to give an exquisite setting to exquisite
thought. Elsewhere, Clay insists on the
need of brevity; but I suppose this is only his
aggravating way. He annoys me, too, by com-
paring “Ghosts” to [“jewels five-words long”]
“which should sparkle, as Tennyson says, ‘on
the stretched forefinger of all time.’” Now,
Tennyson never dreamed of saying that four four-
line verses would sparkle on the stretched fore-
finger of all time; and I am surprised that so
hypercritical a Walter should accuse him of the
incongruity. Further, Olay is of opinion that
“Ghosts” is a finer poem than “Crossing the
Bar.” I respect his opinion; but prefer my own,
which is that “Ghosts” is a remarkably fine bit
of verse, and “Crossing the Bar” is a remark-
ably fine poem. As I see that the names
which Walter chooses for special mention
in discussing modern poetry are those of Kipling
(a brilliant verse-writer), Swinburne (a poet of
limited range), and Watson (a poet of more limited
range), I shall not try to convince Clay; especially
as I might waste a page without making the
ground of my distinction clear. Poetry-readers
will understand when I take the first names that
come to mind, and say that Kendall had more of
the poet than Gordon, and Gordon than Stephens;
that Matthew Arnold was a poet, and Edwin
isn’t; that Whitman was a poet, a.nd Longfellow
wasn’t—nor Emerson ; that “ Nevermore ”is not
a poem, and “The Haunted Palace” is; that the
“Ingoldsby Legends ” are not poems, and “ As I
Lave a-Thynkynge” is; and so on ad in.
Noting, in passing, that the “ uniformity of
metrical construction,” in which Mr. Ropes is
alleged to “score heavily against the dead
laureate,” is, for the most part, the monotonous
companion of mediocrity ; I close by venturing
the reflection that, if an age is coming when a
poet will write, nd will have to write, in the
fashion of the cablegram correspondent, then a
very sad age will be mated to a very bad poet.—
Yours, Arthur Bray.
