The Song of the Dead.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I.
Death came to us, and beckoned,
We feigned we did not see,
We hid our eyes a second,
And blindly prayed that he,
The Raven-tinted Angel,
Would overlook our souls,
And find new prey to gather
To the muster of his rolls.
In vain we prayed and labored,
In vain we did not see,
For Death, the mailed and sabred.
Had beckoned us, and we,
All bent and blind with weeping,
And wild with wish to stay,
Were driven forth relentless
From the heat of life’s brief day.
11.
Oh, the sweet of lying dead,
Of whispering to yourself, Death’s done, Death’s
done ;
To feel the Death-bands round your head ;
To know the Death tears all are shed,
And Death is past. Death that all life doth
shun.
Oh, the sweet of lonely miles,
Of wanting not to sing, nor speak, nor sigh.
The lips have done with shallow smiles,
The eyes with tears and woven wiles,
And lips and eyes in rest’s own settings lie.
Oh, the sweet of lying still,
Of lying stiller than a sea-deserted shore,
The hands have lost the weary will
That watched their working at life’s mill,
The feet that ached to rest need run no more.
Oh, the sweet of lying still;
Of lying still and still, long year on year.
Wild overhead lives throb and thrill,
Waves beat the shore, and winds the hill,
But only silence ever enters here.
111.
Lying here still in these valleys of death,
Lying all dead, and happy so to bide.
What if there came from life’s far shores a breath
Of those old prayers we uttered when we died?
What if there came the haunting of those tears
We shed and suffered, begging Death to stay
His sword a space, and spare our little years
Of light awhile, and turn his head away ?
What if there came an answer to the prayer
We deafened Death with? What if Death
heard now,
And, smote with sudden pity, cried “Return !”
And straightway took his seal from off our brow?
Lying here still in the valleys of Death;
Lying all dead, and happy so to lie ;
God, God, the horror of a livened breath,
The death-bound body stirring from its tie.
Oh, God ! the horror of this perfect sleep
Made loud with life, and broken with the rain
Of living voices, while our bodies creep*.
With shuddering footsteps, back to life again,
To find therein all old joys turned to gall!
More wild our tears than those we dying shed. , .
Thank God, not all our prayers are answered us,
And “No Returning” bounds the soul once
dead.
Sydney.
M. LOUUSE MACK.
Bits from a Blackleg’s Diary.
I was a blackleg once. I do not think I
shall ever be again. The Bulletin shall judge
by my experiences whether the pledge is a rash
one, I was engaged as a shearer in Maoriland.
We all thought over there that the Australian
shearer was too high and mighty a being to con-
descend to work. Our impression was that he
preferred to lie around in the shade and swap lies
with his mates, and that if he did favor any par-
ticular kind of exertion it was riding a buckjumper,
varied by howling orgies at bush shanties. Con-
sequently we thought it quite fair to accept the
offered terms of 17s. per 100 for shearing with the
machine, and 20s. by hand. We believed in indi-
vidual liberty, and our notion of it was that any-
one should be free to accept whatever terms he
chose. Freedom of contract cuts both ways. The
Maoriland agents engaged anyone who offered.
It was to their advantage to be complaisant.
1 hey were paid 10s. per head for all they engaged
—just like shipping Kanakas. So the halt, the
lame, and the blind came to shear, knowing that
if they proved incompetent they would be em-
ployed as rouseabouts. It would be well within
the mark to say that not 250 competent shearers
have come from there, though Maoriland was
“combed ” from end to end.
* • *
We were shipped like convicts, or free
immigrants, or cattle. The steerage was crowded
with 175 persons, and there were not berths for
100. Some slept on the tables. Some under
them. _ Some in the seats. Some in the passages.
Some in the lavatory. Some on the foot of the
companion. Some on deck. It any alarm had
arisen there would not have been boat-accom-
modation for half the number on board. Some of
these days there will be a sensational chapter in
interprovincial travelling. It is supposed to be
the duty of the Customs to see that sufficient
boat-accommodation is found. Yet the shipping-
reports of the day after our arrival stated that
100 odd were “in the steerage.” No account was
taken of the 60 odd shearers shipped on their
agreements,” a copy of which served for a
ticket. The passenger-manifest, therefore, lied.
Yet nobody has been punished.
* * *
The “shearers ” included a large number
of larrikins from Lyttelton and Wellington.
They made the ship a Sheol. Yet the officers en-
forced no order. Marcus Clarke’s undeseribed cell
that drove young Andrews mad was a Sunday-
school compared to that steerage. The steamer
anchored in the harbor, and we were removed by
launch to Chowder Bay, and kept under strict
surveillance for two days. Thirty of our number
had shown their real intention by refusing to
leave the steamer, and the rest of us were counted
several times a day lest any should escape. This
was for our own good, and for fear that any
naughty unionists should-get near us and persuade
us to go away. The P.U. had not much faith in
our tenacity of purpose. The prison treatment
was also used by the landlord and landlady at our
hotel. We were treated with contempt and
suspicion, and some of us suggested to our gaolers
that we might as well be searched at once.
* * *
At the precise moment fixed upon we
were landed at Fort Macquarie and whisked off in
’buses to the station, where special trains waited.
The night-journey was beguiled with buckets of
tea and bread and cold meat and cards and smok-
ing and things. At Orange our carriage was cut
off, the rest going towards Bourke, while an inter-
mediate town was our destination. We were met
by the coaches accompanied by armed troopers
and a squatter —also conspicuously armed. We
cut off through the bush, under the impression
that we had given the slip to the unionists as-
sembled close by. There was not room for all in
the coaches and many of us had to pant behind,
laggards being rounded up by the police. By
devious routes we made 16 miles that day and
camped out, police guarding us all night. A band
of unionists was camped opposite and it was re-
ported, but not believed, that they had tried to
stampede our horses in the night. They met us
next morning on the road but did nothing except
try to persuade us to “come out” and be men.
We were not men—not just yet. Again we
out the wire fence and went through private
property. But we had to come on the main
road again to pass a ford, and here we were
met by a hundred mounted unionists. They
were wild-eyed men who had evidently
roughed it for some time ; good, but ungraceful,
riders on rough, but wiry, horses. They escorted
us tumultuously to near the station, where we
were met by about a hundred on foot, who had
been waiting for us. But they offered no violence,
nor even threats. Some of us were frightened by
them. Those who were so were of that kind who
asked whether kangaroos had horns, and won-
dered who planted all the gum-trees.
* * *
This was on Saturday, and next day the
unionists, "encamped in some spare station huts,
were permitted to confer with us for a-quarter of
an hour. Two of them came, but were sadly lack-
ing in debating-power. They went back to their
mates, saying that God Almighty wouldn’t drag
some of us out. Then they were driven off by the
police, the ground being “ private property,” and
gave us a piece of their mind in passing. The
unionist shearer is a child of nature. His vocabu-
lary is limited, but rose-colored. What he lacks
in variety he makes up in richness and force.
« • «
Next morning shearing began. Stud ewes
were put in—wool down to the toes, and even
between them. New machines. None accus-
tomed to them. After shearing one hour—that is
till dinner-time—the biggest tally was seven. The
shorn sheep looked as if they had been attacked
with blunt adzes. What else could be expected ?
Our numbers had been augmented at several way-
side stations, and we were 46 shearers all told, and
of that number only about 20 could shear at all.
Barbers and tailors and barmen showed how, in
their opinion, sheep ought to be shorn. One
fellow was on his knees, the sheep being on its
feet, and holding it affectionately round the neck
while he gingerly pushed the machine along its
backbone. Some did not know even where to
begin, and one fellow left a skirt of wool hanging
to a ewe’s rearward part that made her the envy
of her sex. However, nature, as exemplified in a
squatter, could not be expected to stand this, so,
after another hour’s trial after dinner, and the
employment of four of our number as experts to
teach the incompetent, work was suspended and
not afterwards resumed by us.
• • »
Then followed two days’ inaction and un-
certainty. Rumours were rife that the boss had
engaged union men to take our places: but at
length instructions came to “roll up ” for a head
station, 10 miles away. Eleven complied com
petent and incompetent, and we had another pi c '
nic of a so-called coach journey. The squatter
rode to Forbes, visited the union camp there and
engaged men under verbal agreement to fill his
board. The unionists thought at first they were
beaten in the West, for that head station was the
key to the position. Afterwards they won uncon
ditionally, and for the defeat the P.IJ. h as on j’
itself to thank. The squatter indicated had
always been against the nefarious agreement of
1894, and only assented to it on condition that he
was supplied with 80 competent machine shearers
on the first of August, and by the end of that
month they sent him 46 of whom only 20 were
competent.
* * *
When we got to the home station we were
informed that we were to be divided into four
gangs for as many different stations. All these
places were infested by unionists, and yours truly
and a mate decided that it was not good enough
to go any further. We felt that we were on the
wrong side, and fighting against the sympathy of
the country. The risk of a broken head may
be incurred in a right cause, but not in an endea-
vor to cut down wages by sneakery. The police
tried to dissuade us from returning, saying that
we would be hurt, and the P.U. emissary said the
same. But we decided to risk it, and were allowed
to return that 36 miles to Forbes through country
almost submerged, and threading our way through
parties of jubilant union men on their way to take
our places. But we only received a civil “ Good
day, mate.” And my own belief is that most of
the shootings and so on stone-throwings
elsewhere have been due to provocation. Some
on each side carry arms, and nothing is more
natural than to resort to them in the height of
quarrel. Of our journey to Forbes I say nothing
nor shall I speak at present of our tramp to Sydney
in a state of destitution. No more blacklegging
for me. 8
PAKEHA.
A Southern doctor, lately called to attend
a farmer suffering from severe d.t., had no sooner
entered the room than he rushed like a bull-dog at
the sick man’s throat. In about ten minutes
when nothing breakable or tearable in the room
remained whole, the two naked, bleeding figures
were at last hauled apart, and the fact was elicited
that physician and patient were both equal suf-
ferers from the same complaint.
Two medical items by cable : “A congress
of German scientists approves of Prof. Behring’s
injeetion-of-animal-serum treatment for diph-
theria, which is expected to save the lives of
1,500,000 people in Austria and Germany during
the next decade.” “ A professor, placed on trial
at Buenos Ayres for having poisoned 15 of his
friends at various times with cholera germs frozen
in ice and wine, committed suicide.”
